# CYBERSPACE AND

# ELECTROMAGNETIC

# WARFARE OPERATIONS

# SEPTEMBER 2025

# DISTRIBUTION RESTRICTION:

## Approved for public release; distribution is unlimited.

## This publication supersedes FM 3-12, dated 24 August 2021.

# HEADQUARTERS, DEPARTMENT OF THE ARMY

# Foreword

Over the past several years, the global security environment has changed in ways once considered
unthinkable in the post-Cold War world order. Where the Department of Defense once believed the future of
warfare was antiterrorism and limited contingency operations, it is now clear that the prospect of large-scale
combat against a regional peer is much more likely than once believed. In recent years, Russia has engaged
in the largest land war in Europe of the past 80 years. China, with an economy nearly equal in size to ours,
is fostering new international partnerships through its Belt and Road Initiative and building a world-class
military to challenge the United States, its allies, and neighbors in the Pacific Rim, including in Taiwan.
China and Russia, along with other regional peers, have heavily invested in and demonstrated formidable
capabilities to challenge U.S. dominance in multiple domains, including cyberspace and the electromagnetic
spectrum.
The Army’s operating concept of multidomain operations anticipates the return of great power competition
and large-scale combat operations and stresses the need to integrate and converge warfighting capabilities in
all domains to achieve and press positions of relative advantage. At the same time, the Army’s newest
doctrine for information presents a new paradigm for how the Army views information in a military context.
Data and information are central to command and control, for our adversaries as much as for ourselves.
Advances in sensors, networking technologies, and global connectivity provide commanders an
unprecedented amount of data and information to support informed decision making. These capabilities are
almost wholly dependent on cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum. Peer threats continually attempt
to infiltrate, exploit, and degrade access to and ability to use our networks and data. It is therefore vital that
the Army not only protect its own information and cyberspace capabilities, but also deny our adversaries the
use of similar capabilities.
Creating effects in cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum across the competition continuum is key to
achieving relative advantages. To maximize these advantages, commanders must integrate and synchronize
cyberspace operations and electromagnetic warfare with all other available military capabilities through a
combined arms approach.
Competent leadership, informed by sound doctrine, is critical as the Army prepares for the complexities of
modern warfare. We must actively assess and refine our approach to cyberspace operations and
electromagnetic warfare through continuous learning, guided by lessons learned from the operational force.
By embracing doctrinal principles and integrating military capabilities across all domains, we will enhance
our ability to achieve relative advantages and ensure the Army remains ready to fight and win.
Forge and project power!
RYAN M. JANOVIC
MAJOR GENERAL, UNITED STATES ARMY
COMMANDING

## Preface

FM 3-12 nests with and supports joint doctrine for cyberspace operations and joint electromagnetic spectrum
operations and with higher-level Army doctrine. To understand and apply the doctrine in this publication,
readers should be familiar with Army operations doctrine found in ADP 3-0 and FM 3-0. They should also
be familiar with the information advantage framework outlined in ADP 3-13.
The principal audience for FM 3-12 is commanders and staffs at echelons theater army and below. The
secondary audience is cyber and electromagnetic warfare professionals. Commanders and staffs of Army
headquarters serving as joint task force or multinational headquarters should also refer to applicable joint or
multinational doctrine concerning the competition continuum and joint or multinational forces. Trainers and
educators throughout the Army will also use this publication.
Commanders, staffs, and subordinates ensure that their decisions and actions comply with applicable United
States, international, and in some cases host-nation laws and regulations. Commanders at all levels ensure
that their Soldiers operate in accordance with the law of armed conflict and the rules of engagement. (See
FM 6-27.) They also adhere to the Army ethic as described in ADP 6-22.
FM 3-12 uses joint terms where applicable. Selected joint and Army terms and definitions appear in both the
glossary and the text. Terms for which FM 3-12 is the proponent publication (the authority) are italicized in
the text and are marked with an asterisk (*) in the glossary. When first defined in the text, terms for which
FM 3-12 is the proponent publication are boldfaced and italicized, and definitions are boldfaced. When first
defining other proponent definitions in the text, the term is italicized and the number of the proponent
publication follows the definition. Subsequent uses of the term are not italicized.
This publication applies to the Active Army, Army National Guard/Army National Guard of the United States
and United States Army Reserve unless otherwise stated.
The proponent for this publication is the United States Army Cyber Center of Excellence. The preparing
agency is the Doctrine Branch, United States Army Cyber Center of Excellence. Send comments and
recommendations on a DA Form 2028 (Recommended Changes to Publications and Blank Forms) to
Commander, United States Army Cyber Center of Excellence and Fort Gordon, ATTN: ATZH-OPD (FM
3-12), 419 B Street, Fort Gordon, GA 30905-5735, or e-mail to usarmy.gordon.cyber-coe.mbx.gord-fg-
doctrine@army.mil.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**v**

## This page intentionally left blank.

## Introduction

FM 3-12 provides Army doctrine for cyberspace operations, electromagnetic warfare, and cyberspace
electromagnetic activities as part of a combined arms approach to achieve objectives across the competition
continuum. FM 3-12 provides commanders and staffs an understanding of Army cyberspace operations,
electromagnetic warfare, cyberspace electromagnetic activities, staff roles and responsibilities, and those
echelons above corps organizations that support cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare operations.
FM 3-12 describes how personnel who conduct cyberspace electromagnetic activities plan, integrate, and
synchronize cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare operations and how cyberspace electromagnetic
activities sections integrate with other staff sections through various integrating processes and working
groups throughout the operations process.
FM 3-12 contains five chapters and nine appendixes:
•
**Chapter 1 – Overview and Threat**—begins with an overview of the context for cyberspace and
electromagnetic warfare as part of a combined arms approach. It goes on to discuss threats and
vulnerabilities. The chapter concludes with a discussion of systems analysis to support targeting and
protection planning.
•
**Chapter 2 – Cyberspace Operations**—describes how different portions of cyberspace may be
controlled and operated by different entities, with users relying on systems they neither control nor
operate. It goes on to discuss how Army forces protect against threat use of cyberspace and preserve
friendly ability to use it. The chapter concludes with an explanation of the conduct of operations
against threats in and through cyberspace as well as in the air and land domains based on their
reliance upon cyberspace.
•
**Chapter 3 – Electromagnetic Warfare**—introduces electromagnetic warfare and the divisions of
electromagnetic warfare consistent with joint doctrine. It goes on to discuss the application of
electromagnetic attack, electromagnetic protection, electromagnetic support, and electromagnetic
warfare reprogramming in Army operations. The chapter then addresses the synchronization of
electromagnetic warfare with complementary signals intelligence capabilities. The chapter
concludes with a description of organic electromagnetic warfare forces at echelons corps and below.
•
**Chapter 4 – Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities**—describes the integration and
synchronization of cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare operations into combined arms through
the operations process—planning, preparation, execution, and assessment, the role of the cyberspace
electromagnetic activities section in targeting. The chapter concludes with a discussion of
integration with the joint force.
•
**Chapter 5 – Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**—
addresses the contributions of cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare operations in a combined
arms approach. The chapter begins with a discussion of targeting, convergence, and integration with
the joint force. It then discusses the contributions of cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare
operations across the competition continuum.
•
**Appendix A – Cyber Incident Response**—describes the types and severity of cyber incidents and
the reporting processes and procedures for reporting cyber incidents to higher echelons and joint
force authorities.
•
**Appendix B – Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities Planning and Integration**—discusses the
cyberspace electromagnetic activities section’s role in the military decision-making process and
integrating processes.
•
**Appendix C – Effects Requests**—introduces the forms and instructions for the cyber effects request
format, the electromagnetic attack request format, and the joint tactical airstrike request.
•
**Appendix D – Recognizing and Responding to Electromagnetic Attacks and Malicious**
**Cyberspace Activity**—addresses the means to recognize and overcome threat malicious cyberspace
activities and electromagnetic attacks in a contested environment. It includes an overview of peer
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**vii**
**Introduction**
threat tactics, techniques, and procedures and methods to prevent, recognize, and counter threat
electromagnetic attacks and malicious cyberspace activity.
•
**Appendix E – Message Formats**—provides the formats and instructions for the various
electromagnetic warfare messages.
•
**Appendix F – Echelons Above Corps Cyber and Electromagnetic Warfare Organizations**—
discusses the national, Department of Defense, and Reserve Components organizations that conduct
or support cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare operations. This appendix also provides an
overview of the United States Cyber Command and its subordinate joint organizations that deliver
cyberspace operations and electromagnetic warfare support to Army commanders using cyber
mission forces.
•
**Appendix G – Training**—discusses training to prepare Soldiers to execute missions to shape
operational environments, prevent conflict, and support large-scale combat operations against peer
threats. This discussion includes institutional training, home-station training, combat training center
rotations, and deployments.
•
**Appendix H – Network Hardening Guide**—This guide should provide units a risk-based
assessment of their network security posture and ultimately a more secure network. The network
hardening guide organizes local network hardening procedures around four distinct but interrelated
areas:
▪
Domain management and security.
▪
Network device management and security.
▪
Network traffic monitoring and alerts.
▪
Endpoint (host and server) security.
•
**Appendix I – Army Integrated Reprogramming Cycle**—defines the future state Army
reprogramming enterprise.

## SUMMARY OF CHANGES

This is a major revision of FM 3-12. Significant changes in this revision include—
•
**Operational focus**—this revision of FM 3-12 focuses on the application of available military
capabilities at echelons theater army and below as part of a combined arms approach to create effects
in cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum.
•
**Information advantage**—describes how the employment of cyberspace operations and
electromagnetic warfare contributes to relative information advantages as outlined in ADP 3-13.
•
**Threat**—introduces the categories of threat—enemy, adversary, and insider threat—and their
significance in cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum. Details cyberspace- and
electromagnetic spectrum-relevant aspects of the most challenging state threats.
•
**Hazards**—describes natural and manmade hazards that affect operations in the electromagnetic
spectrum.
•
**Systems analysis**—introduces the analysis of mission threads (systems of systems) to identify
dependencies and vulnerabilities to support either targeting (threat systems) or mission thread
defense (friendly systems).
•
**Cyberspace electromagnetic activities**—FM 3-12 becomes the proponent for and modifies the
term cyberspace electromagnetic activities. Details the cyberspace electromagnetic activities
section’s role in the military decision-making process, integrating processes, and working groups.
Describes how cyberspace electromagnetic activities support the joint task force’s cyberspace
operations, joint electromagnetic spectrum operations, and joint targeting.
•
**Synchronization with signals intelligence**—discusses the synchronization of electromagnetic
support with signals intelligence to maximize their complementary capabilities.
•
**Targeting**—describes the cyber electromagnetic warfare officer’s participation in the targeting
working group and targeting coordination board to identify and nominate targets in cyberspace and
the electromagnetic spectrum and match the targets with available military capabilities to create
desired effects in cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum.
•
**Cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare across the competition continuum**—describes how
commanders can leverage cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare capabilities with other available
**viii**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Introduction**
military capabilities as part of a combined arms approach in competition below armed conflict, in
crisis, and during armed conflict.
•
**Cyber incident response**—outlines the procedures to recognize and respond to cyber incidents,
restore network capabilities, and report incidents through command channels (see appendix A).
•
**Effects requests**—describes how Army units at echelons theater army and below request
cyberspace operations and electromagnetic warfare support from joint, theater-level, and national
capabilities (see appendix C).
•
**Electromagnetic warfare reprogramming**—implements electromagnetic warfare reprogramming
messages (see appendix E) and discusses the Army integrated reprogramming cycle and future
reprogramming enterprise (see appendix I).
•
**Cyberspace operations and electromagnetic warfare training**—describes institutional and
home-station training for cyber and electromagnetic warfare personnel; training exercises; and
combat training center rotations.
•
**Network hardening guide**—describes how cybersecurity personnel and local network defenders
can strengthen the baseline security of the network against known threat tactics, techniques, and
procedures (see appendix H).

## This page intentionally left blank.

### Chapter 1

## Overview and Threat

This chapter begins with an overview of the context for cyberspace and electromagnetic
warfare operations as part of combined arms. It goes on to discuss operational environments,
threats, and hazards. The chapter concludes with a discussion of systems analysis to support
targeting and protection planning.

### SECTION I – CONTEXT FOR CYBERSPACE AND ELECTROMAGNETIC

### WARFARE OPERATIONS

1-1. The Army’s primary responsibility is to organize, train, and equip its forces to conduct prompt and
sustained land combat to defeat enemy ground forces and seize, occupy, and defend land areas. While
operations in cyberspace or the electromagnetic spectrum might dominate military considerations in a
specific, narrow context, conflicts are ultimately resolved on land because that is where people live and make
political decisions and where the basis of national power exists.
1-2. Army forces achieve objectives through the conduct of operations. Operations change factors in the
physical, information, and human dimensions of an operational environment. It is important to understand
that cyberspace operations and electromagnetic warfare (EW) operations exist primarily in the larger context
of combined arms operations.
1-3. Most Army operations occur either below the threshold of armed conflict or during limited
contingencies. Cyberspace and EW operations play valuable roles in shaping operational environments to
U.S. forces’ advantage across the competition continuum. In addition, a wide range of state and non-state
threats continually engage U.S. cyberspace networks during competition below armed conflict to collect
information about friendly forces, steal or modify data, assess vulnerabilities, or plant malicious software in
U.S. systems.
1-4. The United States always retains the option to employ greater levels of force when less coercive methods
are ineffective, and when vital interests or national survival are at stake. This requires Army forces to be
prepared for the most demanding and dangerous types of operations. Offensive and defensive cyberspace and
EW capabilities are parts of the Army’s combined arms approach to operations, both to protect itself and to
create converged effects in multiple domains during large-scale combat operations.

## MULTIDOMAIN OPERATIONS

1-5. All Army operations are multidomain operations. Multidomain operations are the combined arms
employment of joint and Army capabilities to create and exploit relative advantages to achieve objectives,
defeat enemy forces, and consolidate gains on behalf of joint force commanders (FM 3-0). Multidomain
operations are the Army’s contribution to joint operations, which span the competition continuum. Army
forces use all available combat power in each domain to accomplish missions at least cost. Cyberspace and
EW operations contribute to operations by affecting conditions in an assigned area to create temporary
windows of relative advantage in the air, land, maritime, space, and cyberspace domains and the
electromagnetic spectrum. Refer to FM 3-0 for more information about multidomain operations.

### COMBINEDARMS

1-6. Cyberspace and EW operations are key contributors to a combined arms approach. Combined arms is
the synchronized and simultaneous application of arms to achieve an effect greater than if each element was
used separately or sequentially (ADP 3-0). Leaders apply all relevant military capabilities—including
cyberspace and EW capabilities—in complementary and reinforcing ways to amplify their effects. The
combined arms approach to operations during competition, crisis, and armed conflict is foundational to
exploiting capabilities across all domains.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**1**
**Chapter 1**
1-7. Army operations take place as part of a larger joint or multinational effort. Commanders must understand
not only their assigned forces and capabilities, but how they can leverage the forces or capabilities provided
by higher echelon Army, joint, and multinational partners. As part of a combined arms approach,
commanders integrate and employ organic capabilities with capabilities provided by higher headquarters to
achieve objectives. Some multinational partners may offer greater capabilities to support a particular mission
or operate under less restrictive rules of engagement. Integration of joint and multinational cyberspace and
EW capabilities into operations increases the commander’s ability to converge effects in multiple domains
to achieve objectives.
1-8. Army forces also benefit from air, maritime, space, and cyberspace capabilities they do not control.
Examples of this support include—
•
Satellite-based positioning, navigation, and timing.
•
Satellite communications.
•
Intelligence, surveillance, and reconnaissance.
•
Close air support.
•
Over-the-shore logistics.
•
Other Services’ support.
•
Multinational partners’ support.
•
Effects from joint offensive cyberspace operations.
1-9. Many supporting joint and multinational capabilities are integrated at the corps or field army to shape
conditions in the deep area, though they may provide reinforcing support in the close area. Lower echelons
may not always realize the opportunities created by higher echelons or other forces operating in other
domains, but leaders should understand that the absence of those supporting capabilities affects their concepts
of operations, decision making, and operational risk. Refer to FM 3-0 for more information about combined
arms and FM 3-16 for more information about the Army in multinational operations.

### RELATIVEADVANTAGES

1-10. A position of relative advantage is a location or the establishment of a favorable condition within the
area of operations that provides the commander with temporary freedom of action to enhance combat power
over an enemy or influence the enemy to accept risk and move to a position of disadvantage. Relative
advantages are characterized as human, information, or physical, and they complement each other. During
operations, small advantages can have significant impacts on the outcome of the mission, particularly when
they accrue over time. Creating and exploiting relative advantages are therefore necessary for all operations,
and they become even more critical when opposing sides are evenly matched. The ability to plan, integrate,
and synchronize space, cyberspace, and EW capabilities into combined arms at the most effective echelon
expands options for creating relative advantages to exploit. Most maneuver commanders are skilled at
creating positions of relative advantage using available combined arms capabilities. Commanders must
integrate organic and supporting cyberspace and EW capabilities into their combined arms approach to
protect friendly capabilities and affect threat sensing, decision making, and information warfare capabilities.
Refer to FM 3-0 for more information about relative advantages.

### INFORMATIONADVANTAGE

1-11. In addition to creating physical and human advantages, cyberspace and EW capabilities are key
contributors to creating and exploiting information advantages. Information advantage is a condition when a
force holds the initiative in terms of situational understanding, decision making, and relevant actor behavior
(ADP 3-13). While friendly forces seek information advantages, threat forces are doing the same. As such,
an information advantage is something to gain, protect, and exploit across as many domains as possible.
Commanders can achieve a relative advantage by making better and faster decisions than the enemy.
Cyberspace and EW operations can protect and speed friendly decisions and degrade or slow enemy decisions
to contribute to information advantage.
1-12. Army forces integrate all relevant military capabilities, including cyberspace and EW operations,
through the planning and execution of information activities. An information activity is a collection of tasks
linked by purpose to affect how humans and automated systems derive meaning from, use, and act upon, or
are influenced by, information (ADP 3-13). Each information activity incorporates several tasks and subtasks
**2**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Overview and Threat**
from the warfighting functions to achieve a variety of friendly and threat-based objectives. The information
activities are—
•
Enable.
•
Protect.
•
Inform.
•
Influence.
•
Attack.
1-13. Friendly actions in cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum may contribute to information
advantage by—
•
Enabling friendly decision making.
•
Protecting friendly information.
•
Defending networks and command and control information systems.
•
Informing friendly audiences.
•
Influencing threat and neutral actors.
•
Attacking enemy command and control or information warfare capabilities.
**Enable**
1-14. The enable information activity includes tasks that enhance friendly command and control. The focus
of this activity is to improve situational understanding, decision making, and communications. Cyberspace
operations and electromagnetic support, augmented with intelligence support, contribute to a holistic
understanding of an operational environment, including friendly, threat, and neutral use of cyberspace and
the electromagnetic spectrum. Up-to-date situational understanding enables commanders’ decision making
and assists in targeting.
**Electromagnetic Support**
1-15. Electromagnetic support provides the ability to—
•
See and understand friendly use of, and appearance within, the electromagnetic spectrum and
identify friendly vulnerabilities in the electromagnetic spectrum.
•
See and understand the threat’s use of the electromagnetic spectrum and identify threat weaknesses
to exploit through attack activities.
•
Support rapid decision making and dynamic targeting by locating and characterizing threat emitters.
**Electromagnetic Protection**
1-16. Electromagnetic protection provides capabilities to—
•
Ensure spectrum-dependent devices can operate effectively despite threat or environmental
interference.
•
Protect friendly electromagnetic emissions from threat interception, direction finding, or
exploitation.
**Cyberspace-Enabled Activities**
1-17. Cyberspace-enabled activities support the ability to—
•
Create conditions of shared situational understanding.
•
Coordinate across echelons.
•
Link sensors with decision makers and decision makers with lethal and nonlethal attack assets.
**Intelligence Support**
1-18. Units at echelons corps and below may have limited ability to gain situational understanding of threat
and neutral use of cyberspace. Units may require augmentation from theater- or national-level intelligence
elements to develop this understanding. Signals intelligence (SIGINT) elements can perform many of the
same functions as electromagnetic support to improve commanders’ understanding of friendly, neutral, and
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**3**
**Chapter 1**
threat use of the electromagnetic spectrum. SIGINT functions differ from EW beyond initial signals
collection and geolocation.
**Protect**
1-19. In the cyberspace domain, the protect information activity includes tasks that secure friendly data,
information, and networks. This activity focuses on denying threat access to friendly data and information
while preserving friendly communications capabilities. Preventing threat access to friendly data and
information not only protects the friendly force but hinders the threat’s ability to accurately understand
situations and make effective decisions. Information protection contributes to broader protection activities.
**Defensive Cyberspace Operations and Cybersecurity**
1-20. Defensive cyberspace operations and cybersecurity combine active and passive measures to secure
friendly information resident on or transiting communications and information technology systems. See
paragraph 2-8 for more information about defensive cyberspace operations. Refer to ATP 6-02.71 for more
information about cybersecurity.
**Electromagnetic Protection**
1-21. Electromagnetic protection actions—including emission control (EMCON)—secure friendly
information against threat intercept and analysis. Electromagnetic protection enables information protection
and force protection by minimizing unnecessary electromagnetic energy to avoid threat detection and
geolocation. Encryption, both communications security and transmission security, prevent threat forces from
exploiting detected communications signals. For transmissions in the clear, the use of brevity codes according
to signal operating instructions may provide short-term protection against exploitation. Electromagnetic
protection activities include implementing techniques for—
•
Low probability of intercept.
•
Low probability of detection.
•
Low probability of exploitation.
•
Low probability of positioning.
1-22. Refer to ATP 3-12.3 for more information about electromagnetic protection. Refer to ATP 6-02.75 for
more information about communications security and transmission security. Refer to ATP 1-02.1 for more
information about brevity codes.
**Inform**
1-23. The inform information activity includes tasks that explain relevant military operations and activities
to various audiences. The focus of this activity is to maintain the trust and confidence of internal audiences
(Soldiers, Department of the Army Civilians, contractors, and family members) and external audiences
(domestic and international audiences). Cyberspace and EW operations’ primary contribution to the inform
activity results from enabling and protecting the friendly network used to inform internal audiences.
**Influence**
1-24. The influence information activity includes tasks that affect the thinking and ultimately the behavior of
threats and other foreign audiences. This activity focuses on reinforcing or changing how individuals and
groups think, feel, and act in support of objectives. Actions in cyberspace can influence foreign audiences by
delivering real or perceived impacts to the threat military or civilian population. Army forces deliberately
plan actions and supporting messages to affect threat perceptions and behavior. As part of operations,
commanders direct or coordinate for military deception, military information support operations, civil affairs
operations, and Soldier and leader engagements to influence foreign relevant actors. EW elements can
conduct electromagnetic deception to deceive threat commanders regarding friendly positions, disposition,
and activities and cloud threat decision making. Refer to ATP 3-12.3 for more information about
electromagnetic deception.
**4**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Overview and Threat**
**Attack**
1-25. The attack information activity includes tasks that affect the threat’s ability to exercise command and
control. The focus of this activity is to affect the threat’s physical capabilities used to communicate and
conduct information warfare. Threat command posts, communications nodes, networks, information systems,
and sensors—surveillance, target acquisition, and radar—are often high-payoff targets for Army forces. As
part of the scheme of fires, Army forces attack these targets using all available fires, including lethal fires,
electromagnetic attack, and offensive cyberspace operations to destroy or degrade the threat’s command and
control and information warfare capabilities. U.S. forces may also use offensive cyberspace capabilities to
attack the threat data and information used to support decision making.
**Degrade Threat Command and Control**
1-26. Army forces can degrade threat command and control capabilities through movement and maneuver or
lethal and nonlethal attack. Faced with degraded command and control capabilities, threat commanders will
have difficulty synchronizing maneuver and lethal and nonlethal fires against friendly forces. Nonlethal
capabilities used to attack threat command and control include electromagnetic attacks and cyberspace
attacks. Electromagnetic support activities help identify and locate threat command and control nodes. The
cyber electromagnetic warfare officer (CEWO) participates in the targeting working group and the targeting
coordination board to nominate targets and pair the targets with the appropriate attack or delivery system.
Refer to ATP 3-12.3 for more information about electromagnetic support techniques. See chapter 4 for more
information about the CEWO’s role in targeting.
**Affect Threat Information Warfare Capabilities**
1-27. Threat information warfare capabilities disrupt friendly networks, use of the electromagnetic spectrum,
and use of information. Threat information warfare also includes deception and perception management.
Many threat information warfare capabilities rely on cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum. While
the purpose of attacking threat information warfare capabilities differs from attacking threat command and
control, the means of targeting and attack are largely the same. Disrupting, degrading, or denying threat
information warfare makes it difficult for threat commanders to conduct integrated malicious cyberspace
activities, electromagnetic attack, and influence operations against friendly forces. See paragraph 1-125 for
more information about threat information warfare.

## CHALLENGES FOR ARMY FORCES

1-28. Trained and ready Army forces support joint force commanders in three strategic contexts—
competition below armed conflict, crisis, and armed conflict. Within these contexts, Army forces shape
operational environments, counter aggression on land during crisis, prevail during large-scale ground combat,
and consolidate gains.

### COMPETITIONBELOWARMEDCONFLICT

1-29. During competition below armed conflict, adversaries may pursue political and military objectives
through malign activities calculated to fall below the threshold which would trigger an armed U.S. response.
These adversaries may conduct cyberspace surveillance and reconnaissance to collect intelligence and
malicious cyberspace activity to disrupt U.S. command and control capabilities or critical infrastructure, or
to corrupt data storage or weapon system software. Some adversaries have the ability and the will to conduct
damaging malicious cyberspace activity against critical infrastructure and the economy in the homeland to
extend the battlefield globally and prevent or disrupt deployment of U.S. forces from home station into an
overseas theater. During competition, adversaries will likely employ cyberspace and EW capabilities covertly
with limited attribution to remain below the level that might justify an armed response.
**Note****.**The DOD cyberspace operations mission labels of Department of Defense information
network operations (DODIN operations), defensive cyberspace operations and offensive
cyberspace operations are based on commanders’ objectives and intent. Because threat
commanders’ objectives and intent cannot be known with certainty, ‘malicious cyberspace
activity’ generally describes all threat activities in cyberspace.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**5**
**Chapter 1**

### CRISIS

1-30. During a crisis, adversaries are not yet using lethal force as the primary means for achieving their
objectives, but the situation may require a rapid response by forces prepared to fight to deter further
aggression. When directed, the Army provides a joint force commander with forces to help deter further
provocation and build sufficient combat power to maintain or reestablish conventional deterrence.
Adversaries may attempt to conceal attribution for malicious cyberspace and EW activities to avoid
provoking a military response. Under some conditions, adversaries may conduct some of these activities
overtly to signal their resolve and discourage U.S. and allied forces while remaining below the level of armed
conflict. Adversaries will likely use malicious cyberspace activities and electromagnetic attacks against
friendly forces and critical civilian infrastructure to disrupt deployment of forces into theater.
1-31. The introduction of significant land forces demonstrates the will to impose costs, provides options to
joint force and national leaders, and signals a high level of national commitment. As a crisis develops, the
responsible joint force commander continues to employ capabilities from all Services to gain and maintain
information advantages against the threat and set conditions in the operational area for success if the crisis
transitions to armed conflict. Adversaries will attempt to penetrate U.S. networks, either to collect
intelligence about U.S. capabilities, disposition, and intentions, or to plant malicious code to disrupt the
friendly network. Adversaries will use electromagnetic sensing to determine forward deployed friendly
forces’ locations, strength, and disposition. Adversaries may use electromagnetic jamming to identify
friendly tactics, techniques, and procedures to counter jamming effects.

### ARMEDCONFLICT

1-32. The employment of lethal force is the defining characteristic of armed conflict, and it is the primary
function of the Army. Lethality’s immediate effect is in the physical dimension—reducing the enemy’s
capability and capacity to fight. Lethality extends into the information and human dimensions where it
influences the enemy’s behavior, decision making, and their will to fight. During armed conflict, the enemy
will use offensive cyberspace and EW capabilities to attack friendly communications, information, and data
to affect friendly situational understanding and ability to exercise command and control. Army forces
integrate cyberspace and EW operations into combined arms to—
•
Counter the enemy’s efforts to affect friendly command and control capabilities.
•
Confuse, manipulate, or deceive enemy understanding and decision making.
•
Degrade, deny, disrupt, corrupt, or destroy the enemy’s command and control systems and their
surveillance and reconnaissance capabilities.

### PERSISTENTTHREAT

1-33. Army forces must understand the persistent nature of threat cyberspace and EW activities as they
prepare to conduct operations in competition below armed conflict, including in the United States. Army
forces must account for being under constant contact in the cyberspace domain and the electromagnetic
spectrum and the threat’s ability to gain valuable information about the conditions, disposition, and
capabilities of U.S. forces, wherever they are located. Army forces and individuals are in constant
electromagnetic contact with adversaries who persistently probe and disrupt individual, group, and Army
capabilities dependent on space, cyberspace, and the electromagnetic spectrum. Adversaries will attempt to
establish persistent access to U.S. systems and wait to create an effect until the time and place of their
choosing to maximize their strategic to tactical advantage. In some cases, the threat may maintain covert
access to U.S. systems for years or decades before attempting to create an effect at the most impactful time
and place.

### SETTING THETHEATER

1-34. Setting the theater is the broad range of activities continuously conducted to establish conditions for
the successful execution of operations in a theater (FM 3-0). Army forces—primarily the theater army—set
the theater during competition below armed conflict to enable quick transitions during crisis and conflict.
Setting the theater for cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare operations supports commanders’ ability to—
•
See themselves physically and virtually.
•
See the threat and account for being under constant observation.
•
Employ combinations of active and passive protection.
**6**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Overview and Threat**
1-35. The theater army leverages theater-assigned and national-level capabilities to develop comprehensive
understanding of—
•
Friendly, adversary, and civilian use of cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum in theater.
•
Adversary cyberspace and EW capabilities and activities.
•
Friendly vulnerabilities in cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum.
1-36. Theater army efforts to set the theater may include collaboration with multinational partners to assess
and improve their cyberspace and EW capabilities and defenses. The Army will likely depend on host nations
for network support. These assessments may require support from cyber protection teams or national-level
cyber defense forces.

### CONTESTEDDEPLOYMENT

1-37. Army forces cannot expect to deploy without being challenged by the threat. For decades, U.S. military
forces have conducted uncontested and generally predictable deployments from home stations to operational
theaters because threats could not significantly affect deploying units at home station or while in transit to a
theater of operations. This is no longer the case. Peer threats have the capability and capacity to observe,
disrupt, delay, and attack U.S. forces at any stage of force projection, including at home stations in the United
States and overseas. Commanders and staffs must therefore assume friendly forces are always under
observation and contact when planning and executing deployments. Threat activities to contest deployment
begin with attacks at the home station and on critical infrastructure and continue through fort-to-port
movement. See chapter 5 for more information about mitigating contested deployments and protecting
deploying forces from fort to port.

### EARLY ANDINITIALENTRY

1-38. Army forces must be ready to deploy on short notice to austere locations and be capable of immediately
conducting combat operations. During the initial phases of an operation, Army units may face numerically
or qualitatively superior threats. The first deploying units must be able to defend themselves and collect
information about threat activities so they can provide reaction time and freedom of maneuver for follow-on
forces. During joint forcible entry, Army cyberspace and EW capabilities may provide critical support to the
suppression of enemy air defenses and protection from enemy long-range fires to overcome enemy antiaccess
and area denial. Forward-stationed forces may defend critical terrain with other multinational forces to delay
enemy offensive operations. Some forward-stationed forces may defend joint bases and aerial and seaports
of debarkation to enable flow of follow on forces and protect strategic and operational lines of
communications. Electromagnetic protection is critical to the survival of forward-deployed Army forces as
they fight in relative isolation in the early stages of combat operations.

### PRECISIONFIRES ANDWEAPONS OFMASSDESTRUCTION

1-39. The likelihood an enemy will employ long-range precision fires and weapons of mass destruction
increases during large-scale combat operations—particularly against command and control nodes,
sustainment nodes, assembly areas, and critical infrastructure. Commanders should disperse forces as much
as is tactically prudent to manage the risk that an enemy will locate friendly forces and engage them with
long-range precision fires and weapons of mass destruction. Units should pay particular attention to reducing
the electromagnetic signatures of command posts through EMCON to reduce the probability the enemy will
locate the command post and target it with lethal fires. Dispersion increases the difficulty of both command
and control and sustainment and may increase reliance on spectrum-dependent communications capabilities.
Commanders should balance command post protection measures with the need for effective command and
control and accept prudent risk. Operation plans should account for increased security, site defense, and
EMCON requirements for dispersed forces.

### CONGESTEDENVIRONMENT

1-40. Gaining and maintaining access to the electromagnetic spectrum is critical to achieving freedom of
action in cyberspace in support of joint and Army operations. All U.S. forces and supporting agencies depend
on a wide range of spectrum-dependent capabilities to enable and execute operations in the air, land,
maritime, space, and cyberspace domains. Spectrum-dependent capabilities include—
•
Communications.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**7**
**Chapter 1**
•
Information collection.
•
EW.
•
Precision-guided munitions.
•
Aviation.
•
Unmanned aircraft systems.
•
Unmanned ground vehicles.
•
Radars.
•
Satellite-based positioning, navigation, and timing.
•
Any system that transports data or voice communication through the electromagnetic spectrum.
1-41. Within the electromagnetic spectrum, joint forces contend with civil agencies, commercial entities,
allied forces, and threats for use of a common electromagnetic spectrum resource. Competing for the finite
available bandwidth sometimes results in an extremely congested electromagnetic spectrum, particularly
when operating in developed nations.
1-42. Staffs plan communications and network capabilities to support all anticipated requirements in their
assigned area. However, in a congested electromagnetic environment, there may be inadequate spectrum
availability to support all missions. Staffs must deconflict EW and signal use of the electromagnetic spectrum
in collaboration with the G-6 or S-6 spectrum manager, the G-2 or S-2, and SIGINT elements. Changes in
the operational variables—political, military, economic, social, information, infrastructure, physical
environment, and time or the mission variables of mission, enemy, terrain and weather, troops and support
available, time available, civil considerations, and informational considerations—METT-TC (I) could
change spectrum requirements beyond the capabilities of assigned and attached forces. Informational
considerations are those aspects of the human, information, and physical dimensions that affect how humans
and automated systems derive meaning from, use, act upon, and are impacted by information (FM 3-0). Refer
to FM 5-0 for a more complete discussion of the operational and mission variables.

### CONTESTEDENVIRONMENT

1-43. Enemies and adversaries may deliberately attempt to deny friendly use of the electromagnetic spectrum,
space, cyberspace, and terrestrial systems. Due to heavy joint reliance on advanced communications systems,
such an attack may be a central element of any enemy or adversary antiaccess and area denial strategy,
requiring a higher degree of protection for friendly command and control systems and planning for operations
in a denied or degraded environment.
1-44. Threat cyberspace and EW capabilities jeopardize U.S. freedom of action in cyberspace and the
electromagnetic spectrum. Adversaries understand the extent of U.S. forces’ reliance on communications and
automated information systems. Because communications are key to the exercise of command and control,
U.S. military communications and information networks present high-value targets for enemies and
adversaries. In future conflicts, enemies and adversaries will contest U.S. capabilities in cyberspace and the
electromagnetic spectrum to deny operational access and diminish the effectiveness of U.S. and allied forces.
1-45. An enemy can use radio frequency direction-finding equipment to locate radio frequency emitters.
Once the enemy determines an accurate location, enemy forces can maneuver or direct lethal or nonlethal
fires to destroy or degrade the capability. Vulnerable emitters include—
•
Radios.
•
Satellite communications terminals.
•
Counter-improvised explosive device systems.
•
Radars.
•
Unmanned aircraft systems.
•
Unmanned ground vehicles.
•
Cell phones.
1-46. Implementing electromagnetic protection techniques based on current intelligence estimates may
mitigate a threat’s ability to find and attack key communications nodes or command posts. The G-2 or S-2
section defines the threat’s electromagnetic order of battle. The CEWO’s knowledge of threat EW capabilities
and techniques helps inform communications and electromagnetic protection plans that limit the threat’s
effectiveness.
**8**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Overview and Threat**
1-47. G-6 or S-6 section cybersecurity personnel must protect network capabilities by applying the DOD risk
management framework and cybersecurity policies. Local network defenders must be prepared to defend the
network in case of malicious cyberspace activity.
1-48. Synchronizing signal support with cyberspace, EW, intelligence, space, and other information activities
is key to achieving and maintaining freedom of action in contested cyberspace and the electromagnetic
spectrum while denying the same to threats. Synchronization is the arrangement of military actions in time,
space, and purpose to produce maximum relative combat power at a decisive place and time (JP 2-0).
Synchronization provides the ability to execute multiple related and mutually supporting tasks in different
locations at the same time, producing greater effects than executing each in isolation.
1-49. The cyberspace electromagnetic activities (CEMA) section plays an important role in several
integrating processes and working groups, including—
•
Intelligence preparation of the operational environment.
•
Information collection.
•
Risk management
•
Targeting process.
•
CEMA working group.
•
Targeting working group.
1-50. Synchronization of capabilities across multiple domains and warfighting functions maximizes their
inherently complementary effects in and through cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum. Planning for
locations of automated systems and network transport must account for threat conditions. Planning must also
support maneuver if attacked.
**Degraded**
1-51. The cyberspace and space domains grow in importance as global competitors, regional competitors,
and non-state actors invest in capabilities to protect their access and disrupt or deny access to others. Although
Army forces have grown accustomed to communicating freely without fear of jamming or interception,
threats are likely to use technological advances in cyberspace and vulnerabilities in the electromagnetic
spectrum to attack U.S. cyberspace and spectrum-dependent capabilities.
1-52. A broad array of threat actors will challenge the joint force’s freedom of action in space, cyberspace,
and the electromagnetic spectrum. For example, a threat that jams positioning, navigation, and timing
satellites may degrade precision fires, situational understanding, and battlespace deconfliction. Army
commanders must protect their own systems and disrupt the enemy’s ability to operate while being prepared
to operate with degraded communications and reduced access to cyberspace- and spectrum-dependent
capabilities.
1-53. While threat action might cause a degraded environment, degraded capabilities may also occur because
of insufficient resources to support all communications requirements. For example, inadequate
electromagnetic spectrum availability in an operational area causes congestion and network latency.
Unintentional electromagnetic interference may also cause degradation.
1-54. Careful operational planning can minimize disrupted operations in a degraded environment. The
CEWO collaborates with the G-3 or S-3 and the G-6 or S-6 to develop electromagnetic protection plans to
preserve spectrum-dependent capabilities. The commander’s priority of effort includes priorities of service.
The staff’s prioritization of effort must support the commander’s plan. Continuity of operations plans;
primary, alternate, contingency, and emergency communication plans; and disciplined initiative under the
mission command philosophy can mitigate the effects of degraded capabilities in cyberspace and the
electromagnetic spectrum.
**Denied**
1-55. Threats may adopt an antiaccess and area denial strategy against U.S. forces. An enemy or adversary
with the ability to field layered and fully integrated antiaccess and area denial capabilities may try to deny
U.S. access to an operational area altogether. Defeating threat antiaccess and area denial to support joint
forcible entry may depend heavily on effects in cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**9**
**Chapter 1**
1-56. Targeting in cyberspace to defeat threat standoff and layered air and missile defenses depends on
targeting undertaken by the theater army and combatant command during competition and crisis in their
efforts to set the theater. Offensive cyberspace effects to defeat threat antiaccess and area denial will likely
be delivered by national-level cyber mission forces.

## UNDERSTANDING AN OPERATIONAL ENVIRONMENT

1-57. An operational environment is the aggregate of the conditions, circumstances, and influences that affect
the employment of capabilities and bear on the decisions of the commander (JP 3-0). For Army forces, an
operational environment includes portions of the air, land, maritime, space, and cyberspace domains
understood through three dimensions (human, physical, and information). The air, land, maritime, and space
domains are defined by their physical characteristics. Cyberspace transits and connects the other domains.
Figure 1-1 illustrates the relationship between the domains and the dimensions of an operational environment.
**Note****.** Joint doctrine describes the components of an operational environment as the physical areas
of the air, land, maritime, and space domains; the information environment (which includes
cyberspace); the electromagnetic spectrum; and other factors. The Army does not use ‘information
environment’ to understand, visualize, and describe an operational environment. Refer to JP 2-0
and JP 5-0 for more information about describing and analyzing an operational environment from
a joint perspective and FM 3-0 for more information about the Army’s approach to understanding
operational environments.
**Figure 1-1. Domains and dimensions of an operational environment**
1-58. Situational understanding of an operational environment accounts for all factors, circumstances, and
conditions that impact the conduct of operations. Thorough understanding of the environment allows leaders
to identify problems, anticipate outcomes, and understand the results of various actions and how these actions
affect planned operations. The common operational picture enables situational understanding by helping
commanders and staffs visualize their operational environment, including all the factors they need to consider
in planning and conducting operations.
1-59. Understanding an operational environment requires continuous reconnaissance, surveillance, security,
and intelligence operations to acquire information. Commanders gather information from multiple sources.
EW and SIGINT elements conducting electromagnetic support in the close area are important reconnaissance
assets. EW and SIGINT teams can use radio frequency direction finding to geolocate threat emitters,
including radios, other communications systems, and radars. This location information can be used
**10**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Overview and Threat**
immediately as combat information or may feed into intelligence channels for further processing and analysis
as intelligence. Combat information is a report that is gathered by or provided to the tactical commander
which, due to its highly perishable nature or the criticality of the situation, cannot be processed into tactical
intelligence before being used to support decision making (FM 2-0).
**Note****.** Despite its name, ‘combat information’ refers to all information of this type provided to
tactical commanders across the competition continuum. It is not limited to armed conflict.
1-60. Commanders and staffs use all available relevant information, whether combat information or
intelligence, to determine how the operational environment affects operations. This allows commanders to
understand which courses of action are the most feasible, acceptable, and suitable. Commanders and staffs
rely on integrated information collection throughout the course of operations to develop an accurate picture
of their operational environment. Information collection is an activity that synchronizes and integrates the
planning and employment of sensors and assets as well as the processing, exploitation, and dissemination
systems in direct support of current and future operations (FM 3-55). EW elements conducting
electromagnetic support are positioned to answer many critical information requirements and can provide
time-sensitive combat information to support decision making and targeting. The CEMA section may refer
some information collected through electromagnetic support activities to the G-2 or S-2 to feed into
intelligence channels for further processing to meet intelligence requirements. Refer to FM 3-55 for more
detailed discussion of information collection.
1-61. Operational environments undergo continual change due to the interactions between various friendly,
threat, and neutral actors and as actors learn and adapt to conditions in the environment. Because operational
environments are complex and dynamic, it is sometimes challenging to determine cause and effect
relationships, leading to ambiguity. There are several tools, processes, and frameworks to assist commanders
and staffs in understanding their operational environments. They include—
•
Domains (refer to FM 3-0).
•
Dimensions (refer to FM 3-0).
•
Operational and mission variables (refer to FM 5-0).
•
Running estimates (refer to ADP 5-0).
•
The Army design methodology (refer to ATP 5-0.1).
•
The military decision-making process—battalion and above (refer to FM 5-0).
•
Troop leading procedures—companies and platoons (refer to FM 5-0).
•
Pre-mission analysis of the operational environment (refer to FM 2-0).
•
Intelligence preparation of the operational environment (see chapter 4).
•
Sustainment preparation of the operational environment (refer to FM 4-0).

## THE CYBERSPACE DOMAIN

1-62. For Army forces, the cyberspace domain is the interdependent networks of information technology
infrastructures and resident data, including the Internet, telecommunication networks, computer systems,
embedded processors and controllers, and relevant portions of the electromagnetic spectrum (FM 3-0). The
cyberspace domain crosses and connects all other domains. Friendly, enemy, adversary, and host-nation
networks, communications systems, computers, cellular telephone systems, social media, and technical
infrastructures are all parts of cyberspace. Cyberspace is congested, contested, and critical to successful
operations. Operations in a geographic area may rely on data and analysis conducted elsewhere but connected
through cyberspace. Planners must account for dependence on these reachback capabilities when making
network and contingency plans.
1-63. Communications and information systems in cyberspace use links and nodes located in the air, land,
maritime, and space domains for connectivity. Therefore, cyberspace is highly dependent on the other
domains and the electromagnetic spectrum. At the same time, due to their interconnected nature, conditions
and effects in cyberspace can affect the other domains. The relationship between space and cyberspace is
unique in that virtually all space operations depend on cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum, and a
critical portion of cyberspace bandwidth can only be provided via satellite communications, which provide
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**11**
**Chapter 1**
a key global connectivity option for cyberspace operations. These interrelationships are important
considerations during planning.
1-64. The Army conducts cyberspace operations and supporting activities as part of Army, joint, and
multinational operations. Because cyberspace is a global communications and data-sharing medium, it is
inherently a joint, interorganizational, and multinational shared resource with signal and intelligence
organizations maintaining significant equities. Data sensed and collected during operations can be correlated
with other data to generate relevant insight. Cyberspace capabilities enable decision making and protect
friendly information.
1-65. Commanders can leverage cyberspace intelligence and reconnaissance capabilities from higher-level
joint and national-level units to gain enhanced understanding of the threat situation. Cyberspace intelligence
and reconnaissance can augment and enhance the understanding a commander gains from other forms of
information collection and intelligence processes.

## THE ELECTROMAGNETIC SPECTRUM

1-66. The electromagnetic spectrum consists of the entire range of wavelengths or frequencies of
electromagnetic radiation extending from the longest radio waves to gamma and cosmic rays (the shortest
wavelengths). The electromagnetic spectrum is a maneuver space that is essential for facilitating control
within the operational environment and impacts all portions of the operational environment and military
operations. Despite the wide range of the electromagnetic spectrum, the usable portions are a limited resource
with competing demands from military, governmental, commercial, and personal users for communications
and noncommunications purposes.
1-67. Because of the dependence on spectrum-dependent capabilities, Army forces have the imperative to
own the electromagnetic spectrum. Military operations and training are executed in an environment
complicated by increasingly challenging demands and constraints on the electromagnetic spectrum. Just as
in the physical domains and in cyberspace, military forces maneuver and conduct operations within the
electromagnetic spectrum to achieve tactical, operational, and strategic advantage. Freedom of maneuver and
action within the electromagnetic spectrum are essential to U.S. and multinational operations. Spectrum use
must be planned, deconflicted, and protected to support command and control, sensing, information
collection, data sharing, and effects delivery at the right time and tempo to support combined arms operations.
1-68. In early use of the electromagnetic spectrum, military forces only relied on radio communications. In
the early years of World War II, spectrum use expanded to extensive use of radar systems. Over the past
several decades, military use of, and dependence on, the electromagnetic spectrum have increased greatly. In
the modern world, nearly every vehicle, combat platform, and Soldier relies to some extent on one or more
spectrum-dependent devices or capabilities, including—
•
Communications and information systems.
•
Positioning, navigation, and timing.
•
Detection and monitoring.
•
Precision fires.
•
Radar.
•
Air defenses.
•
Unmanned aircraft systems or unmanned ground vehicles.
1-69. Nearly every person, civilian and military, relies on the electromagnetic spectrum for many of their
everyday activities, including cellular communications, wireless Internet access, satellite navigation, and
radio and television broadcast. Military users share the limited bandwidth in the electromagnetic spectrum
with many other users, including other militaries (friendly and threat) and civilian users. Spectrum managers
plan friendly use of the electromagnetic spectrum and perform host-nation coordination to deconflict with
friendly military and civilian spectrum use. Preserving friendly civilian access to the electromagnetic
spectrum has significant implications for quality of life and may contribute to, or degrade, civilian
populations’ support of U.S. military action. G-6 or S-6 spectrum managers also collaborate with the CEMA
section and the G-2 or S-2 to deconflict frequency use with EW and SIGINT. Figure 1-2 on page 13 illustrates
the competing friendly, threat, and neutral spectrum-dependent devices sharing the electromagnetic
spectrum.
**12**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Overview and Threat**
**Figure 1-2. Competition for available electromagnetic spectrum resources**
1-70. To preserve warfighting capabilities, commanders must take actions to maintain an operational
advantage with freedom of action in the electromagnetic spectrum. EW capabilities enable commanders to
achieve relative advantages in the electromagnetic spectrum when operating against peer threats who are
equally reliant on spectrum-dependent devices. Electromagnetic support allows leaders to maintain
situational understanding of friendly, threat, and neutral electromagnetic signatures to support protection
planning and targeting. By protecting friendly information systems and signals from threat disruption or
exploitation, a commander can ensure command and control and maintain operations security. Conversely, a
commander might use electromagnetic attack to degrade an enemy’s decision-making processes by
disrupting enemy sensors, communications, or data processing. To achieve an information advantage, a
commander must plan early to integrate EW activities into the overall scheme of maneuver.

### SECTION II – THREATS AND HAZARDS

1-71. U.S. forces face a variety of threats and hazards through cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum.
It is important for commanders and staffs to understand the nature and operational impact of these threats
and hazards to mitigate their effects on operations.

## OVERVIEW

1-72. A threat is any combination of actors, entities, or forces that have the capability and intent to harm
United States forces, United States national interests, or the homeland (ADP 3-0). Threats can generally be
categorized as adversaries, enemies, or insider threats. An adversary is a party acknowledged as potentially
hostile to a friendly party and against which the use of force may be envisaged (JP 3-0). An enemy is a party
identified as hostile against which the use of force is authorized (ADP 3-0). Insider threats present unique
challenges because they are trusted individuals with access to Army capabilities and sensitive operational
information. Hazards are natural or artificial conditions that present risk to operations. The effects of hazards
are considered neutral, since they affect friendly, threat, and neutral parties equally.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**13**
**Chapter 1**

## THREAT ACTIVITIES IN CYBERSPACE AND THE

## ELECTROMAGNETIC SPECTRUM

1-73. Threats will likely have the advantage of initiative, delivery mechanisms, and timing of malicious
cyberspace activity. Some state-sponsored threats commit significant resources to offensive cyberspace
capabilities and follow less restrictive rules for employing these capabilities than U.S. forces. Developing,
testing, and employing system patches and mitigating techniques to counter threat capabilities is time
consuming. A threat exploiting a previously unknown cyberspace vulnerability has an asymmetric advantage
and may significantly disrupt U.S. operations until defensive cyber forces can develop, test, and deploy a
software patch or other mitigating technique. U.S. forces are sometimes heavily dependent on civilian
network infrastructure that may or may not be properly secured against known vulnerabilities and exploits.
1-74. Setting the conditions for significant malicious cyberspace activity or electromagnetic attack can be
very time consuming, but the speed of execution is fast and exceeds the reaction time and decision-making
process of most tactical units. Commanders should recognize the factors U.S. forces will contend with in
cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum. Commanders and staffs must maintain situational
understanding of cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum to enable timely decision making.

### THREATACTIVITIES INCYBERSPACE

1-75. Threats can exploit system vulnerabilities to cause a loss of confidentiality, integrity, or availability of
network and information technology system capabilities. Protecting the information on the Army network is
essential to maintaining freedom of action in cyberspace.
1-76. The distinction between enemies and adversaries is clear in the air, land, maritime, and space domains,
but less so in cyberspace. An adversary may well take hostile actions against U.S. interests in cyberspace
without becoming an enemy that U.S. forces might engage in the other domains. For this discussion, ‘threats’
encompasses both adversaries and enemies.
1-77. U.S. networks and information systems, including the civilian infrastructure on which U.S. forces
depend, face continuous risk from a variety of threats. Every day, these networks come under attack by threats
including—
•
Insider threats.
•
Foreign intelligence entities.
•
Military opponents.
•
Geopolitical rivals.
•
Terrorist groups.
•
Non-state actors (criminal and activist organizations).
**Note****.** While DOD cyber forces secure and defend the Department of Defense information network
(DODIN), the Department of Homeland Security has the primary mission to defend federal
civilian networks within the United States and engages state and local governments and the private
sector to synchronize efforts to defend critical infrastructure.
1-78. Threats use many methods to disrupt, degrade, destroy, exploit, alter, or otherwise adversely affect the
Army’s use of cyberspace. Elements of the network reside across the physical domains, with different
methods of communicating and varying levels of interconnectivity and isolation. Threats may seek to exploit
any network node, communications link, or the data residing in or traversing the nodes and links. Connecting
critical infrastructure to the network exposes it to the risk of malicious cyberspace activity.
1-79. Cyberspace allows threats to operate anonymously or to obscure their identity and location. This allows
threats to pose as friendly actors and deceive individuals into divulging sensitive information or
circumventing security measures—likely without realizing they have done so—through social engineering
or phishing. Threat cyber forces can attack Army systems, networks, and information by manipulating
unsecured systems.
**14**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Overview and Threat**
**Note****.** Social engineering uses techniques that rely on weaknesses in human nature rather than
software. The goal is to deceive people into revealing passwords and other information that
compromise the security of automated information systems and networks.
1-80. The same gateways that enable information exchange and allow legitimate outside users to access the
network present attack vectors threats will attempt to exploit. Threat forces often use the same commercial
networks as noncombatant civilian populations, complicating malicious cyberspace activity attribution and
response.
1-81. The broad range of hostile actors in cyberspace increases complexity in securing and defending the
Army network. The enduring nature of threat malicious cyberspace activities causes U.S. forces to expend
considerable resources and effort securing DOD networks. Network-enabled operations are a force multiplier
and a traditional strength of U.S. forces. However, network-enabled operations also create significant
vulnerabilities. The extent to which U.S. forces rely on networked capabilities presents broad cyberspace and
electromagnetic spectrum attack surfaces.
1-82. Failing to protect even one system can create a foothold into the network and place sensitive data, U.S.
operations, and lives at risk. Even when cyberspace defenders discover and stop an attack, they might not be
able to attribute it to a particular source. Following cybersecurity best practices, defending the network, and
adhering to operations security guidelines help protect DOD networks and data.
1-83. Threats may undertake intelligence gathering in friendly networks, either to support their operations or
to counter U.S. operations. Exploitation reveals information resident on, or transiting through, a system.
Threat intelligence activities can compromise sensitive operational information.
1-84. Trusted insiders with legitimate access to systems pose one of the most difficult threats to counter.
Insider threats arguably present the greatest risk to operations security because they can readily access
sensitive information.
1-85. Threats will systematically and continuously combine all available means to attack U.S. and allied
network capabilities. Peer threats will use deceptive tactics such as phishing attacks to gain access to Army
networks for follow-on malicious cyberspace activities.
1-86. Commanders must recognize the proliferation of threat cyberspace capabilities and their impact on
operations. Threat malicious cyberspace activities can disrupt friendly information systems and degrade joint
command and control. Threat operations in cyberspace are often less encumbered by treaty, law, and policy
restrictions than those self-imposed on U.S. forces. This may give threats an initial advantage in cyberspace.
Army units must maintain an effective cybersecurity program, integrated with defensive cyberspace
capabilities, to secure the network against threat malicious cyberspace activities.
1-87. Malicious cyberspace activity may originate from any of the categories of state and non-state threats;
there is no way to quickly identify the source. This complicates understanding of the threat’s intent, purpose,
and objectives.

### THREATACTIVITIES IN THEELECTROMAGNETICSPECTRUM

1-88. The Russo-Ukrainian War provides an undeniable lesson—mastery of the electromagnetic spectrum is
no longer a luxury, but a prerequisite for success in large-scale combat operations. Russia’s initial
overwhelming use of EW, from crippling command and control networks to blinding Ukrainian defenses,
underscored its potential as a decisive force multiplier. While Ukraine has adapted with commendable
ingenuity, the conflict exposes a critical vulnerability for the Army—a potential gap in preparedness to fight
and win in a highly contested electromagnetic operational environment.
1-89. In the electromagnetic spectrum, radio frequency transmitters used to jam radars and communications
systems may also provide a transmission path to insert viruses and other malicious computer code into
spectrum-dependent devices to spoof or disable them. Electromagnetic attacks applied from the air, land,
maritime, or space domain by manned and unmanned systems can target humans, communications systems,
radars, or other assets. Threat electromagnetic attacks can affect—
•
Satellite-based positioning, navigation, and timing (spoofing or jamming).
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**15**
**Chapter 1**
•
Satellite-based and terrestrial radio frequency communications (jamming).
•
Precision guided munitions (spoofing or jamming).
•
Radars (spoofing or jamming).
•
Unmanned aircraft systems or unmanned ground vehicles (spoofing or jamming).
1-90. Any device that emits electromagnetic energy can be located through radio frequency direction finding.
Once an enemy or adversary locates an emitter, they can exploit the information for combat information or
target the emitter for intelligence collection or lethal or nonlethal fires. This has long been a vulnerability
with peer threats; in recent years there has been a proliferation of relatively inexpensive commercial off-the-
shelf equipment that can accomplish many of the same things. Even a relatively unsophisticated non-state
threat can now locate U.S. communications systems and command posts with relative precision at low cost.
1-91. Any electromagnetic signature that is not common in the operational area will attract attention from the
threat. Examples of electromagnetic signatures that stand out include—
•
Single-channel radios operating in military-specific portions of the spectrum.
•
Encrypted radio signals.
•
X band satellite communications signals.
•
Cell phones with U.S. subscriber identification modules in an overseas area.
1-92. If a threat locates a high density of emitters that are not common to the operational area, they might
correctly assume they have pinpointed a military formation. Experience at combat training centers shows the
radio frequency heat maps of military formations often allow the opposing force to determine position,
disposition, and actions of command posts and maneuver formations. Metadata from government-issued cell
phones sometimes identifies the user by role (for example, the commander, executive officer, or S-3),
identifying them as high-value targets.

## STATE THREATS

1-93. Traditionally, state threats pose the greatest threat in cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum due
to their ability to match or exceed U.S. capabilities and resources. State threats assess that the Army’s reliance
on the electromagnetic spectrum is an exploitable weakness and strive to develop and field EW systems to
disrupt current and projected U.S. capabilities. State threats typically employ broad cyberspace capabilities
as coercive elements of great power competition. State threats design their activities to disrupt U.S. operations
and undermine perceived U.S. technological advantages.
1-94. State agents continually probe U.S. networks to identify areas of weakness, attack to gain a foothold,
and maneuver to a position of advantage so they can create an effect at the time and place of their choosing.
The weakest link is the entry point. State threats employ a wide array of techniques ranging from phishing
scams to insider threats to gain access to critical systems. Opportunistic states may also use coercive methods
with allies or partners to obtain access to shared systems and data. Since state threats assess that they can
conduct these operations with limited attribution and avoid a direct military response, these methods continue
across the competition continuum.
1-95. Many state threats exercise strict control over communications and Internet infrastructure within their
borders and their connections with global Internet. This strict state control offers a measure of sanctuary from
U.S. cyberspace attacks in these nations.

## PEER THREAT

1-96. Peer threats are adversaries or enemies with capabilities and capacity to oppose U.S. forces across
multiple domains worldwide or in a region where they enjoy a relative advantage. Not all state threats are
global or regional peers. Peer threats possess roughly equal combat power in geographic proximity to a
conflict area with U.S. forces. Peer threats employ their resources across multiple domains to exploit
perceived U.S. vulnerabilities.
1-97. Because peer threats can contest the force in all domains, Army forces must be prepared to defend
themselves in all domains and continue to conduct operations when supporting capabilities are not available.
For instance, under the mission command approach, subordinates can continue their missions, understanding
the commander’s intent when communications are disrupted. Units must be able to navigate using alternate
**16**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Overview and Threat**
means, such as map and compass if space-based positioning, navigation, and timing capabilities are
unavailable.
1-98. Peer threats have demonstrated advanced capabilities in long-range precision fires, integrated air
defense, and EW. These threat capabilities demand changes to tactics, techniques, and procedures to counter
the risks they present. Friendly command and control nodes and other electromagnetic emitters must displace
frequently during large-scale combat operations to avoid destruction by enemy fires.
1-99. Peer threats consider U.S. communications, command and control nodes, massed formations, and
critical infrastructure key targets during large-scale combat operations. Commanders should ensure as much
dispersion of their formations as tactically prudent. Locating radio frequency emitters, such as satellite
communications antennas and line of sight radio systems, away from major command posts minimizes loss
of life and command and control capabilities if the enemy targets the communications systems with lethal
fires. Global and regional peers include China, Russia, Iran, and the Democratic People’s Republic of Korea
(North Korea).

### CHINA

1-100. China is the United States’ most active strategic competitor, both militarily and economically, and
will likely continue to challenge U.S. and partner nation strategic interests in the foreseeable future. While
the United States can currently overmatch Chinese power projection capabilities, Chinese asymmetric attacks
may place significant pressure on U.S. and partner nation command and control systems.
