### PREPARATION

4-46. Preparation consists of those activities performed by units and Soldiers to improve their ability to
execute an operation. Preparation creates conditions that improve friendly forces’ opportunities for success
and includes activities such as—
•
Rehearsals—including EMCON plan and triggers.
•
Training.
•
Pre-combat checks and pre-combat inspections.
4-47. Preparation requires commander, staff, unit, and Soldier actions to ensure the force is ready to execute
operations. The CEMA section’s role in preparation focuses on contributions to collection management,
information collection, target development, and protection tasks. The CEWO or CEMA chief participates in
unit rehearsals to prepare for operations. The CEMA section continues to host the CEMA working group and
participates in other scheduled working groups. See paragraph 4-67 for more information about information
collection. See paragraph 4-77 for more information about the CEMA section’s role in targeting. See
appendix B for information about the CEMA section’s role in the protection working group.

### EXECUTION

4-48. Planning and preparation accomplish nothing if the command does not execute effectively. Execution
is the act of putting a plan into action by applying combat power to accomplish the mission and adjusting
operations based on changes in the situation (ADP 5-0).
4-49. Throughout execution, commanders, supported by their staffs, assess the operation’s progress, make
decisions, and direct the application of combat power to seize, retain, and exploit the initiative or counter
threats. Major activities of execution include—
•
Assessment—monitoring current operations and evaluating progress and variances.
•
Decision making—making decisions to exploit opportunities or counter threats.
•
Directing action—applying combat power and resources at decisive points and times.
4-50. During execution, the CEMA section—
•
Ensures cyberspace and EW operations are aligned and sequenced with the scheme of maneuver.
•
Directs the activities of organic EW platoons.
•
Requests cyberspace and EW support as needed from higher echelons and joint and multinational
forces.
**Frequency Deconfliction**
4-51. The CEMA spectrum manager deconflicts frequency use for EW with the spectrum management
operations conducted by the G-6 or S-6 spectrum manager. The G-6 or S-6 spectrum manager maintains a
local database of all known emitters and receivers in the assigned area and provides input for the joint
restricted frequency list through the chain of command to the joint task force joint electromagnetic spectrum
operations cell. The joint electromagnetic spectrum operations cell prepares the joint restricted frequency list
based on coordinated inputs from the J-2, J-3, and J-6 staffs and input from subordinate commands. The joint
restricted frequency list is a time and geographically oriented listing of TABOO, protected, and guarded
functions, nets, and frequencies and limited to the minimum number of frequencies necessary for friendly
forces to accomplish objectives (JP 3-85).
**58**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities**
•
**TABOO frequencies** are any friendly frequency of such importance that it must never be
deliberately jammed or interfered with by friendly forces including international distress, safety,
and controller frequencies (JP 3-85).
•
**Protected frequencies** are friendly, generally time-oriented, frequencies used for a particular
operation, identified and protected to prevent them from being inadvertently jammed by friendly
forces while active electromagnetic warfare operations are directed against hostile forces (JP 3-85).
•
**Guarded frequencies** are a list of time-oriented, enemy frequencies that are currently being
exploited for combat information and intelligence or jammed after the commander has weighed the
potential operational gain against the loss of the technical information (JP 3-85).
4-52. Using frequencies employed by other friendly forces in the same area may cause undesirable
electromagnetic interference or frequency fratricide. Transmitting on guarded frequencies may disrupt
friendly SIGINT operations. Knowing the purpose of spectrum resources and their characteristics allows the
G-6 or S-6 spectrum manager and the CEMA spectrum manager deconflict friendly frequency use with EW
operations and assists with preparation of electromagnetic protection plans.
**Support to Intelligence Operations**
4-53. The CEMA section shares electromagnetic support data from EW teams operating in the close area
with the G-2 or S-2 for possible further processing into intelligence information. The CEWO and CEMA
section exercise technical control over assigned EW forces and coordinate with the G-2 or S-2 to synchronize
EW with SIGINT operations.
**Electromagnetic Attack**
4-54. During execution, EW teams in the close area may conduct electromagnetic attack activities. The
CEMA section exercises technical control over the EW teams and provides electromagnetic attack guidance
based on approved targets within delegated electromagnetic attack control authority. See chapter 3 for details
about electromagnetic attack methods.
**Responding to Enemy Malicious Cyberspace Activity and Electromagnetic Attacks**
4-55. During execution, units may experience enemy malicious cyberspace activity and electromagnetic
attacks. All personnel in the unit need to be able to recognize attack indicators and prepared to respond to
malicious cyberspace activity and electromagnetic attacks. See appendix D for more information about
recognizing and responding to malicious cyberspace activity and electromagnetic attacks.
**Cyberspace Effects**
4-56. During execution, units at echelons corps and below may create effects in cyberspace using available
military capabilities. Units may also request cyberspace effects from theater- or national-level cyberspace
forces based on approved targets. Units submit the CERF (see appendix C for instructions for completing the
CERF).

### ASSESSMENT

4-57. Assessment is determination of the progress toward accomplishing a task, creating a condition, or
achieving an objective (JP 3-0). During assessment, the commander and staff compare intended outcomes
from the operation plan or order and commander’s intent with actual events to determine whether the unit is
effectively employing its forces. Based on ongoing assessments, commanders refine plans and orders, and
issue updated guidance to subordinates.
4-58. Assessment is a continuous process where the staff observes and evaluates the operational environment
and the impact of friendly unit actions against their mission to better inform the commander. Successful staffs
conduct assessments using existing staff elements, supported and coached by the assessment team. The staff
may use indicators in cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum to aid in their assessments. For example,
civilian activity on social media may contribute to assessing the effectiveness of messaging campaigns. The
location and volume of enemy radio traffic may corroborate the effectiveness of a feint. The staff incorporates
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**59**
**Chapter 4**
assessment into their planning of cyberspace and EW operations. Refer to ATP 5-0.3 for more information
about operational assessment.
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Effects**
4-59. The CEWO collaborates with fires elements and the G-2 or S-2 to support battle damage assessment
for cyberspace and EW effects. This includes when lethal fires or other military actions are used to create an
effect in cyberspace or the electromagnetic spectrum. Cyberspace and EW effects are usually difficult to
observe directly. Assessments may rely heavily on measures of performance and may require inference and
second order observations to evaluate measures of effectiveness. For example, a unit may assess the
effectiveness of jamming by observing the enemy’s transition to an alternate communication method.
Assessment of EW effects may be enhanced by coordination with the G-2 or S-2 and SIGINT elements.
Assessment of cyberspace effects may require support from theater- or national-level intelligence
organizations. Understanding the method for assessment and the associated controlling organization is a key
planning task such that coordination is required during execution.
**Protection**
4-60. The protection working group monitors and evaluates the progress of current operations to validate
assumptions made in planning and to continually update changes to the situation. The protection working
group continually meets to monitor threats to protection priorities, and recommend changes to the protection
plan, as required. They also monitor the conduct of operations, looking for variances from the operation order
that affect their areas of expertise. Units assess the impact protection actions have on operational objectives.
As an example, overly restrictive EMCON measures may reduce operational effectiveness while overly
permissive EMCON reduces survivability. Assessment determines whether implementation of these controls
meets or fails to meet established measures of performance and effectiveness.
4-61. Units may also test the effectiveness of their protection plan. They may attempt to perform unauthorized
actions on the network to assess cybersecurity or deploy sensors to measure the effectiveness of EMCON or
other electromagnetic protection actions. Enemy actions offer an alternate perspective to assess friendly
protection. Poor EMCON may result in the rapid engagement of units by enemy indirect fires. Assessment
based on enemy activity generally requires the ability to correlate effective reporting and may trigger planned
or hasty response actions.

## CYBERSPACE ELECTROMAGNETIC ACTIVITIES IN THE

## INTEGRATING PROCESSES

4-62. The CEMA section plays critical roles in some of the integrating processes and shares some interaction
with the rest of the integrating processes. The integrating processes in which the CEMA section participates
are—
•
Intelligence preparation of the operational environment.
•
Information collection.
•
Targeting.
•
Risk management.
•
Knowledge management.

### INTELLIGENCEPREPARATION OF THEOPERATIONALENVIRONMENT

4-63. Intelligence preparation of the operational environment is the systematic process of analyzing the
mission variables of enemy, terrain, weather, and civil considerations in an area of interest to determine their
effect on operations (FM 2-0). The G-2 or S-2 leads intelligence preparation of the operational environment.
Intelligence preparation of the operational environment begins at the start of the military decision-making
process, is synchronized with planning, and continues throughout the operations process.
**G-2 or S-2 Role**
4-64. During intelligence preparation of the operational environment, the G-2 or S-2 supports cyberspace and
EW operations by providing the commander and staff with all-source intelligence on the operational and
**60**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities**
mission variables in an area of interest to determine conditions relevant to those operations. This information
assists the commander and staff in understanding the operational environment, identifies opportunities for
cyberspace and EW operations, and helps in course of action comparison and approval during the military
decision-making process. During intelligence preparation of the operational environment, the G-2 or S-2
provides critical planning inputs to the rest of the staff, including—
•
Threat doctrine, tactics, equipment, capabilities, vulnerabilities, and intent.
•
Threat systems.
•
Threat characteristics, including the electromagnetic order of battle.
•
Identification of areas of interest and areas of influence.
•
Terrain analysis.
•
Threat courses of action (number driven by time available or directed by the commander).
**Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities Section Role**
4-65. The CEMA section provides subject matter expertise on cyberspace and electromagnetic spectrum
aspects of the operational environment, threat, and impacts to the operational environment, including—
•
Civilian use of cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum in the assigned area.
•
Cyberspace- and electromagnetic spectrum-related infrastructure in the assigned area.
•
Threat cyberspace and EW doctrine, tactics, equipment, capabilities, vulnerabilities, and intent.
•
Threat use of cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum.
•
Known or templated locations for threat cyberspace and EW assets (this includes facilities for
national and law enforcement).
•
Ground-based, airborne, and functional EW employment considerations, including line of sight
analysis. This supports the G-2 or S-2 development and refinement of the electromagnetic order of
battle.
4-66. The CEMA section’s subject matter expertise may be augmented or enhanced by access to cyberspace
and electromagnetic support capabilities to support information collection. Refer to ATP 2-01.3 for more
information about intelligence preparation of the operational environment.

### INFORMATIONCOLLECTION

4-67. Information collection helps the commander understand and visualize the operation by aligning
reconnaissance, surveillance, security, and intelligence assets to collect information about identified
information gaps. It begins early in planning and continues throughout the operations process. Information
collection is directly linked to intelligence preparation of the operational environment and supports planning,
targeting, risk management, and decision making throughout the operations process.
4-68. The CEWO coordinates with the collection manager and the technical control and analysis cell to
coordinate how and when in time and space the CEMA section and EW elements can answer information
requirements with organic assets. This coordination includes priority intelligence requirements and friendly
force information requirements. See appendix B for more information about the CEMA section’s role in the
information collection working group.
**Collection Management**
4-69. Collection management is a coordinated staff effort led by the G-2 or S-2. Collection management
involves—
•
Analyzing information and collection requirements
•
Evaluating available collection assets (internal and external)
•
Recommending taskings for information collection assets to the operations staff
•
Submitting requests for information for adjacent and higher echelon collection support
•
Assessing the effectiveness of the information collection plan.
4-70. The CEWO participates in the information collection working group to support collection management.
Refer to ATP 2-01 for more information about collection management.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**61**
**Chapter 4**
**Information Collection Plan**
4-71. The G-3 or S-3 collaborates with the G-2 or S-2 to develop the information collection plan based on
the analysis performed by the entire staff. Through analysis, the staff determines the best way to satisfy each
information requirement. The staff receives information collection tasks and requests for information from
subordinate and adjacent units and higher echelon headquarters. The information collection plan aligns assets
to collection tasks. Additionally, the plan provides coordinating instructions to ensure adequate coverage in
the assigned area.
4-72. As the tactical situation changes, staffs adjust the overall information collection plan to synchronize
collection. This optimizes collection and exploitation capabilities. The staff continually updates requirements
to ensure information collection efforts synchronize with current operations and support future operations
planning. As the information collected answers the commander’s information requirements, the staff updates
the information collection plan.
4-73. The CEWO is a key participant in the information collection working group led by the G-2 or S-2.
During planning, the information collection working group develops the unit’s collection plan to ensure
effective use of collection assets. See appendix B for details about the CEWO’s role in the information
collection working group.
**Combat Information**
4-74. EW teams in the close area collect combat information through electromagnetic support. EW sensors
survey the electromagnetic spectrum and conduct direction finding to provide combat information to support
electromagnetic protection, immediate threat recognition, targeting, assessment, and planning for future
operations. SIGINT teams also conduct electromagnetic support sensing for direction finding, geolocation,
and decision support. Combining the capabilities of EW and SIGINT teams in the close area provides greater
coverage of the assigned area and more accurate direction finding.
**Decision Support**
4-75. EW teams in the close area may be positioned to provide combat information through both
electromagnetic support and direct observation. This combat information helps commanders understand
enemy strength, position, and disposition to support decision making. The CEMA section exercises technical
control of EW teams and reports combat information to support decision making to the G-3 or S-3.
**Targeting**
4-76. EW teams conduct direction finding to locate enemy emitters and report this information to the CEMA
section and the G-2 or S-2. Locating enemy emitters of interest and characterizing their activities supports
the CEMA section’s role in targeting. This includes either deliberate targets based on the approved target list,
or dynamic targeting—prosecuting targets of opportunity based on the tactical situation.

### TARGETINGPROCESS

4-77. CEMA sections play a critical role in the targeting process at echelon. They provide key insights about
a target’s criticality in cyberspace, its accessibility in cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum, and its
vulnerability to existing cyberspace and EW capabilities. They assess the ability to engage targets through
cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum in the short, medium, and long term and request the
development of new capabilities to posture for future operations. The CEMA section uses systems analysis
to identify additional effects chains to meet the commander’s intent.
**Targeting Guidance**
4-78. The commander’s targeting guidance must be articulated clearly and simply to enhance understanding.
The guidance must be easily understood by all staff sections. Targeting guidance focuses on essential threat
capabilities and functions that could interfere with the achievement of friendly objectives.
4-79. The commander’s targeting guidance describes the desired effects to be generated by lethal fires,
physical attack, cyberspace attack, electromagnetic attack, or other available capabilities. Targeting enables
**62**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities**
the commander to produce desired effects through various lethal and nonlethal capabilities. Capabilities
associated with one desired effect may also contribute to other desired effects. For example, a delay can result
from disrupting, diverting, or destroying enemy capabilities or targets.
4-80. The desired effects for cyberspace and electromagnetic attacks generally affect systems or
transmissions between systems rather than units and formations. As a result, certain effects terms and tactical
tasks are generally more applicable than others for describing cyberspace and electromagnetic attack effects.
For example, units may employ omnidirectional jamming to deny the enemy use of the electromagnetic
spectrum, or an attack may trigger a system reboot in a radar system to temporarily neutralize enemy air
defense. Through convergence with other combined arms actions, these attacks may contribute to compound
effects on enemy units and actions.
4-81. When multiple terms could apply to a given attack, the targeting guidance should use the terms best
aligned with the commander’s intent. Cyberspace and EW capabilities may target transmission media, enemy
systems, or enemy units.

### Transmission or Medium-Focused Effects

**Interdict**—a tactical mission task where a unit prevents, disrupts, or delays the
enemy’s use of an area or route in any domain (FM 3-90). It is an offensive action that
desynchronizes the enemy.
**Deny**—task to hinder or prevent the enemy from using terrain, space, personnel,
supplies, or facilities. (ATP 3-21.20).
**Example:** Use of omnidirectional jamming to interdict enemy radio communications or
deny the use of a particular portion of the electromagnetic spectrum.

### System-Focused Effects

**Suppress**—a tactical mission task in which a unit temporarily degrades a force or
weapon system from accomplishing its mission (FM 3-90).
**Neutralize**—a tactical mission task in which a unit renders the enemy incapable of
interfering with an operation (FM 3-90).
**Destroy**—a tactical mission task that physically renders an enemy force combat
ineffective until it is reconstituted (FM 3-90).
**Deceive**—a unit may provide specific input or otherwise manipulate a system to create
an inaccurate or misleading interpretation of conditions. The target of the deception
may include the system itself, the operator of the system, or enemy decision makers.
**Examples:**
• Jamming an enemy radar system to suppress integrated air defenses.
• Using directed energy to destroy an unmanned aircraft system or cause it to crash.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**63**
**Chapter 4**

### Unit-Focused Effects

**Disrupt**—a tactical mission task in which a unit upsets an enemy’s formation or tempo
and causes the enemy force to attack prematurely or in a piecemeal fashion (FM
3-90).
**Delay**—when a force under pressure trades space for time by slowing down the
enemy’s momentum and inflicting maximum damage on enemy forces without
becoming decisively engaged. (ADP 3-90)
**Fix**—a tactical mission task in which a unit prevents the enemy from moving from a
specific location for a specific period. (FM 3-90)
**Defeat**—to render a force incapable of achieving its objectives. (ADP 3-0)
**Deceive**—The target of the deception may include a system, the operators of a
system, or enemy decision makers. The deception may be undertaken to achieve a
specific result or to support a larger deception operation.
**Example:** Denying enemy positioning, navigation, and timing or communications to
delay or disrupt their movement or delay the enemy advance.
**Note****.** Unit-focused effects are generally achieved by employing cyber or EW
capabilities in conjunction with other combined arms capabilities such as lethal fires or
maneuver to create mutually supporting or compound effects.
4-82. The commander can direct a variety of nonlethal actions or effects separately or in conjunction with
lethal actions or effects. The commander can also provide restrictions as part of targeting guidance. Targeting
restrictions fall into two categories—the no-strike list and the restricted target list. The no-strike list consists
of objects or entities protected by—
•
The law of armed conflict.
•
International laws.
•
Rules of engagement.
•
Other considerations.
4-83. A restricted target list is a list of valid targets with identified restrictions such as—
•
Limit collateral damage.
•
Preserve select ammo for final protective fires.
•
Do not strike during daytime.
•
Strike only with a certain weapon.
•
Proximity to protected facilities and locations.
**Note****.** Refer to FM 3-57, FM 3-84, FM 6-27, JP 2-0, JP 3-57, and JP 3-60 for additional information
about legal considerations and targeting restrictions.
**The Targeting Process**
4-84. Targeting is the process of selecting and prioritizing targets and matching the appropriate response to
them, considering operational requirements and capabilities (JP 3-0). It is a continuous analytic process used
to identify, prioritize, develop, and affect high-value and high-payoff targets to meet commanders’ objectives.
Targeting provides planners access to detailed target information, supported by the nominating component’s
analytic reasoning. The targeting working group uses this information to select targets and pair them with
**64**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities**
capabilities to deny, degrade, disrupt, destroy, or manipulate the target’s ability to perform its primary
functions.
4-85. The targeting process should align sensors, decision makers, and effects delivery platforms for
validation by the commander to align the necessary resources to shape the operational environment and set
conditions for mission success. The CEMA section’s participation in the targeting process helps align EW
sensors and electromagnetic attack capabilities to possible targets.
4-86. Cyberspace and EW operations support provide timely and accurate targeting information for lethal or
nonlethal attack. Cyberspace and EW assets may be able to detect threats well ahead of visual or direct
contact. The CEWO is a key participant in the targeting working group and targeting coordination board to
identify spectrum-dependent and cyberspace-dependent capabilities used by threat forces, aid in selecting
targets, and align effects options that can provide a tactical advantage to support the scheme of maneuver.
See appendix B for more information about the CEWO and CEMA section’s role in the targeting working
group.
**Targeting Restrictions**
4-87. Commanders, leaders, and personnel must understand and apply targeting restrictions regarding—
•
The basic principles of the law of armed conflict.
•
Rules of engagement.
•
General restrictions.
•
Precautions in attack.
•
Separation of military activities.
•
Special protections.
•
National sovereignty.
•
Environmental considerations.
4-88. Targeting restricted areas, civilian populations, and civilian or protected infrastructure is prohibited
under the law of armed conflict. Civilians enjoy protected status unless they participate or take a direct part
in combat, singularly or as a group. Likewise, protected infrastructure that harbors combatants loses its
protected status. Staffs conduct target analysis and direct the application of information attack, possibly
including cyberspace and EW effects.
4-89. Commanders must take positive steps and precautions to avoid excessive incidental civilian casualties
and damage to civilian property. Targeting team members should ensure sound intelligence and vetting are
used during target development and in selecting enemy objectives, not civilian objects, for attacks. Attacks
against installations containing structures that affect natural resources—including dams, dikes, and power
generation facilities—must be carefully considered due to the possibility of catastrophic collateral damage.
**Deliberate and Dynamic Targeting**
4-90. The targeting process breaks down into deliberate and dynamic targeting. Deliberate targets are either
scheduled or on-call. Dynamic targeting is used to prosecute targets of opportunity (unscheduled or
unanticipated targets).
**Deliberate Targeting**
4-91. Deliberate targeting prosecutes planned targets. These targets are known to exist in the assigned area
and have actions scheduled against them. Examples range from targets on target lists in the applicable plan
or order, to targets detected in sufficient time to be placed in the joint air tasking cycle, mission type orders,
or fire support plans. Deliberate targeting produces preplanned, scheduled, and on-call targets for operations.
The targeting process is scalable and tailorable to fit the operational need and planning horizon of the
executing command. Deliberate targeting is used to engage—
•
**Scheduled targets**—planned targets upon which fires or other actions are scheduled for prosecution
at a specified time.
•
**On-call targets**—planned targets upon which fires or other actions are determined using deliberate
targeting and triggered when detected or located using dynamic targeting.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**65**
**Chapter 4**
**Dynamic Targeting**
4-92. When planned targets must be adjusted, dynamic targeting provides a methodology for managing
changes. Dynamic targeting prosecutes targets not scheduled for action in deliberate targeting or that emerge
during an operation. Dynamic targeting provides commanders flexibility for generating effects against targets
during an execution period within a compressed timeline. Dynamic targeting may also be employed to create
lethal and nonlethal effects against on-call targets. Dynamic targeting prosecutes targets of opportunity,
which are categorized as—
•
**Unscheduled targets**—targets that are known to exist in the assigned area, but not deliberately
included in the targeting schedule for a given time period. As an example, EW elements may detect
a fire finding radar placed closer to the forward edge of the battle area than the enemy doctrinal
template predicted. The radar might be prosecuted as an unscheduled target.
•
**Unanticipated targets**—targets that are neither known nor expected to exist in the assigned area.
An unanticipated target could be a known enemy unit assigned to a different theater of operations
showing up in the deep area or an enemy air defense radar not known to be employed in the
operational area. Refer to ATP 3-60.1 for more information about dynamic targeting.
**Targeting in Cyberspace and the Electromagnetic Spectrum**
4-93. When nominating targets in cyberspace, it is important to note that the targeting process focuses on
targets and desired effects over capabilities. This methodology provides flexible employment of combined
arms where multiple capabilities may offer a desired effect. Like any other target, the targeting working group
should focus on what effect would achieve the commander’s desired end state, and when the effect is needed
to support operations. Targeting in cyberspace should match the target and desired effect with available
military capabilities. The denial, degradation, disruption, destruction, or manipulation effects can be created
through lethal or nonlethal attack capabilities—the delivery mechanism is less important compared to target
nomination and validation.
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Effects**
4-94. When requesting or considering delivering effects through cyberspace or the electromagnetic spectrum,
the targeting working group must ensure they integrate closely with their higher joint command. Attacks
through cyberspace may have second- and third-order effects outside of the assigned area or theater of
operations which require coordination with other combatant commands. The use of an offensive cyberspace
capability may preclude its future use against either that target or against any other targets. The attacks may
require high-level approvals to evaluate their military utility relative to the value of ongoing intelligence
operations and planned future operations and the risk of collateral damage to civilian infrastructure.
4-95. The CEMA section uses electromagnetic support data from organic EW forces to identify targets in the
electromagnetic spectrum. These can either be prosecuted as deliberate targets based on the phase of an
operation, or as targets of opportunity through dynamic targeting.
4-96. The CEMA section identifies key terrain in cyberspace during intelligence preparation of the
operational environment and mission relevant terrain in cyberspace through systems analysis and nominates
cyberspace targets through the Army targeting methodology. Key terrain in cyberspace must meet the same
criteria as key terrain in the land domain; it must provide a marked advantage to whichever force holds that
terrain. This includes cyberspace terrain owned by friendly forces—if the enemy gains control of host-nation
cellular or Internet infrastructure, that is an advantage to them—if friendly forces maintain control of the
host-nation infrastructure, the advantage lies with the multinational force.

### RISKMANAGEMENT

4-97. The CEWO is a key participant in risk management throughout the operations process and as a member
of the protection working group. The CEMA section provides subject matter expertise on cyberspace and
electromagnetic risk and electromagnetic radiation hazards during operations. Units incur risk by using
cyberspace and spectrum-dependent devices and relying on cyberspace or EW capabilities and activities.
Threat actions, adjacent friendly forces, and civilian populations can contribute to the cyberspace and EW
risk.
**66**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities**
4-98. Units can manage risk by applying protection actions such as electromagnetic protection, cybersecurity,
operations security, and force protection. They may also implement operational controls such as
contingencies, redundancies, and assessments. Frequency deconfliction between the CEMA spectrum
manager and the G-6 or S-6 spectrum manager is an example of a protection measure to reduce the risk of
frequency fratricide. CEWOs recommend protective measures to mitigate hazards of electromagnetic
radiation to personnel, fuels, and ordnance. Units must also consider the operational risk posed by pervasive
civilian use of cyberspace- and electromagnetic spectrum-dependent devices throughout their operational
environment.

### KNOWLEDGEMANAGEMENT

4-99. The CEWO collaborates with the knowledge manager to ensure knowledge management and
information dissemination management processes get the right information to the right staff personnel to
support targeting and decision making. Units must be prepared to conduct knowledge management in a
denied and degraded environment. In some instances, this may require units being prepared to transition to
analog planning methods and minimizing or ceasing electromagnetic emissions.
4-100. In collaboration with the G-6 or S-6 and the rest of the staff, the CEWO advises on risk decisions
relating to the use of cyberspace- and spectrum-dependent knowledge management systems. They help
balance the operational benefits of knowledge management against the limitations and risks within their
operational environment and the capabilities of the enemy to maximize resilience and survivability. Refer to
ATP 6-01.1 for more information about knowledge management. Refer to ATP 6-02.71 for more information
about information dissemination management.

## INTEGRATION WITH THE JOINT FORCE

4-101. An ARFOR is the Army component and senior Army headquarters of all Army forces assigned or
attached to a combatant command, subordinate joint force command, joint functional command, or
multinational command (FM 3-94). The ARFOR headquarters CEMA section integrates with the joint force
through the combatant command joint cyber center for cyberspace operations, the joint task force’s joint
electromagnetic spectrum operations cell for EW, and the joint frequency management office (if not part of
the joint electromagnetic spectrum operations cell) for spectrum management operations.

### JOINTCYBERSPACEOPERATIONS

4-102. Army units may work as subordinate elements of a joint or combined joint task force, or they may
form the core headquarters of a joint or multinational task force. The Army uses its CEMA staff to integrate
cyberspace and EW capabilities into the joint and multinational operations processes. If not acting as a joint
task force headquarters, the ARFOR headquarters CEMA section coordinates cyberspace operations through
the joint task force or the combatant command joint cyber center.

### JOINTELECTROMAGNETICSPECTRUMOPERATIONS

4-103. Joint electromagnetic spectrum operations include all joint force actions to exploit, attack, protect,
and manage the electromagnetic operational environment. Joint electromagnetic spectrum operations rely on
personnel and systems from EW, electromagnetic spectrum management, intelligence, space, and cyberspace
mission areas. Joint electromagnetic spectrum operations integrate and synchronize EW, spectrum
management, intelligence (including SIGINT), space, and other mission areas to achieve electromagnetic
spectrum superiority. Instead of these mission areas being planned and executed in a minimally coordinated
and stovepiped fashion, joint electromagnetic spectrum operations guidance and processes prioritize,
integrate, synchronize, and deconflict all joint force operations in the electromagnetic operational
environment, enhancing unity of effort. The result is a fully integrated scheme of maneuver in the
electromagnetic operational environment to achieve electromagnetic spectrum superiority and joint force
commander objectives (JP 3-85). Figure 4-2 on page 68 shows the joint electromagnetic spectrum operations
actions.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**

**Chapter 4**
**Figure 4-2. Joint electromagnetic spectrum operations actions**
**Army Approach to Electromagnetic Spectrum Operations**
4-104. Each Service has a unique approach to electromagnetic spectrum operations; the Army’s Service
approach is CEMA. Army commanders and their staffs conduct CEMA to plan, integrate, and synchronize
cyberspace and EW operations as a unified effort to project power in and through cyberspace and the
electromagnetic spectrum (JP 3-85).
4-105. Component commanders should establish electromagnetic spectrum operations cells (or equivalent)
to enable command and control of their respective electromagnetic spectrum operations and to coordinate
their electromagnetic spectrum operations with the joint force through the joint electromagnetic spectrum
operations cell (CJSCM 3320.01D). For the Army, the theater army CEMA section performs the functions
of an electromagnetic spectrum operations cell for all Army forces in the combatant command through the
CEMA working group. The ARFOR CEMA section and CEMA working group are the equivalent for a joint
task force.
4-106. The CEWO hosts the CEMA working group with representation from maneuver, intelligence, fires,
space, information advantage, and spectrum management operations personnel (see paragraph
4-42) to integrate cyberspace operations and EW in operation plans and orders. The G-6 or S-6 spectrum
manager participates in the CEMA working group to deconflict friendly spectrum use with EW and SIGINT
elements and update the joint restricted frequency list (including TABOO, protected, and guarded
frequencies). The ARFOR coordinates EW and spectrum management operations with the joint task force
joint electromagnetic spectrum operations cell and joint frequency management office. Refer to ATP 6-02.70
for more information about the roles and responsibilities of the G-6 or S-6 spectrum manager.
**Joint Integration**
4-107. The joint task force’s joint electromagnetic spectrum operations cell coordinates electromagnetic
spectrum management with the joint frequency management office, and integrates cyberspace and EW
operations with SIGINT, collection management, and space operations to support electromagnetic spectrum
superiority. Refer to AR 525-24 for more information about the relationship of CEMA to joint and
multinational electromagnetic spectrum operations.

**Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities**

### JOINTELECTROMAGNETICSPECTRUMOPERATIONSPLANNING

4-108. Joint force commanders centralize joint electromagnetic spectrum operations planning under the
designated electromagnetic spectrum coordinating authority and decentralize execution to ensure unity of
effort while maintaining tactical flexibility. The joint force prioritizes, integrates, and synchronizes
operations in the electromagnetic spectrum to—
•
Achieve electromagnetic spectrum superiority.
•
Achieve the commander’s objectives.
•
Mitigate electromagnetic interference.
•
Avoid friendly fire electromagnetic attack incidents (frequency fratricide).
**Commander’s Guidance**
4-109. The joint force commander conveys guidance for achieving electromagnetic spectrum superiority
within the joint operations area through their concept of operations. Subordinate commanders plan operations
within the joint force commander’s guidance and intent. The joint force commander’s guidance includes—
•
Operational approach.
•
Mission statement.
•
Commander’s planning guidance.
•
Commander’s intent.
•
Commander’s critical information requirements.
**Staff Estimate**
4-110. The joint electromagnetic spectrum operations staff estimate forms the basis for the commander’s
approach to achieve electromagnetic spectrum superiority. The staff estimate is used during course of action
development and analysis to determine—
•
The spectrum activities and capabilities needed to accomplish the mission.
•
The joint electromagnetic spectrum capabilities required to support operations.
•
The risk to the operation if electromagnetic spectrum superiority is not achieved.
**Joint Electromagnetic Spectrum Operations Appendix**
4-111. The joint electromagnetic spectrum operations appendix to Annex C (Operations) of joint plans and
orders establishes procedures for command and control of forces conducting joint electromagnetic spectrum
operations in the joint operations area and includes spectrum coordination measures, specifying procedures
and rules of engagement for joint force use of the electromagnetic spectrum.
4-112. The joint electromagnetic spectrum operations appendix should form the basis of Appendix 12
(Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities) to Annex C (Operations) to the ARFOR’s operation plan or order
to ensure the Army component operates within the higher commander’s mission and intent. The ARFOR
CEMA appendix becomes the basis of subordinate Army units’ CEMA appendixes.

### JOINTTARGETING

4-113. While the Army plans effects through the Army targeting methodology—decide, detect, deliver, and
assess—as the primary integrating echelon for converged effects, the corps must also integrate into the joint
targeting cycle:
•
Commander’s objectives, targeting guidance, and intent.
•
Target development and prioritization.
•
Capabilities analysis.
•
Commander’s decision and force assignment.
•
Mission planning and force execution.
•
Combat assessment.
4-114. CEMA sections must integrate with the joint targeting cycle to allow time to develop tailored
cyberspace and EW capabilities. While the delivery of effects in cyberspace and the electromagnetic
spectrum can occur quickly, generating capabilities requires significant planning, preparation, software
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**69**
**Chapter 4**
development, and iterations of intelligence and reconnaissance operations. Generally, these types of effects
are only feasible for deliberate targets that are preplanned during concept plan or operation plan development.
Depending on the nature of a capability, it may be able to engage unscheduled targets if the target is exposed
for long enough or meets certain conditions. In these instances, using a tailored capability may or may not
preclude its future use.
4-115. In the event a division or corps is tasked as the ARFOR or tactical headquarters, the targeting team
must be prepared to participate in the joint targeting cycle of the higher headquarters to submit target
nominations for joint capabilities. All effects that cannot be created by organic capability must be requested
through predetermined channels. Higher headquarters typically prescribe the methods for support requests
and target nominations. In addition to participating in the higher headquarters processes, divisions and corps
conduct joint targeting working groups and joint targeting coordination boards at their level for echelon-
specific synchronization and integration. Working group and board facilitators must be conscious of roles,
targeting tasks, and command and support relationships and include all necessary participants. Refer to JP
3-60 for more information about joint targeting.
4-116. Targeting in cyberspace requires close integration with the joint targeting cycle through the joint force
commander and combatant command. This integration is necessary for three main reasons:
•
Targets engaged through cyberspace must be on an approved joint target list or restricted target list.
•
Cyberspace attacks may generate adverse second- and third-order effects that extend far beyond the
requesting commander’s assigned area.
•
National-level intelligence assets may be conducting ongoing exploitation of a requested target.
Approval to engage these targets requires a high-level intelligence gain-loss decision, balancing
continued intelligence value versus the military utility of engaging the target.
4-117. Other Services employ EW capabilities the corps and joint task force may leverage to engage targets
nominated by Army units. Navy and Air Force airborne EW platforms can conduct electromagnetic support
sensing and electromagnetic attack in the deep area, which greatly enhances the corps and division’s ability
to set conditions for lower echelons.
4-118. The CEMA section nominates targets in cyberspace through the Army targeting methodology.
Identified targets should support the commander’s desired end state and objectives. Units forward CERFs
prepared through the Army targeting methodology through their chain of command to the joint task force
headquarters, where the CERF is entered into the joint targeting cycle as a request for service. Developing a
cyberspace capability to engage targets requires extended lead time and high-level approval. Figure 4-3 on
page 71 shows the integration of Army targeting in cyberspace at echelons corps and below with the joint
targeting cycle.

## This page intentionally left blank.

### Chapter 5

## Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in

## Combined Arms

This chapter addresses the contributions of cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare operations
in a combined arms approach. The chapter begins with a discussion of convergence, and
integration with the joint force. It then discusses cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare
operations across the competition continuum.

### SECTION I – CONVERGENCE AND INTEGRATION WITH THE JOINT FORCE

5-1. During large-scale combat operations, a corps headquarters normally functions as a tactical headquarters
under a joint or multinational land component or as the joint force land component command. The corps is
the echelon best positioned and resourced to converge Army and joint capabilities. Convergence is an
outcome created by the concerted employment of capabilities from multiple domains and echelons against
combinations of decisive points in any domain to create effects against a system, formation, decision maker,
or in a specific geographic area (FM 3-0).

## CONVERGING EFFECTS ACROSS DOMAINS

5-2. The corps converges effects in the air, land, maritime, space, and cyberspace domains using organic fires
capabilities, subordinate division and brigade combat team fires and maneuver, and support from the joint
force, other Services, or multinational partners to apply combat power from and in multiple domains to
multiple decisive points. The fires capabilities include both lethal and nonlethal attack or delivery systems.
Electromagnetic attack capabilities supporting convergence may come from the division or brigade combat
teams’ organic EW forces; be provided by supporting Navy or Air Force aerial EW platforms; or be provided
by multinational partners. Offensive cyberspace capabilities supporting convergence are provided by
national-level cyber forces or multinational partners and require extended planning time and coordination
through the combatant command.
5-3. Cyberspace and EW capabilities are key contributors to convergence. Cyberspace and EW effects can
affect the function of enemy command and control communications, sensors, radars, electromagnetic
countermeasures, and navigation systems. Tactical advantages from denied, degraded, disrupted, destroyed,
or manipulated enemy capabilities often come from second- and third-order effects in the air, land, maritime,
and space domains as technical equipment temporarily ceases to function as the enemy requires. This creates
windows of opportunity to apply combat power to decisive points in other domains to present enemy forces
multiple dilemmas they cannot overcome. Refer to FM 3-0 for more information about convergence.

### ELECTROMAGNETICATTACK

5-4. The cascading effects of an electromagnetic attack on enemy command and control communications
may eventually include the enemy adapting their electromagnetic protection tactics, techniques, and
procedures. This could include—
•
Changing hop sets.
•
Using an alternate frequency.
•
Using highly directional antennas.
•
Changing to alternate communications devices.
•
Moving electromagnetic sensors closer to the forward line of enemy troops to geolocate friendly
jammers.
5-5. An enemy adapting their tactics, techniques, and procedures requires commanders and their staffs to
forecast and plan accordingly and may require friendly maneuver to counter.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**73**
**Chapter 5**

### ENGAGINGCYBERSPACETARGETSWITHOTHERMILITARYCAPABILITIES

5-6. The CEMA section and targeting working group must do their due diligence in identifying and
nominating targets in cyberspace. While units at echelons corps and below do not have organic offensive
cyberspace operations capabilities, they can still create effects in cyberspace using available military
capabilities. Units may be able to create desired effects in cyberspace by engaging an approved target with
lethal fires, electromagnetic attack, maneuver forces, or air attack at critical points in an operation.

### COUNTERINGAIR ANDMISSILETHREATS

5-7. Army EW forces counter the air and missile threat by integrating electromagnetic jamming,
electromagnetic deception (decoys), and electromagnetic masking to deny, degrade, or disrupt enemy sensors
from producing accurate and timely targeting information regarding friendly units.

## SUPPORTING JOINT COMMAND AND CONTROL

5-8. CEMA supports joint command and control through support for joint spectrum interference resolution,
situational awareness of friendly electromagnetic signatures, and integration of defensive cyberspace
operations with cybersecurity to protect friendly networks.

## ENABLING OPERATIONS

5-9. CEMA sections at corps and below and EW elements at division and below support the following
enabling operations:
•
Reconnaissance operations.
▪
Hunt operations.
▪
Electromagnetic support.
•
Security operations.
▪
Cyberspace security (in conjunction with the G-6 or S-6).
▪
Electromagnetic protection.
▪
Counterreconnaissance.

### RECONNAISSANCEOPERATIONS

5-10. Brigade combat team, division, and corps commanders use organic or attached ground or air assets and
intelligence elements to accomplish reconnaissance. The purpose of reconnaissance is to gain information
about enemy forces or terrain, and to gain and maintain contact with the enemy.
5-11. Electromagnetic support at echelons division and brigade combat team, integrated with organic
SIGINT capabilities, contributes to reconnaissance through electromagnetic contact. Additionally, EW teams
in the close area may contribute to reconnaissance through direct visual observation and reporting of enemy
movements and activities.
**Hunt Operations**
5-12. Hunt is a tactical mission task undertaken in friendly controlled or contested cyberspace to identify and
characterize threat presence and activity on the network. A hunt operation is analogous to reconnaissance
and security tasks in the land domain.
5-13. Hunt operations enable offensive and defensive cyberspace operations, DODIN operations, and
influence operations. Defensive cyber forces conduct hunt operations to develop the situation and to identify,
create, and preserve options to seize, retain, and exploit the initiative. Hunt operations allow commanders to
visualize cyberspace in their operational environment, understand the situation, and make decisions. Hunt
operations answer commanders’ critical information requirements, identify threat activities, and help mitigate
cyberspace risk.
5-14. The CEMA section submits information requests through the collection manager to support the
commander’s understanding of the cyberspace environment in their areas of operations. Tactical information
requests are prioritized by supporting defensive cyber forces and theater intelligence units. As information
**74**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**
requirements are answered, the CEMA section updates its running estimate to ensure the commander and
staff are aware of friendly, threat, and neutral activities in cyberspace.
**Electromagnetic Support**
5-15. Electromagnetic contact is one of the nine forms of contact (refer to FM 3-0). Electromagnetic support
is a shared mission space between EW and SIGINT forces. EW and SIGINT teams can detect and identify
threat emissions, direction find, and geolocate threat emission points of origin. Army SIGINT personnel have
the additional mission of in-depth signals analysis, processing, and decryption to generate intelligence about
threat activities, plans, and capabilities. Common functions between electromagnetic support and SIGINT
include—
•
Advising for placement of friendly systems.
•
Monitoring the electromagnetic spectrum for friendly awareness (see ourselves).
•
Advising for EMCON plans.
•
Monitoring for evidence of enemy activity (see the enemy).
•
Demodulating unencrypted signals for the purpose of threat identification.
•
Supporting the commander’s situational understanding (understand the operational environment).
•
Enabling tip and cue of assets from the collection plan to support engagement of high-priority
targets.
**Note****.** Certain signal analysis tasks are strictly limited to SIGINT teams by National Security
Agency policies. Refer to ATP 2-22.6 for more information about SIGINT authorities. Refer to
AR 525-24 for more information about signals externals parameters authorities for EW personnel.

### SECURITYOPERATIONS

5-16. Cyber forces support security operations through cyberspace security. EW forces support security
operations through electromagnetic protection and support to counterreconnaissance.
**Cyberspace Security**
5-17. Protection in cyberspace consists of coordinated cybersecurity and defensive cyberspace operations.
The CEMA section supports the G-6 or S-6 implementing the cybersecurity program to secure the network
against known vulnerabilities and identified threat tactics, techniques, and procedures. The CEMA section
also collaborates with the joint cyberspace operations integrated planning element and the G-2 or S-2 for
current threat information to inform defensive cyberspace operations-internal defensive measures.
**Cybersecurity**
5-18. Signal personnel in the G-6 or S-6 establish the baseline security of their portion of the Army network
by applying the risk management framework and cybersecurity measures, including antivirus updates,
software patching, event logging, and analysis to secure the network against known vulnerabilities and
exploits and identify unusual network activity. Refer to ATP 6-02.71 for more information about
cybersecurity measures.
**Defensive Cyberspace Operations**
5-19. Local network defenders perform defensive cyberspace operations-internal defensive measures tasks
in their respective portions of the Army network. If defensive cyberspace operations capabilities are required
beyond the capabilities of local network defenders, the CEMA section submits a request for cyber protection
team or expeditionary CEMA team support through the joint force headquarters.
**Electromagnetic Protection**
5-20. The CEMA section and organic or supporting EW forces support electromagnetic protection by—
•
Assisting with terrain analysis as it affects electromagnetic propagation.
•
Synchronizing plans for spectrum-dependent capabilities with operations.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**75**
**Chapter 5**
▪
Fire finding radar.
▪
Air defense radar.
▪
EMCON plans.
•
Supporting joint spectrum interference resolution investigation and reporting activities.
**Counterreconnaissance**
5-21. Threat forces use a wide range of space-, air-, maritime-, and land-based surveillance, and
reconnaissance capabilities to detect and collect information about U.S. forces. Cyberspace and EW
capabilities contribute to counterreconnaissance to prevent the threat from collecting critical information
through cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum.
**Cyberspace**
5-22. Army forces and individuals are in constant electromagnetic contact with threats who persistently probe
and disrupt individual, group, and Army network capabilities. Local network defenders perform defensive
cyberspace operations-internal defensive measures to defend the DODIN against enemy cyberspace
surveillance and reconnaissance.
**Electromagnetic Warfare**
5-23. EW and SIGINT elements support counterreconnaissance through the conduct of electromagnetic
support to detect, identify and geolocate emitters used by threat reconnaissance elements. EW teams may
conduct electromagnetic attack to disrupt threat reconnaissance elements’ reporting. Combat information
provided by the EW team further informs targeting decisions regarding the employment of lethal or nonlethal
capabilities against threat reconnaissance elements.

### SECTION II – CYBERSPACE AND ELECTROMAGNETIC WARFARE

### OPERATIONS ACROSS THE COMPETITION CONTINUUM

5-24. The current strategic environment is typified by continuous competition among great powers which
challenges the security environment and operational deterrence. Subsequently, today’s reality is that
commanders face a complex environment that requires employment of available combat power across all
domains to accomplish missions across the competition continuum.

## RELATIVE WEIGHTING OF EFFORT

5-25. The weighting of effort between cyberspace and EW capabilities varies across the competition
continuum. Army units and joint forces must perform defensive cyberspace operations tasks across the
competition continuum to defend friendly network capabilities from threat cyberspace activities.
5-26. In competition and crisis, most friendly offensive cyberspace operations take place at the national and
theater strategic levels to set conditions in the theater. These activities primarily consist of cyberspace
surveillance and reconnaissance and cyberspace exploitation.
5-27. As a crisis transitions to armed conflict, national cyberspace forces will expend most of their available
offensive cyberspace capabilities to overcome enemy antiaccess, area denial, and integrated air defense
capabilities, enabling U.S. forces to gain physical access to the joint operational area through joint forcible
entry operations. After the earliest phases of operations in armed conflict, the available capabilities at
echelons corps and below are heavily weighted toward organic and supporting EW assets. Figure 5-1 on page
77 illustrates the relative weighting of cyberspace and EW capabilities across the competition continuum.
**76**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**
**Figure 5-1. Cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare across the competition continuum**

## COMPETITION

5-28. Competition activities include obtaining access for U.S. forces, maintaining sufficient forward-based
presence within a theater to influence conditions in the strategic environment, and mitigating conditions that
could lead to a crisis or armed conflict. At any time during competition, but especially during times of
heightened tension, leaders must take great care to ensure Army forces avoid activities that may provoke
crisis or armed conflict. U.S. forces attempt to create favorable conditions in theater to support military
operations without crossing the threshold that might provoke an armed response. Army forces play an integral
role in this effort, actively engaging across domains (including space and cyberspace) and in the
electromagnetic spectrum. The goal is to enable the defeat of adversary information warfare and hybrid threat,
conduct intelligence operations, counter threat reconnaissance, and demonstrate credible deterrence.

### SETTING THETHEATER

5-29. The ability to effectively compete below the level of armed conflict and to respond to escalation toward
armed conflict is necessary to establish a position of strength and set favorable conditions if conflict ensues.
This position of strength provides a favorable environment for the Army and mission partners’ efforts to
counter adversary coercion. The combined and persistent effects of defeating adversary information warfare
capabilities and hybrid threat during competition generate additional friendly options and demonstrate
credible deterrence. The combatant command, theater army, multi-domain task force, and units at echelons
corps and below have important roles in setting the theater for future operations.
**Combatant Command**
5-30. An essential step in setting conditions for armed conflict in a theater involves organizing assigned and
allocated forces for employment. Clear command relationships between current and projected units are
essential. Combatant commanders expect the command relationship between the supporting and supported
commands may change based on evolving situations.
5-31. During competition, the combatant command, affected country teams, Service components, and special
operations forces execute the theater campaign plan to create conditions in the theater favorable to U.S.
interests and prepare for anticipated military operations. The theater campaign plan—
•
Integrates target development during competition and crisis.
•
Ensures the combatant commander’s strategic communications initiatives are fully integrated and
convey clear messages to multinational partners, friendly populations, and adversaries.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**77**
**Chapter 5**
•
Connects posture changes to the DOD’s global cyberspace and EW campaign plans and provides a
vehicle for continuously reassessing posture needs, refining those needs, and updating the DOD and
regional posture plans accordingly.
•
Enables the DOD to synchronize theater strategic cyberspace and EW plans with current priorities
to allocate resources addressed through contingency plans.
5-32. Combatant command activities to set the theater may include—
•
Electromagnetic support to develop situational understanding of the electromagnetic spectrum in
theater—
▪
Understand friendly electromagnetic signatures and take steps to reduce or mitigate the
signatures.
▪
Understand neutral use of the electromagnetic spectrum in theater.
▪
Understand adversary use of the electromagnetic spectrum and characterize adversary emitters.
▪
Develop targets in case the situation transitions to armed conflict.

battle data sources.

▪

**Theater Army**
5-33. The theater army contributes to an integrated cyberspace and EW plan to support joint operations. The
theater army supports the combatant commander’s campaign plan by planning Army use of cyberspace and
EW capabilities. During competition, the theater army supports the combatant command in conducting
missions, tasks, and actions that shape the environment to assure friends, deter adversaries, and establish
conditions for future contingencies. The theater army develops contingency plans to support the combatant
command plan. Well in advance of crisis or armed conflict, the theater army ensures the Army’s targeting
requirements are represented during the development of the joint target list, restricted target list, and no-strike
list.
5-34. During competition, the theater army staff generates thorough understanding of friendly, adversary,
and neutral use of cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum in theater to identify—
•
Friendly vulnerabilities.
•
Possible targets in cyberspace.
•
Adversaries’ critical spectrum-dependent capabilities.
5-35. Early targeting during competition is critical to get cyberspace targets supporting joint forcible entry
operations into the joint targeting cycle, approved, and forwarded to USCYBERCOM to develop offensive
cyberspace capabilities to engage the targets in conflict.
**78**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**
**Multi-Domain Task Force**
5-36. During competition, the multi-domain task force may be forward stationed in a combatant
commander’s area of responsibility to employ joint and Army capabilities in support of target development.
The multi-domain task force headquarters coordinates and synchronizes space, cyberspace, EW, and
intelligence operations of its assigned formations with other theater (including theater army and combatant
command) and national assets. The multi-domain task force is designed to operate in a distributed fashion
while retaining the ability to deliver effects that create opportunities for joint force exploitation. Based on the
mission variables of METT-TC (I), the multi-domain task force may be augmented with capabilities from
across the warfighting functions.
**Corps and Division**
5-37. During competition, corps commanders base their planning and decisions on a continually evolving
understanding of their operational environment. Integrated EW, SIGINT, and cyberspace capabilities collect
against and analyze adversary systems and other factors of the operational environment and civil networks.
This effort builds situational understanding and helps bridge between operational-level plans and tactical
planning and execution. The division EW company conducts surveys of the electromagnetic spectrum in
conjunction with SIGINT and space support elements to support situational understanding in the
electromagnetic spectrum and space.
5-38. The division EW companies assist in countering adversary electromagnetic deception while supporting
counterreconnaissance by detecting, identifying and geolocating emitters used by adversary reconnaissance
elements. Collectively, these actions enable the joint force to rapidly transition to armed conflict and create
uncertainty for the adversary as to whether it can achieve its objectives through a surprise attack.
5-39. The division EW companies integrate EW activities with SIGINT and cyberspace operations to
complicate the adversary’s efforts to determine the capability and capacity of friendly forces in theater. While
exercises, training, and alerts demonstrate capabilities, they also provide opportunities to mislead adversaries
regarding friendly use of the electromagnetic spectrum, cyberspace signatures and patterns, and methods of
employment. These actions create unpredictability and complicate the adversary’s reconnaissance efforts,
which increases the likelihood of compromising adversary assets.
5-40. The corps and division CEMA chiefs assist the brigade combat team CEWOs with integrating and
synchronizing cyberspace targeting into the targeting process and provide insight into cyberspace
capabilities. The CEMA chiefs collaborate with CEWOs in vetting and processing target nominations from
subordinate units for effects in cyberspace. The CEMA chiefs—
•
Assist CEWOs in the integration, coordination, and synchronization of cyberspace and EW
targeting.
•
Provide CEWOs information about the effects of cyberspace operations, including associated rules
of engagement, impacts, and constraints used to advise the commander.
•
Assist CEWOs with developing and maintaining a consolidated targeting synchronization matrix.
•
Assist in nominating cyberspace targets through the combatant command.
•
Assist CEWOs in monitoring and assessing measures of performance and effectiveness while
maintaining updates on cyberspace operations’ effects on the operational environment.
•
Assist CEWOs in requesting and coordinating offensive cyberspace operations support as needed.
•
Integrate supporting cyber mission forces into operations.
•
Coordinate with multinational partners for cyberspace capabilities that complement or enhance joint
and Army cyberspace capabilities.
•
Coordinate and deconflict cyberspace and EW operations with the G-2 or S-2 and the G-6 or S-6.
•
Assist in performing target systems analysis and target nomination for development of
electromagnetic attack techniques to meet higher headquarters objectives.
**Note****.** Division EW companies and their equipment sets are in the process of fielding across the
force. Not all divisions will have these formations in place until fielding is complete.
