**Brigade Combat Team**
5-41. The brigade CEMA section—
•
Plans operations for organic EW forces.
•
Prepares requests for external cyberspace and EW support.
•
Nominates targets in cyberspace.
•
Integrates effects.
•
Assesses cyberspace and EW effects in the brigade’s assigned area.
5-42. Forward-deployed brigade combat teams assess and improve protection actions (including defensive
cyberspace operations and electromagnetic protection) against adversary capabilities and promote
interoperability with multinational partners’ tactical units. While most rotational brigades’ training occurs in
the continental United States and does not involve partner nation forces, brigade combat teams should
anticipate the need for multinational integration and plan and train using the multinational secret-releasable
mission partner network to integrate with mission partners.
5-43. During competition, forward-stationed brigade combat team EW platoons conduct electromagnetic
support to identify and locate threat emitters and spectrum-dependent devices within the brigade’s assigned
area. Information gathered through electromagnetic support provides the commander with critical decision-
making and targeting support. This data and information also support electromagnetic battle management by
providing continuous situational understanding to the CEMA spectrum manager to develop and update the
common operational picture.

### COMPETITIONACTIVITIES

5-44. During periods of competition, Army units conduct operations to shape the operational environment in
theater to support the combatant commander’s campaign plan. Competition activities include—
•
Force protection.
•
Security cooperation.
•
Security assistance.
▪
Security force assistance.
▪
Foreign internal defense.
▪
Security sector reform.
•
Nuclear deterrence and countering weapons of mass destruction.
•
Humanitarian assistance.
**Force Protection**
5-45. Cyber and EW forces at echelon, supported by SIGINT elements, support force protection through—
•
Electromagnetic support to—
▪
Understand friendly electromagnetic signatures and take steps to mitigate.
▪
Detect and geolocate adversary emissions.
•
Support to planning and validation of EMCON procedures.
•
Multi-spectral camouflage—camouflage that attempts to hide objects in the visible, infrared, and
radar portions of the electromagnetic spectrum.
•
Defensive cyberspace operations.
•
Support to influence operations dissemination.
**Security Cooperation**
5-46. Cyber and EW forces participate in security cooperation activities to deter aggression against partner
nations, reassure allies and partners of U.S. resolve, and maintain the period of competition. The combatant
command coordinates security cooperation activities with the Department of State country team.
5-47. Security cooperation includes—
•
Cyber and EW exchanges with allies.
•
Assistance in securing and defending allies’ military or civilian cyberspace.
•
Cyberspace- and EW-specific multinational exercise objectives.
**80**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**
•
Collaboration to enable friendly nations to defend their own cyberspace and electromagnetic
spectrum.
**Security Assistance**
5-48. Security assistance is a group of programs by which the U.S. Government provides defense articles,
military education and training, and other defense-related services to eligible foreign governments by grant,
loan, credit, cash sales, or lease. The Department of State supervises and directs the U.S. Government’s
security assistance programs, in consultation and coordination with the DOD and other government entities.
Security assistance programs include—
•
Security force assistance.
•
Foreign internal defense.
•
Security sector reform.
**Security Force Assistance**
5-49. DOD security force assistance activities help strengthen multinational partners’ security forces. The
DOD works with other U.S. government agencies to train and equip foreign security forces and help partners
develop better organizations and systems. Security force assistance programs support either individual
partner nations or regional security organizations (such as North Atlantic Treaty Organization) in addressing
security issues. The goal is to help these countries become more secure and capable, which ultimately helps
the United States achieve its own goals.
5-50. Army forces conduct security force assistance as part of the combatant commander’s campaign plan.
Units conducting security force assistance may conduct electromagnetic spectrum surveys to gain situational
understanding of the electromagnetic spectrum in the host nation or conduct combined EW training with
host-nation forces. Cyberspace forces’ participation in security force assistance might include steps to secure
and defend friendly, host-nation, or allies’ military or civilian cyberspace.
**Foreign Internal Defense**
5-51. Foreign internal defense activities attempt to establish, consolidate, and transfer control of security,
political and economic stability where threats exist. The goal of foreign internal defense is to ensure effective
and legitimate global, regional, and local information is distributed and protected. Cyber and EW forces’
foreign internal defense activities may include cyberspace defense and electromagnetic protection
approaches to protect allies’ communications infrastructure, gain support of a population, disrupt adversary
control over the population, and disrupt the adversary’s relations with the international community. U.S.
forces can contribute to defense of multinational partners’ military and civilian critical infrastructure through
cyberspace defense and electromagnetic protection. Refer to JP 3-22 and ATP 3-05.2 for more information
about foreign internal defense missions.
**Security Sector Reform**
5-52. The objective of security sector reform is to help host-nation governments provide effective, legitimate,
and accountable security for their citizens, consistent with the rule of law. Conducting electromagnetic
spectrum surveys, verifying friendly emitters, and conducting host-nation frequency coordination help host-
nation governments respond appropriately to adversary actions within and outside their borders.
**Nuclear Deterrence and Countering Weapons of Mass Destruction**
5-53. Due to their operational and strategic importance, weapons of mass destruction targets are different
from other tactical targets. These targets present increased risk to friendly forces and civilians, more
destructive effects on strategic or key operational terrain, and unique mitigation requirements. The
identification and prioritization of targets drive the development and allocation of reconnaissance, targeting
guidance, and fires assets.
5-54. EW and SIGINT may provide critical reconnaissance capabilities to support targeting for countering
weapons of mass destruction. The possibility of catastrophic second- or third-order effects when engaging a
weapons of mass destruction site might make cyberspace attack or electromagnetic attack the most
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**81**
**Chapter 5**
appropriate capability to engage it. Offensive cyberspace targets will likely need to be engaged by national-
level assets, while electromagnetic attack would probably be delivered by forces closer to the target site.
**Humanitarian Assistance**
5-55. Planning with nongovernmental organizations may be necessary for foreign humanitarian assistance,
peace operations, and civil-military operations. Incorporating these organizations into an operation requires
the commander to balance the need of the nongovernmental organization for information with operations
security concerns. Planning for the inclusion of nongovernmental organizations into civil-military operations
will likely involve cybersecurity coordination with nongovernmental partners. Army network defenders may
need to assist nongovernmental partners securing shared network resources.

### CONSOLIDATION OFGAINS

5-56. Consolidation of gains is an integral and continuous part of competition, and it is necessary for
achieving success across the competition continuum. Successful consolidation of gains in competition
requires a realistic and pragmatic assessment of—
•
Strategic conditions.
•
Allies and partners’ legitimacy.
•
Friendly and adversary relative advantages.
•
The viability of a sustainable political outcome.
5-57. Local network defenders consolidate gains by maintaining defense of their respective portions of the
network. If required and authorized, cyber defenders may assist partner nations and host-nation civil
authorities securing and defending networks and critical infrastructure against malicious cyberspace activity.

### TRANSITION TOCRISIS

5-58. Army forces and leaders anticipate the potential for armed conflict in their assigned areas. This is
informed by input from the intelligence community and direction from strategic-level leaders. Security force
assistance brigades and other regionally-aligned forces maintain access to sensitive areas and allied and
partner nation leaders. Their access and robust communications enable them to gain insight into actual
conditions on the ground and provide near real-time updates to decision makers in fluid situations. Decisions
made before and during the initial stages of a crisis have significant impact on adversary decisions and the
ultimate outcome of a particular situation.

## CRISIS

5-59. A crisis is an emerging incident or situation involving a possible threat to the United States, its citizens,
military forces, or vital interests that develops rapidly and creates a condition of such diplomatic, economic,
or military importance that commitment of military forces and resources is contemplated to achieve national
and/or strategic objectives (JP 3-0). The joint force competes to seek advantages relative to adversaries during
crisis. Army forces play an integral role in this effort, actively engaging across domains (including space and
cyberspace) and in the electromagnetic spectrum. The goal is to—
•
Consolidate gains in cyberspace into relative advantages.
•
Enable the defeat of adversary information warfare and hybrid threat.
•
Enable information collection.
•
Counter adversary reconnaissance activities.
•
Demonstrate credible deterrence.
5-60. Adversary forces conduct a detailed analysis of their available capabilities, capacity, and operational
reach within a theater relative to friendly forces; their overall desired end state; and their willingness to
achieve that end state before deciding to escalate. A regional peer may have an inherent numerical and
proximity advantage over U.S. forces operating in an overseas theater. The adversary will attempt to prevent
U.S. forces from overcoming their relative advantages.
**82**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**

### BEGINNING OFCRISIS

5-61. Once a crisis starts, adversaries use all capabilities at their disposal to disrupt the deployment of Army
forces. They will attempt to prevent the Army and the joint force from obtaining the time needed to deploy
and build combat power. This chaos, with its resultant frictions, is the environment into which Army forces
will respond. Army cyber and EW forces at echelon support early identification of transition to crisis through
the reporting of relevant indicators and warnings of adversary activity in cyberspace and the electromagnetic
spectrum.

### INITIALEMPLOYMENT OFFORWARD-STATIONEDFORCES

5-62. Army ground-based EW capabilities must be placed forward on terrain advantageous for the detection
of electromagnetic emissions or the conduct of electromagnetic attack. Airborne EW capabilities require
sufficient altitude and proximity to produce accurate lines of bearing which can produce or refine targeting
information. Due to the possibility of extreme distances, electromagnetic support information may require
confirmation by other intelligence, surveillance, and reconnaissance capabilities to produce actionable
targets.

### COUNTERINGADVERSARYACTIVITIES INCYBERSPACE AND THEELECTROMAGNETIC

### SPECTRUM

5-63. Two key information activities are protecting friendly information and attacking the adversary’s ability
to communicate, sense, make effective decisions, and maintain influence with relevant actors and
populations. Cyberspace and EW capabilities are key to both functions. The goal is to make better decisions
faster than the adversary.
**Cyberspace**
5-64. Adversaries are likely to calculate that they can conduct malicious cyberspace activities against U.S.
networks to deny friendly forces this critical enabler without crossing the threshold which would elicit an
armed response. In crisis, the adversary will attempt to penetrate U.S. networks, either to collect intelligence
about U.S. capabilities, disposition, and intentions, or to plant malicious code to disrupt the friendly network.
Local network defenders conduct defensive cyberspace operations-internal defensive measures to defeat
adversary attempts to collect information from or otherwise disrupt the Army network.
5-65. In crisis, authorities to attack adversary systems will be limited and likely retained at the national level.
CEMA sections identify, nominate, and continuously refine possible targets in cyberspace for engagement
by higher echelons. If available, cyberspace effects delivered by higher echelons can enable operations at
echelons corps and below. Units develop and refine their high-priority target lists in case the crisis escalates
to the point U.S. forces need to engage approved targets with available military capabilities.
**Electromagnetic Spectrum**
5-66. Electromagnetic protection helps protect friendly spectrum-dependent devices from adversary direction
finding, geolocation, and intelligence collection. Denying the adversary’s ability to collect information
through the study of U.S. emissions helps complicate the adversary’s decision cycle. CEMA sections
nominate targets in the electromagnetic spectrum. EW forces prepare to conduct electromagnetic attacks to
disrupt the adversary’s communications, fire control systems, information warfare capabilities, and other
spectrum-dependent capabilities. EW and SIGINT elements conduct electromagnetic support sensing and
direction finding to develop situational understanding of adversary emitters in their assigned area. The
direction-finding data contributes to the threat template and common operational picture.

### SUPPORT TOOPERATIONSSECURITY

5-67. Operations security to deny the adversary information about friendly forces is vital to the success of
operations during crisis. Effective operations security makes it harder for adversaries to identify units and
their locations and plan operations against them if the crisis escalates to armed conflict.
5-68. Units and individuals contribute to operations security through disciplined use of cyberspace.
Protective measures include—
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**83**
**Chapter 5**
•
Cybersecurity program compliance.
•
Individuals following cybersecurity best practices.
•
Secure access controls.
•
Defensive cyberspace operations.
•
Adhering to social media policies for both official and personal social media accounts.
•
Limiting or eliminating use of personal electronic devices.
5-69. Units employ electromagnetic protection—including EMCON—to support operations security.
Electromagnetic protection measures to support operations security include—
•
Minimizing electromagnetic emissions.
•
Limiting the duration of radio transmissions to the maximum possible extent.
•
Limiting use of personal cell phones, particularly those with U.S. provider subscriber identification
modules.
**Note****.** Refer to ATP 6-02.53 for techniques to minimize emissions and limit the duration of radio
transmissions.

### CREATING ANDEXPLOITINGRELATIVEADVANTAGES

5-70. Commanders seek to achieve relative advantages over adversaries in the physical, information, and
human dimensions of their operational environment. Army forces build upon the physical, information, and
human advantages gained during competition to mitigate friction, deter adversaries, and when necessary,
transition into armed conflict.
**Physical Advantages**
5-71. If there is key or decisive terrain, Army forces and host-nation partners may seek to deter adversaries
by setting a defense of that ground early in a crisis. Army forces combine capabilities in multiple domains to
control key terrain and deny it to adversaries. Electromagnetic support and electromagnetic attack capabilities
may contribute to the converged approach to controlling key terrain.
**Information Advantages**
5-72. During crisis, commanders lead their staffs to refine information activities based upon plans and
processes developed during competition. One of the challenges as a multinational force responds to a crisis
is building, securing, and defending the secret-releasable mission partner network to enable secure
information sharing during operations. Cybersecurity personnel and local network defenders secure the U.S.
portion of the mission partner network and may need to assist mission partners’ network security and defense.
Army forces implement EMCON and electromagnetic masking to deny adversaries the ability to collect
information about friendly forces’ position, strength, and disposition through the electromagnetic spectrum.
**Human Advantages**
5-73. While forward-stationed units commonly train with host-nation and other allied forces, those deploying
into an operational area from home stations will likely have limited experience collaborating with host-nation
and other multinational partner forces. This requires leaders to focus their staffs on the most critical
interoperability tasks necessary for effective multinational operations. A critical element in interoperability
is information exchange. Units consider the releasability of classified information to multinational partners
and establish the classified mission partner network to enable interoperability. CEMA sections and EW forces
share situational awareness of the electromagnetic spectrum with mission partners to enable interoperability.

### SUPPORT TO THEJOINTFORCE

5-74. Army forces plan, prepare, execute, and assess cyberspace and EW operations in collaboration with
joint forces and multinational partners as required. Army commanders and staffs will likely coordinate or
interact with joint forces to enable cyberspace and EW operations. For this reason, commanders and staff
**84**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**
should be aware of joint planning systems and processes that enable cyberspace and EW operations. Some
of these systems and processes include—
•
The joint planning process (refer to JP 5-0).
•
Adaptive planning and execution (refer to JP 5-0).
•
Joint electromagnetic spectrum operations (refer to JP 3-85).
•
Review and approval process for cyberspace operations (refer to JP 3-12).

### FORCEPROJECTION ANDCONTESTEDDEPLOYMENT

5-75. Force projection is the ability to project the military instrument of national power from the United
States or another theater in response to requirements for military operations (JP 3-0). The demonstrated
ability to project Army forces into an operational area is an essential element of conventional deterrence.
Army forces depend almost entirely upon joint lift capabilities for deployment.
**Opening the Theater**
5-76. In the transition to crisis, USCYBERCOM sends forces forward to theater to provide subject matter
expertise in cyberspace operations. USCYBERCOM collaborates with other specialized units to assist with
planning other information activities into their operations.
**Mobilization**
5-77. During mobilization, adversaries will conduct malicious cyberspace activities to collect intelligence or
disrupt network capabilities. They may also attempt to sabotage critical infrastructure—for example, aerial
ports and seaports of embarkation, fuel distribution, or utilities—through malicious cyberspace activities.
Local network defenders conduct defensive cyberspace operations-internal defensive measures to protect the
DODIN from adversary malicious cyberspace activities. See paragraph 5-84 for responsibilities for
protecting federal civilian, state, and private sector networks.
**Deployment from Home Station**
5-78. Peer threats will attempt to interfere with the Army formations deploying from home station, in transit
to the theater of operations, and at aerial ports and seaports of debarkation. Theater army, corps, and division
staffs coordinate closely to identify information and assets that need protection, and they request and apply
appropriate protection and security measures consistent with their collective threat analysis. Cybersecurity,
defensive cyberspace operations-internal defensive measures, and electromagnetic protection actions support
protection by securing and defending the DODIN-A.
5-79. Adversary information warfare can be conducted at a very low cost compared to conventional warfare
methods, and these operations may have global reach. Adversaries will conduct strategic information warfare
to slow or otherwise degrade force projection. Adversary information warfare activities to disrupt deployment
from home station may include—
•
Targeted content sent through social media, e-mail, or other means designed to frighten and distract
deploying Soldiers and their families.
•
Malicious cyberspace activities against—
▪
Soldiers and family members’ banks and credit agencies, cutting off or disrupting access to
personal funds.
▪
Civilian infrastructure—including transportation, supply, fuel, and navigation—used to support
military operations.
▪
The DODIN to disrupt communications between units, installations, and civilian partners that
assist in deployment.
•
Disinformation designed to—
▪
Undermine the legitimacy of, or otherwise reduce support for, U.S. Government action.
▪
Incite civil unrest in local communities and along rail and road lines of communications that
deploying forces need or plan to use to reach ports of embarkation.
▪
Reduce trust in future official communications from government, law enforcement, or military
officials by releasing disinformation that appears genuine but contains incorrect or confusing
information.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**85**
**Chapter 5**
5-80. As U.S. forces transition from crisis to armed conflict, adversaries will likely increase the intensity and
lethality of their attacks within the homeland. This could include sabotaging critical military and civilian
infrastructure by pre-positioned agents or malicious cyberspace activity.
5-81. Defensive cyberspace operations and electromagnetic protection are key capabilities in the integrated
approach to countering threat information warfare. Refer to ADP 3-13 for more information about protecting
friendly information, data, and networks.
**Fort to Port**
5-82. Army forces at all echelons must comprehensively assess threat capabilities that may impede
deployment in a contested environment. Units should account for being under constant observation in all
domains and take steps to mitigate adversaries’ ability to collect critical information through the protect
information activity, including safeguarding information about deployment timelines and locations,
maintaining dispersion of critical assets, and securing friendly networks and data.
5-83. Successful fort-to-port deployment involves coordination and collaboration with numerous civilian
partners. Besides attacks on the DOD network, adversaries may conduct malicious cyberspace activity on
critical civilian infrastructure to disrupt movement of forces from home station to an aerial or seaport of
embarkation. Units must balance the value of the use of computers and data to plan and support operations
with the risk of adversary malicious cyberspace activity. The effects of adversary malicious cyberspace
activity on critical military, national, or private infrastructure could halt or delay unit deployment operations
before units have departed the continental United States.
5-84. Planning to mitigate deployment disruptions requires collaboration between the deploying unit, the
installation, appropriate federal, state, and local government and law enforcement agencies, and U.S. Army
Reserve and Army National Guard elements. Installations build these relationships during competition.
Installations and mission partners develop understanding of how threats will likely affect their local areas by
modifying their threat working groups to incorporate relevant military, government, and other local and
regional stakeholders. The working group shares information about threat efforts to promote civil unrest,
cyberspace vulnerabilities that may adversely affect critical transportation infrastructure, and other adversary
activities that may impact deployment operations. Mitigation planning includes establishing cyberspace
defenses for systems and associated data used to support movement. Responsibility for defending some of
these systems may fall under the Department of Homeland Security; state, local, or tribal governments; or
private industry. Shared information about threat capabilities, tactics, techniques, and procedures contributes
to a holistic defense of critical networks and infrastructure. Prior understanding of the operational
environment, dependencies on cyberspace and spectrum-related capabilities, dependencies on data, and the
roles of mission partners is critical to mitigation planning.
**Reception, Staging, Onward Movement, and Integration**
5-85. During reception, staging, onward movement, and integration, U.S. forces and host-nation civilian
infrastructure are vulnerable to malware, denial-of-service attacks, and phishing as ways to conduct brute
force attacks. Local network defenders perform defensive cyberspace operations-internal defensive measures
within their capabilities. The G-3 or S-3 requests support through higher headquarters if they determine
adversary malicious cyberspace activity is beyond the scope of local network defenders.
**Initial Employment of Forces**
5-86. Army forces have limited ability to conduct large-scale defense on initial arrival in theater until
sufficient forces arrive. On early entry, signal elements of the deploying forces establish the initial network
connectivity for reachback to the sustaining base and to support the flow of follow-on forces into theater.
5-87. Adversaries will attempt to disrupt communications and network capabilities at aerial ports and
seaports of debarkation. Electromagnetic protection, cybersecurity, and defensive cyberspace operations-
internal defensive measures are critical to secure and defend the network as units arrive in theater.
**86**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**

### CYBERSPACE ANDELECTROMAGNETICWARFAREROLESDURINGCRISIS

5-88. As competition transitions to crisis, Army forces will deploy into theater to deter adversaries from
conflict. Adversaries will use cyberspace and EW capabilities to interfere with force projection and gain
intelligence on friendly positions, disposition, and capabilities.
**Joint Force Commander**
5-89. At the onset of a crisis that may escalate into armed conflict, the combatant commander generally
designates a joint task force to provide operational control of all Service components supporting the
operation. If the Army is designated the lead Service in the joint task force, the role of commander, joint task
force usually falls to the corps commander. If another Service is the designated joint task force lead, the corps
will provide augmentation to the joint task force headquarters. The lead Service in the joint task force will
stand up a joint electromagnetic spectrum operations cell and a joint cyber center, with augmentation from
the other Service components. Refer to JP 3-85 for more information about the role of the joint
electromagnetic spectrum operations cell and JP 3-12 for more information about the role of the joint cyber
center.
**Theater Army**
5-90. As forces deploy into theater, the theater army exercises administrative control of all Army forces
allocated to the combatant command to support the operation. The theater army CEMA section disseminates
situational awareness of cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum to corps, divisions, and brigade combat
teams in theater. The theater army CEMA section continues to develop cyberspace and EW targets for
engagement in case the crisis escalates to armed conflict.
**Corps**
5-91. As the primary integrating echelon for multidomain convergence, the corps’ CEMA section integrates
subordinate divisions and brigade combat teams’ EW activities and CEMA reporting. The corps CEMA
section reports to the joint task force joint cyber center (for cyberspace operations) and joint electromagnetic
spectrum operations cell for EW. The G-6 spectrum manager reports to the joint electromagnetic spectrum
operations cell for spectrum management operations. During crisis, the corps CEMA section participates in
the targeting process to nominate cyberspace and EW targets in the corps deep area and validate target
nominations from subordinate divisions and brigade combat teams.
**Note****.** Per AR 525-24, the Army uses its CEMA staff to integrate cyberspace and EW capabilities
into the joint and multinational operations process. The theater army or ARFOR CEMA section
coordinates cyberspace operations with the joint cyber center and EW with the joint task force
joint electromagnetic spectrum operations cell. The corps G-6 coordinates spectrum use with the
joint electromagnetic spectrum operations cell. The joint task force integrates EW and spectrum
management into joint electromagnetic spectrum operations.
**Division**
5-92. The division CEMA section nominates cyberspace and EW targets in the division deep and close areas
and validates targets nominated by subordinate brigade combat teams. The division EW company and
SIGINT elements conduct electromagnetic support in the division’s assigned area to develop thorough
situational understanding of friendly, adversary, and neutral use of the electromagnetic spectrum. This
baseline understanding helps the division EW elements better characterize adversary activities in the
electromagnetic spectrum in case the crisis transitions to armed conflict. The division EW company also
augments subordinate brigade combat teams’ EW platoons in the close area.
**Brigade Combat Teams**
5-93. During crisis, the brigade EW platoons conduct electromagnetic support to understand friendly,
adversary, and neutral use of the electromagnetic spectrum in the brigade’s assigned area. Understanding
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**87**
**Chapter 5**
friendly spectrum use informs electromagnetic protection efforts, including EMCON plans. The brigade
CEMA section participates in the targeting process to nominate cyberspace and EW targets in case the crisis
escalates to armed conflict.

### CONSOLIDATION OFGAINS

5-94. During and after crisis response, Army forces consolidate gains to deny adversary forces the means to
extend the crisis or create a similar crisis in the future. This will often entail maintaining an enhanced force
posture in a joint operations area to demonstrate the United States’ continued willingness to defend allies and
partners. Army cyber and EW forces may be called on to support improvements to host-nation cyberspace
defense and EW capabilities through a security cooperation plan to make them less vulnerable to future crises.
If an adversary directly targets partner nations’ military or civil networks, joint or Army cyber forces may
need to help the partner nation reconstitute, secure, and defend their networks.

### TRANSITION TOCOMPETITION ORARMEDCONFLICT

5-95. There are two outcomes of a crisis—a de-escalation to competition or an escalation to armed conflict.
Transitions are typically points of friction. Commanders emphasize information collection before and during
transitions to maintain a detailed understanding of the adversary and continuously assess the situation so they
can position their forces to retain the initiative.
5-96. Cyberspace surveillance and reconnaissance and electromagnetic support contribute to situational
understanding of cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum in the unit’s assigned area. EW and SIGINT
forces conduct direction finding and geolocation of adversary emitters to help assess adversary positions,
capabilities, and disposition.
**Transition Back to Competition**
5-97. During a crisis, partner nation security forces and government institutions may suffer losses that reduce
their capability and capacity due to the actions of adversary or proxy forces. Army forces may be tasked to
execute security cooperation programs to help restore or maintain partner nation capabilities and capacity to
consolidate gains. Army forces seek to restore partner nation security forces and government institutions as
quickly as possible to maintain popular support. Army cyber forces may be called on to help secure partner
nation military or civilian network infrastructure in the transition back to competition.
**Transition to Armed Conflict**
5-98. Army forces responding to a crisis are prepared to and expect to fight. This saves time during the
transition and requires an understanding of the operation plan or likely concept of operations as early as
possible. Units continue to defend the DODIN against adversary malicious cyberspace activity. EW forces
locate adversary emitters for situational awareness and targeting. CEMA sections at echelons corps and below
continue to nominate cyberspace and EW targets for lethal or nonlethal attack in case the force transitions to
armed conflict.

## ARMED CONFLICT

5-99. Armed conflict causes significant changes to regional security environments. The corps and division
EW companies provide a persistent presence to ensure the new security environment is advantageous for
U.S. forces and mission partners. The corps and division CEMA sections coordinate with the combatant
command and joint task force headquarters to best adapt force posture to the changing operational
environment. This ensures Army EW forces retain the ability to immediately counter aggression and enables
maneuver forces to rapidly commence offensive operations.
5-100. During armed conflict, forward-deployed and expeditionary Army forces enable the rapid defeat of
aggression through a combination of calibrated force posture, multidomain formations, and convergence to
immediately contest an enemy attack in-depth.
5-101. Corps and divisions are the formations central to the conduct of large-scale combat. These echelons
synchronize available capabilities across all domains to converge effects to destroy enemy systems and
formations and break the enemy’s capability and will to continue a conventional conflict. As the primary
**88**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**
integrating echelon for converged effects, the corps integrates joint, other Services, and multinational partner
support, including—
•
Joint fires.
•
Other Services or allies’ air support.
•
Airborne electromagnetic attack capabilities.
•
Theater- and national-level intelligence and cyberspace operations support.
•
Multinational partners’ cyberspace and EW capabilities.
•
Multinational partners’ maneuver and fires elements.
•
Special operations forces.
5-102. Refer to FM 3-0 for more information about large-scale combat operations and multidomain
integration. Refer to FM 3-16 for more information about the Army in multinational operations.

### RELATIVEADVANTAGES

5-103. In conflict, Army commanders seek to create and exploit relative advantages in all domains. Relative
advantages in this context include physical, human, and information advantages. Cyberspace and EW
operations provide key contributions to information advantage, both by protecting friendly information and
information systems and by attacking enemy command and control and information warfare capabilities.
5-104. Cyberspace and EW operations can also contribute to relative advantages in the other domains. For
instance, a cyberspace or electromagnetic attack that neutralizes or degrades an enemy air defense or fire
control system can create a window of advantage in the air or land domain a commander can exploit to enable
maneuver.
**Physical**
5-105. Throughout armed conflict, leaders seek physical advantages that include—
•
Position.
•
Range.
•
Speed of movement.
•
Technologically superior capabilities.
•
Terrain and weather.
5-106. The G-2 or S-2 staff identifies enemy key terrain as part of intelligence preparation of the operational
environment. Identified key terrain in cyberspace is subject to actions the controlling combatant—friendly
or enemy—deems advantageous such as defending, exploiting, or attacking. Key terrain in cyberspace
corresponds to nodes, links, processes, or assets in cyberspace, whether part of the physical, logical, or cyber-
persona layer. Key terrain in cyberspace may include—
•
Locations in cyberspace in which friendly forces can gather intelligence.
•
Locations in cyberspace that support network connectivity.
•
Entry points to friendly networks.
•
Locations in cyberspace that friendly forces rely on to provide essential functions or capabilities.
5-107. EW forces contribute to positional advantages by geolocating enemy forces to allow commanders to
maneuver to a position of advantage that negates some of the benefits of the enemy’s prepared defenses.
Long-range EW sensors—including those provided by the joint force or other Services—can contribute to a
range advantage. Counter radio-controlled improvised explosive device electromagnetic warfare systems can
help protect maneuver elements while maintaining their speed of movement. Cyber and EW forces, in
conjunction with intelligence forces, develop a thorough understanding of the effects of terrain on friendly
and enemy forces. Thorough situational understanding may allow commanders to exploit a terrain advantage.
**Information**
5-108. Creating and exploiting relative advantages to achieve objectives is central to multidomain operations.
Like physical and human advantages, information advantages are often temporary and change over time
relative to an operational environment and how the enemy responds to friendly activities. While friendly
forces seek information advantages, enemy forces are doing the same. As such, an information advantage is
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**89**
**Chapter 5**
something to gain, protect, and exploit across all domains. Examples of information advantages during armed
conflict to which Army cyberspace and EW capabilities contribute include—
•
The ability to access enemy command and control systems to disrupt, degrade, or exploit enemy
information through cyberspace or electromagnetic attack.
•
Opportunities created through tactical deception, including electromagnetic deception, to achieve
surprise and thwart enemy targeting.
•
The ability to mask electromagnetic signatures through EMCON.
•
The ability to rapidly share and analyze information among commanders and staffs to enable
decision making and orders dissemination.
**Human**
5-109. A human advantage occurs when a force holds the initiative in terms of training, morale, perception,
and will. Human advantages enable friendly morale and will, degrade enemy morale and will, and influence
popular support. Examples of human advantages include leader and Soldier competence, morale of troops,
and the health and physical fitness of the force. The exploitation of physical and information advantages may
reduce an enemy force’s capacity to fight, creating human advantages. Physical and information advantages
gained through cyberspace and EW operations can implicitly or explicitly communicate a message that can
influence enemy forces’ will to fight, sway popular support, and influence enemy risk calculus.
5-110. Examples of cyberspace and EW activities that help achieve human advantages include—
•
Routine interaction with allies and other mission partners to build and maintain human, technical,
and procedural interoperability through agreed-upon standards.
•
Exchange of liaison officers with multinational partners.
•
Sustained presence by theater-aligned advisor teams to build relationships and promote
interoperability over time.

### COUNTERINGENEMYACTIVITIES INCYBERSPACE

5-111. Enemies will undertake malicious cyberspace activities to disrupt U.S. and allies’ command and
control and to collect intelligence. Countering enemy malicious cyberspace activities requires coordinated
cyberspace defense. G-6 or S-6 DODIN operations personnel, local network defenders, and CEMA staff
across echelons will synchronize their efforts to implement the cybersecurity program, defensive cyberspace
operations-internal defensive measures, and cyber incident reporting to prevent, identify, quarantine, and
mitigate threat malicious cyberspace activity on the DODIN-A.
**Defensive Cyberspace Operations-Internal Defensive Measures**
5-112. Cyber forces act on cues from cybersecurity or intelligence alerts of enemy activity within friendly
networks. Cyberspace tasks during defensive cyberspace operations-internal defensive measures include
hunting for enemy activities on friendly networks and responding to mitigate their effects.
**Defensive Cyberspace Operations-Response Actions**
5-113. Defensive cyberspace operations-response actions are carried out outside the protected network
without permission of the owner of the affected system. Some defensive cyberspace operations-response
actions may include actions that rise to the level of use of force and may cause physical damage or destruction
of enemy systems. These actions consist of cyberspace attacks and cyberspace exploitation as with offensive
cyberspace operations. However, defensive cyberspace operations-response actions use those actions to
prevent or stop enemy action.
5-114. Decisions to conduct defensive cyberspace operations-response actions depend heavily on broader
strategic and operational contexts such as the existence or imminence of open hostilities, the degree of
certainty in attribution of the threat, the damage the enemy has or is expected to cause, and national policy
considerations. These missions are conducted by a cyber national mission team and require a properly
coordinated military order; coordination with interagency partners; and unified scope, rules of engagement,
and operational objectives.
**90**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**

### DEFEATINGENEMYANTIACCESS ANDAREADENIAL

5-115. Defeating layered antiaccess and area denial relies on targeting efforts undertaken as part of setting
the theater during competition and crisis. Developing targets in cyberspace well in advance of conflict allows
the necessary lead time to develop tailored capabilities to engage the targets at the beginning of conflict. The
theater army and multi-domain effects battalion’s targeting conducted during competition and crisis supports
delivery of converged effects to overcome enemy antiaccess and area denial and supports joint forcible entry
at the onset of armed conflict. The multi-domain task force can coordinate and integrate cyberspace, EW,
and space capabilities with long-range surface fires to deceive, neutralize, or disrupt enemy formations,
command and control nodes, layered air defenses, and cyberspace and EW activities. This allows Army and
joint forces to penetrate and disintegrate enemy antiaccess and area denial capabilities, enable command and
control, and exploit the resulting freedom of maneuver. Disintegrate means to disrupt the enemy’s command
and control, degrading the synchronization and cohesion of its operations (ADP 3-0).

### JOINTFORCIBLEENTRY

5-116. Neutralizing enemy long-range systems enables theater strategic- and operational-level operations by
reducing the vulnerability of friendly lines of communications. Simultaneously, forward-deployed forces
begin to defeat enemy stand-off from the inside by operating within the range of enemy long- and mid-range
systems in conjunction with the joint force and other Services. Cyberspace attacks conducted by national-
level assets can partially neutralize some enemy standoff capabilities, including long-range precision fires
and integrated air defenses, long enough for joint and Army forces to destroy the systems. Electromagnetic
attack against enemy air defense radars also contributes to suppression of enemy air defenses to defeat enemy
antiaccess and area denial. Electromagnetic jamming or spoofing of enemy positioning, navigation, and
timing satellite signals can render guidance systems of enemy long-range precision fires ineffective.
5-117. National- and theater strategic-level cyberspace attacks are critical to support joint forcible entry
operations. National-level assets will engage cyberspace targets developed during competition and crisis to
defeat enemy long-range precision fires and integrated air defenses to allow combat forces to enter and
establish a foothold in the theater and commence offensive operations. Joint forcible entry operations can
extend from the close area into the deep area, based on the enemy’s antiaccess and area denial capabilities.
5-118. Success in joint forcible entry entails neutralizing enemy air defenses in the close area and long-range
precision fires and strategic command and control in the deep area. Cyberspace and electromagnetic attack
capabilities from the national and theater strategic levels are key contributors to joint forcible entry
operations. Electromagnetic attack capabilities employed at echelons corps and below may support
suppression of enemy air defenses in the close area. The corps may need to integrate airborne deep sensing
capabilities provided by other Services. Refer to ATP 3-04.1 for more information about suppression of
enemy air defenses.

### CYBERSPACE ANDELECTROMAGNETICWARFAREOPERATIONS IN THEDEFENSE

5-119. In the defense, commanders typically retain the deep and rear areas, but they divide the close area into
two distinct portions: the security area and main battle area. Commanders use this approach to synchronize
operations, including those in air, space, and cyberspace to defeat an enemy force throughout its depth.
5-120. During large-scale combat operations, the enemy will employ extensive malicious cyberspace activity
and electromagnetic attacks to disrupt U.S. communications; automated information systems; fire control;
and positioning, navigation, and timing. Local network defenders continue to protect their respective portions
of the network against malicious cyberspace activity. Units implement electromagnetic protection plans—
including EMCON—to protect spectrum-dependent devices from detection, geolocation, jamming, and lethal
fires.
**Deep Operations in the Defense**
5-121. The corps staff conducts planning to counter enemy long-range fires and shape conditions in the deep
area for future division and brigade combat team operations. The corps’ planning horizon for deep operations
in large-scale combat operations is 48 hours–5 days. This gives the corps ample time to integrate required
capabilities from the joint force, other Services, or multinational partners to support shaping activities.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**91**
**Chapter 5**
**Extended Deep Operations**
5-122. The combatant command plans and conducts operations to shape conditions in the extended deep area,
including lines of communication from the enemy’s strategic base to the joint operational area. Shaping in
the extended deep area may use support from national-level assets (including offensive cyberspace operations
capabilities) to create desired effects. Planning for extended deep operations requires a longer planning
horizon than deep or close operations due to the need to coordinate for joint or national capabilities.
**Deep Operations**
5-123. The division conducts operations in its deep area to neutralize enemy long range fires, disrupt enemy
command and control and sustainment, and shape conditions for future operations as close operations move
forward in the assigned area. The division’s planning horizon for deep operations is at least 24–48 hours to
allow coordination and integration of supporting capabilities from the joint force and other Services. Other
Service EW capabilities in the air tasking order extend the planning horizon for deliberate targeting. Planners
must consider the longer lead time when planning shaping operations in the deep area. Immediate EW targets
in the deep area that arise outside the planning horizon of the joint air tasking cycle can be engaged by re-
tasking air assets.
**Close Operations**
5-124. Divisions employ maneuver brigades in close operations against enemy maneuver forces. Divisions
employ attack aviation and close air support to enable close operations and conduct rear operations to sustain
tempo and operational reach.
**Main Battle Area**
5-125. Brigade combat team EW platoons conduct electromagnetic support in the close area for combat
information to support targeting and electromagnetic attack to jam enemy communications and fire direction
control. The division EW company augments brigade EW capabilities in the close area. The brigade planning
horizon for close operations is much shorter term than the division and corps planning horizons; brigade
combat teams generally plan operations 12–24 hours out.
**Security Area**
5-126. A security area is that area occupied by a unit’s security elements and includes the areas of influence
of those security elements (ADP 3-90). The security area is inside, or close to, an operational area where
forces and sustainment from two or more Services are positioned to conduct or support operations. Enemies
are invested in diminishing friendly security areas. Electromagnetic protection measures minimize the effects
of natural phenomena and mitigate the enemy’s ability to conduct electromagnetic sensing and attack against
friendly forces in the security area.
**Rear Operations**
5-127. The objective of rear operations is to ensure freedom of maneuver and continuity of operations
through sustainment. Enemies often target the rear area because it is vulnerable and critical to sustained
operations. Electromagnetic protection helps defend against lethal attacks by denying enemy use of the
electromagnetic spectrum to target, guide, and trigger weapons. Electromagnetic support supports situational
understanding and enables force protection, self-protection, and operations security efforts by countering an
enemy’s surveillance and reconnaissance capabilities.

### CYBERSPACE ANDELECTROMAGNETICWARFAREOPERATIONS IN THEOFFENSE

5-128. Maneuver commanders may engage approved cyberspace targets with available military capabilities
to support movement and maneuver by opening avenues necessary to disperse and displace enemy forces.
Converging effects in cyberspace with effects in other domains establishes conditions that enable maneuver
forces to gain or exploit positions of relative advantage. Electromagnetic attack—including electromagnetic
jamming, electromagnetic intrusion, and electromagnetic probing—supports movement and maneuver by
**92**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**
projecting power within the time and tempo of the scheme of maneuver. Electromagnetic support provides
combat information to support situational understanding, targeting, and assessment.
5-129. Cyberspace and EW in the offense might involve the following actions, some of which span different
domains and functional areas (for example intelligence or operations security):
•
Countering enemy unmanned aircraft systems.
•
Protecting friendly unmanned aircraft systems.
•
Gaining access to enemy closed networks in or near a unit’s assigned area, including extracting and
injecting data.
•
Using EW systems as delivery platforms for precision cyberspace effects.
•
Exploiting new devices and applications developed and deployed by the enemy that are being tested
for battle.
•
Delivering messages to relevant audiences.
•
Preventing the spread of misinformation and disinformation.

### OPERATIONALFRAMEWORKCONSIDERATIONS IN THEOFFENSE

5-130. Within the context of the higher echelon commander’s operational framework and the general phasing
scheme, commanders must design attacks that defeat enemy forces across all echelons, while enabling
disciplined initiative of subordinate leaders. When designing attacks, commanders divide the task of
defeating an enemy force and maintain an integrated approach through deep, close, and rear operations.
Commanders account for air, space, cyberspace, and when relevant, maritime capabilities across the
operational framework. They also consider friendly, enemy, and neutral use of the electromagnetic spectrum
in their assigned areas.
**Deep Operations**
5-131. Echelons division and above conduct deep operations to establish conditions for subordinates’ success
in close operations. In general, the combatant command conducts extended deep operations to set conditions
for the corps and below; the corps and division conduct deep operations to set conditions for brigade combat
teams conducting close operations.
**Extended Deep Operations**
5-132. Signal recognition, detection, identification, and geolocation are critical requirements in the extended
deep area to enable commanders to discover, exploit, and attack. The combatant command plans and conducts
offensive operations in the extended deep area to set conditions for future events in time and space. Higher
echelon headquarters—the theater army, combatant command, and national assets—are responsible for the
extended deep area. Operational missions seek to disrupt the movement of the enemy’s operational reserves,
prevent enemy forces from employing long-range fires and integrated air defense systems, and interfere with
enemy space and cyberspace capabilities to improve the protection of tactical formations. Special operations
forces can conduct electromagnetic attacks to deny the enemy electromagnetic spectrum access, attack
targets, and ensure maneuverability. Army forces request joint effects in the extended deep area by submitting
CERFs and electromagnetic attack request formats through the chain of command to the combatant
command. See appendix C for more information about the CERF and electromagnetic attack request format.
**Deep Operations**
5-133. In tactical-level deep operations, the corps headquarters might direct fires into its deep area to defeat
enemy long-range fires, disrupt enemy sustainment and command and control, separate maneuver
formations, and enable effective offensive maneuver. Deep operations are inherently joint, since many of the
capabilities employed in support of Army formations are provided by a joint force headquarters or another
Service, including the long-range airborne EW and air-to-surface fires platforms operated by the Navy and
Air Force.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**93**
**Chapter 5**
**Close Operations**
5-134. EW teams at the brigade and division geolocate enemy emitters in the main battle area and security
area for targeting with lethal or nonlethal capabilities or maneuver. They can also jam enemy command and
control communications to open temporary windows of advantage for maneuver forces. Counter radio-
controlled improvised explosive device electromagnetic warfare systems help protect combat and
sustainment forces from improvised explosive devices when maneuvering in the main battle area. Because
they typically perform a scout role well forward of the main body, EW teams may be positioned to directly
observe enemy activities and report combat information to address the commander’s critical information
requirements in the main battle area.
**Rear Operations**
5-135. Enemy deep operations often target friendly rear operations because they are often both vulnerable
and essential to friendly mission success. Units conducting rear operations must protect themselves using
both passive and active measures. Cyberspace defense and electromagnetic protection help protect friendly
communications to support sustainment operations and command and control in the rear area.
5-136. Maneuver forces will need to defeat bypassed forces in the rear area to consolidate gains. EW teams
in the rear area, synchronized with SIGINT forces, can conduct direction finding to geolocate enemy forces
in the rear area for engagement by lethal or nonlethal attack or maneuver forces.

### TRANSITION TOSTABILITYOPERATIONS

5-137. As a transition to stability operations occurs, leaders focus on stability tasks and information activities
to inform and influence populations and conduct security force assistance. Effective collaboration with
diplomatic and humanitarian organizations enhances the ability to achieve stability mechanisms. Army forces
play a key role in enabling the joint force to establish and conduct military governance until a civilian
authority or government is given control of their assigned areas.
5-138. During transition from defensive to stability operations, Army cyber forces may assist host nations
and multinational partners in reconstituting and hardening critical networks and infrastructure. Commanders
anticipate and incorporate mission partner planning factors, such as their domestic laws, regulations, and
operational limitations on the use of various cyberspace defense and cyberspace security actions such as
password management, software patching, encryption and storage device management, mandatory
cyberspace training for all users, and restricting access to suspicious websites. Army EW forces may help the
host nation reestablish spectrum-dependent capabilities and generally provide electromagnetic protection
during transition.

### RETURN TOPOST-CONFLICTCOMPETITION

5-139. Through a successful transition from armed conflict to the return to competition, integrated EW,
SIGINT, and cyberspace operations planning and assessment translate operational success to the achievement
of strategic objectives. During consolidation of gains, friendly EW forces reconstitute and help build or
reinforce the spectrum capacity of host-nation partners to enable long-term deterrence and prevent return to
armed conflict. Cyber forces may help partner nations improve the security and defense of their military and
civilian network infrastructure until they are adequately prepared to conduct their own network defenses.
**Theater Army Role**
5-140. Theater armies play a significant role in the return to post-conflict competition. The theater army’s
continuous, long-term focus on theater strategy, relevant national interests, and established relationship with
host-nation civil and military authorities enables them to holistically plan and conduct the transition. The
theater army CEMA section anticipates requirements and requests additional cyber and EW forces and
capabilities needed to support the transition to post-conflict competition.
5-141. Theater requirements will change as combat operations reduce and the focus on stability tasks
increases. As the transition progresses, the theater army helps ensure the host nation is prepared to defend
itself and continue civil governance post-conflict. This may require assistance securing and defending civilian
network and communications infrastructure.
**94**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace and Electromagnetic Warfare Operations in Combined Arms**
**Corps Role**
5-142. As large-scale combat operations conclude in a corps’ assigned area, the corps continues to adjust its
operational framework to meet the increasing demand on stability tasks while maintaining a posture that
prevents enemies from reconstituting their forces and operations. Operations primarily focus on area security
in high threat areas, followed by the performance of stability tasks in lower threat areas. During transition,
the corps will likely conduct simultaneous offense, defense, and stability operations.
5-143. During the transition, enemy information warfare elements or their proxies may continue to conduct
malicious cyberspace activity against U.S., allied, and host-nation networks and civil infrastructure. Local
network defenders continue to defend the corps’ portion of the network and assist multinational partners in
securing and defending their networks, if required and authorized.
**Division Role**
5-144. Divisions perform the stability tasks necessary to create conditions within their assigned areas that
allow transition to a legitimate civilian authority or a transitional authority. Divisions also control brigades
as they conduct security force assistance to build the capability and capacity of foreign security forces.
5-145. Local network defenders continue to defend the division’s portion of the network against enemy
malicious cyberspace activity. The division CEMA section and EW forces work to understand enemy
activities in the electromagnetic spectrum, since increased activity in the electromagnetic spectrum may be
an early indication of the enemy’s intent to re-commence combat operations.
**Brigade Combat Team Role**
5-146. Task-organized brigade combat teams perform most area security tasks, and they enable the
accomplishment of most stability tasks in concert with multinational partners. Army forces’ actions upon
culmination of large-scale combat operations include—
•
Consolidation.
•
Establishing area security.
•
Conducting stability tasks.
5-147. Local network defenders continue to defend the brigade’s portion of the network. The brigade CEMA
section and EW platoon maintain situational awareness of enemy activities in the electromagnetic spectrum
to provide early warning of enemy attempts to re-commence combat operations.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
