## This page intentionally left blank.

### Appendix A

## Cyber Incident Response

Cyber incident response refers to the processes and technologies for detecting and responding
to malicious cyberspace activity or security breaches. The goal of incident response is to
minimize the cost and mission disruption resulting from threat malicious cyberspace activity.
This appendix explains how different types of malicious cyberspace activity should be
identified, analyzed, reported, contained, and resolved.

### SECTION I – DEPARTMENT OF DEFENSE CYBER INCIDENT HANDLING

### PROGRAM

A-1. Effective cybersecurity requires the ability to respond to malicious cyberspace activity. The DOD
maintains a comprehensive cyber incident handling program to rapidly identify and respond to cyber
incidents. The DOD cyber incident handling program is a component of the overall DOD approach to
cyberspace defense.

## OVERVIEW

A-2. In a dynamic threat environment, it is critical that those responsible for building, operating, defending,
maintaining, and ensuring the continuity of information networks maintain unified and resilient network
capabilities. These capabilities must be able to adapt over time to changes in the threat environment. Refer
to DODI 8530.03 for more information about DOD cyber incident response. The cyber incident handling
program aligns with the defensive cyberspace operations services of—
•
Protect.
•
Monitor.
•
Respond.
A-3. The DOD cyber incident handling program is comprised of activities across many federal agencies
which include—
•
Reporting and notification.
•
Incident analysis.
•
Containment.
•
Eradication and recovery through coordinated incident response actions.
A-4. Coordinated cyber incident response ensures the acquisition and preservation of data required for
tactical analysis, strategic analysis, or further investigation.

## ACTIVITIES

A-5. The cyber incident handling program establishes the requirements for detecting, analyzing, and
responding to cyberspace events and incidents. It establishes standardized processes that ensure timely and
effective response. It enables timely and accurate reporting on the causes, impacts, and current state of
incidents.
A-6. How organizations implement the cyber incident handling program may vary depending on resources,
technical capabilities, and local policies and procedures. The objectives of the cyber incident handling
program are to—
•
Minimize the scope and duration of impacts from incidents.
•
Understand malicious actions taken on the network and the associated risks.
•
Improve the overall defensive posture of the network based on lessons learned.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**97**
**Appendix A**

### CYBERINCIDENTREPORTING

A-7. Cyber incident reporting provides an accurate, meaningful, and comprehensive understanding of the
cyber incident from initial detection to analysis and remediation. This information feeds into the user-defined
operational picture, which provides tactical, operational, and strategic situational understanding of
cyberspace defense actions and their impact. See section IV for ARCYBER reporting models.
A-8. Army cyber incident reporting and handling is subject to the requirements of CJCSM 6510.01B, CJCSI
6510.01F, and DODI 8530.01. Prompt, accurate reporting provides awareness and insight into past and
ongoing cyber incidents.

### CYBERINCIDENTANALYSIS

A-9. Cyber incident analysis identifies critical elements of an incident to determine and characterize its
possible effects on DOD networks, information, operational missions, and other defense programs. This
activity follows the cycle of—
•
Preparation.
•
Detection and analysis.
•
Containment, eradication, and recovery.
•
Post-incident recovery.
A-10. Figure A-1 illustrates the cyber incident analysis process. Refer to DODI 8530.03 for more information
about cyber incident analysis.
**Figure A-1. Cyber incident analysis**

### SECTION II – RESPONSE

## GENERAL

A-11. The cyber incident response life cycle (figure A-2) provides an organized and coordinated series of
response actions to resolve or mitigate a reported incident. It also includes the steps taken to recover affected
systems and return them to a fully operational and secure state. Coordination, communication, and
documentation actions are also taken during incident response to ensure the right organizations are involved,
notified of the outcomes, and provided follow-up reports. Once the incident is resolved, a final report is
submitted, and the unit completes any remaining post-incident actions. The primary objectives for an incident
response are to—
•
Halt or minimize attack effects or damage while maintaining the operational mission.
•
Ensure effective and timely recovery of system in a way that prevents similar incidents.
•
Strengthen the defensive posture and operational readiness of network systems.
•
Protect data throughout the incident response according to its sensitivity.
•
Rapidly and completely characterize the attack.
**Figure A-2. Cyber incident response life cycle**
**98**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyber Incident Response**

## DETECTION OF EVENTS

A-12. Detection of cyber events is a continuous process of identifying any unusual network or information
system activity that can adversely affect DOD networks, systems, or operational missions. The primary
objectives of detecting cyber events include—
•
Detection and reporting of suspicious activity so that further analysis can take place to determine
whether it is a reportable cyber event or incident.
•
Reporting of suspicious activity within required reporting timelines.
•
Coordination with higher echelons and other DOD organizations.
A-13. As part of this process, local defenders gather and report information about possible malicious
cyberspace activities, identified vulnerabilities, or other security or incident information to the appropriate
authorities for analysis and response. This is important because it is the point where an anomalous or unusual
cyber event is first noticed and identified as something that must be reviewed. It may also be the first point
at which a cyber event is reported.
A-14. Detection starts the reporting process. Gathering report information in a database helps analysts
identify emerging trends and patterns. This knowledge can help service desks learn from ongoing activity
and incidents so they can properly secure and defend their portions of the network.
A-15. For proper detection to take place, guidelines must be established defining what to consider abnormal
or suspicious. This information should be passed to appropriate network and information system
administrators or incorporated into the configuration of automated detection systems. Without the detection
process, the Army Enterprise Service Desk would not be alerted that something must be checked or resolved.
If this does not happen in a timely, standard, consistent manner, a serious incident may not be properly
reported and significant damage and loss to the component infrastructure can occur. Detection of a cyber
event may occur in various ways, including—
•
An automated detection system or sensor.
•
A report from an individual or user.
•
An incident report or situational awareness update from other internal or external organizational
components, such as USCYBERCOM, or other external computer security incident response team
entities.
A-16. Cyber incident detection can be from any stakeholder, and initial event details can vary. Alerts from
automated detection systems might include more details than a report from a non-technical user. Additional
information may need to be collected as part of the incident analysis phase. Examples of cyber events and
the various ways they are detected include—
•
The network intrusion detection system sends alerts for suspicious network traffic.
•
The antivirus software alerts that a device is infected with a worm, virus, or other malicious code.
•
A web server crashes.
•
Users complain of slow access to hosts on the Internet or mail servers.
•
The system administrator sees a filename with unusual characters.
•
A user calls the service desk to report a suspicious e-mail message (possible phishing attempt).
•
An information system records a suspicious configuration change in its log.
•
An information system logs multiple failed login attempts from an unfamiliar remote system.
•
An e-mail administrator sees many e-mails with suspicious content.
•
A network administrator notices deviation from typical network traffic flows.
•
A firewall administrator sees unauthorized outbound connections not seen by other means.
A-17. An event is not determined to be an incident until after preliminary analysis to assess and validate the
event against the criteria for determining whether it is an incident. Network defenders should categorize
reportable cyber events and incidents and follow the established cyber incident handling process in
accordance with CJCSM 6510.01B.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**99**
**Appendix A**

## PRELIMINARY ANALYSIS AND IDENTIFICATION

A-18. Initial assessments should be conducted quickly, even with limited details and analysis. As the
investigation continues and analysts more accurately characterize the true impact, the report is reassessed and
modified.
A-19. To make an accurate impact assessment, the analyst performing the preliminary assessment must have
access to personnel with a good understanding of the function and criticality of the information system,
network, or data in question and its role in fulfilling the mission.
A-20. Local network defenders begin to document the incident if documentation has not already begun. If
analysis determines that computer forensics are required (for instance, a Criminal Investigation Division
investigation), defenders begin to document the chain of custody. Documentation should include—
•
All known information about the event or incident.
•
All actions taken during the preliminary analysis and the results of that analysis.
•
A chain of custody record initiation if forensic evidence is collected and further prosecutorial
investigation may be required.

### INITIALREPORTSUBMISSION

A-21. Local network defenders prepare and submit the initial report to the appropriate organizations and
commands and through the established reporting mechanisms. Whenever possible, cyber incident reporting
should take place outside the affected network. Local network defenders should not report through a
potentially compromised network because an attacker may be monitoring the compromised network and
could be warned of detection. There are two different types of reporting—technical and operational.
**Technical Reporting**
A-22. Technical reporting assists with the handling of incidents and enables mitigation of the operational and
technical impact of an incident. Technical reporting may include submitting an incident report to the regional
cyber center or Army Enterprise Service Desk.
**Operational Reporting**
A-23. Operational reporting provides notification to commanders at all levels about the status of their
networks and information systems and the operational impact of the incident on their missions. Operational
reporting is a vital conduit for commanders to identify operational impacts and direct the incident handling
process to mitigate negative impact on their missions. Responding to an incident may require immediate
action by all affected units.

## PRELIMINARY RESPONSE ACTIONS

A-24. Organizations use a combination of cyber incident actions. The types of cyber incident actions are—
•
Technical actions.
•
Command actions.
•
Legal and policy actions.

### TECHNICALACTIONS

A-25. Technical actions contain or eradicate risks or vulnerabilities associated with the incident and rebuild
or restore affected systems to their normal operational state. These actions focus on minimizing incident
effects, facilitating incident analysis, and restoring operational capabilities. The personnel required to execute
technical actions depend on the organization responsible for the affected system and mission components.

### COMMANDACTIONS

A-26. Command actions direct other incident response actions and direct changes to current operations. They
ensure appropriate resources are dedicated to cyber response and ensure changes to operations are
coordinated, nested, and effective. They require some type of command or leader intervention, notification,
**100**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyber Incident Response**
interaction, escalation, or approval as part of any response. Often, directed response actions are based on
technical analysis with short reaction, compliance, and reporting timelines.

### LEGAL ANDPOLICYACTIONS

A-27. Legal and policy actions are associated with—
•
Legal liability.
•
Privacy issues.
•
Creating or reviewing nondisclosure and service level agreements.
•
Other legal and policy actions taken in response to an incident.
A-28. Units prepare to facilitate legal and policy actions through the testing of networks, information systems,
and mission systems. Units archive boot disks and distribution media for all applications, security patches,
and operating systems. This is especially important for deployed units who may lose network capabilities
due to malicious cyberspace activity. Depending on the type of data compromised, commands may have
additional responsibilities (for example, conducting a personnel asset inventory).

## RESPONSE AND RECOVERY

A-29. USCYBERCOM has the authority to direct and assist commands with mitigation actions for incidents
that fall into a DOD enterprise incident set or when actions otherwise affect multiple theater or Service
information networks. For further information about developing incident response courses of action and
approaches, refer to NIST SP 800-61. Incident response courses of action include the actions necessary to—
•
Respond to the reportable cyber event or incident.
•
Restore the information or mission system.
•
Return the system to operation.
•
Assess residual risk for the system or network.

### DEVELOPMENT

A-30. Analysis, comparison, and selection of courses of action should take place at the lowest echelon
practicable. Proposed courses of action should include the development and issuance of notifications to
promote awareness, direct response actions, and ensure compliance. Those with key roles in responding to
an intrusion must be notified and kept informed to fulfill their responsibilities.

### IMPLEMENTATION

A-31. Implementation of the selected course of action and information dissemination procedures may include
contacting users, security personnel, vendors, Internet service providers, other commands, and other internal
or external security organizations. Coordination may be required with DOD agencies or with external law
enforcement organizations when DOD support is not available due to time or distance factors. International
coordination may be needed if attacking hosts reside in a foreign nation.

### CONTAINMENT

A-32. Containment consists of short-term actions to stop an attacker’s access to a compromised information
system, limit the extent of an intrusion, and prevent an intruder from causing further damage. While an
intruder has access to the system, the information system cannot be properly analyzed or restored.
Containment prevents an intruder from accessing or exfiltrating data or other information, destroying
evidence, or tampering with the system during analysis. Containment provides a reasonable security solution
until enough information has been collected to address the vulnerabilities exploited and the damage sustained.
Containment actions are performed by the unit responsible for operating and maintaining the affected
network or system.

### ERADICATION

A-33. During eradication, incident response focuses on eliminating the attacker’s presence and access. All
exploits and vulnerabilities should be removed from networks and systems before returning the system to
service. If the exploit is not removed, the system can be easily compromised or breached again. Eradication
measures remove the cause of the malicious activity and any associated files. Eradication ensures the
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**101**
**Appendix A**
elimination of any access methods used by the intruder, including vulnerabilities, weaknesses in physical
security, or human error.
A-34. Eradication is system-specific. In some cases, full eradication requires long-term policy or
configuration changes. In that case, the exploit should be mitigated to the extent possible before rebuilding
and reconnecting affected systems. Due to the nature of an incident, some systems may not need any
eradication steps, or the eradication may occur as part of recovery when the infected system is wiped and
reimaged.
A-35. The unit responsible for operating and maintaining the affected information network or system
performs eradication. Who performs the measures depends on the type of incident, affected component, and
local policy and procedures. Eradication techniques depend on the nature of the incident. Eradication
techniques include—
•
Removal of malware.
•
Remediation or mitigation of vulnerabilities.
•
Modification of access controls.
**Removal of Malware**
A-36. To remove malware, network or system administrators should quarantine, delete, replace, or restore
the integrity of infected files. This may require reimaging the system from trusted media. It may also involve
updating antivirus signatures.
**Remediation or Mitigation of Vulnerabilities**
A-37. Units can mitigate vulnerabilities through technical and non-technical means. Network or system
administrators can remove vulnerabilities by installing up-to-date patches to vulnerable operating systems or
applications to prevent exploitation. If the system cannot be patched for technical or operational reasons, the
vulnerability may be mitigated by updating the system configuration and defenses to protect or segment the
affected host. If a patch is not available, system or network administrators apply workarounds or temporary
mitigation measures. Units may also use policies, physical security measures, and other mechanisms to
augment technical controls.
**Modification of Access Controls**
A-38. Network administrators may need to update user and network access controls. Examples include—
•
Removing compromised user or administrator accounts.
•
Updating intrusion detection systems or intrusion protection systems.
•
Modifying firewall settings.
•
Filtering content.
•
Updating baseline configurations.
•
Removing other access mechanisms used by the threat.

### RECOVERY

A-39. Recovery restores the integrity of the affected system and implements protective measures to prevent
similar incidents from occurring in the future. The goal of recovery is to return to normal, steady-state
operations. During recovery, administrators restore the integrity of affected systems, return the affected data,
systems, and information networks to an operational state, and implement follow-up measures to prevent
reoccurrence of the incident. As part of recovery, network and system administrators confirm that the systems
operate normally and that the follow-up measures are effective.
A-40. Data and systems are fully restored when the necessary patches and fixes have been installed. The
integrity of all data and software on an affected information system is suspect. The intruder could have
changed the kernel, binaries, data files, running processes, or memory. The only way to be sure a system is
free of malicious code and back doors is to reinstall software from trusted media and install up to date security
patches and upgrades. This includes both operating system and application patches.
**102**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyber Incident Response**
A-41. As part of the recovery process, containment activities completed may need to be removed. This can
include removing blocks that are no longer necessary, re-enabling services, and reconnecting information
systems and networks.
A-42. Recovery measures are implemented by a variety of defensive cyberspace operations. cybersecurity
personnel, or network and system administrators based on their cyberspace workforce roles. Multiple
measures may need to be implemented across affected components. Who implements the measures depends
on the type of incident, affected component, and local policy. Depending on the nature of the incident,
recovery actions may include—
•
Reimaging the system from trusted media—reinstalling the operating system and application
software from a trusted backup or from the original distribution media.
•
Verifying system data—reviewing data on the affected system to ensure its integrity. Someone who
knows what user or other data was on the information system should review the data to ensure it has
not been changed. Alternatively, system administrators can restore data from a trusted backup.
•
Changing system passwords—changing all passwords (including administrator passwords) on the
information system and possibly on all information systems that have trust relationships with the
affected system.
•
Improving network and host security.

### RECOVERINGWITHOUTPERFORMINGTECHNICALANALYSIS

A-43. Data from all incidents must be preserved to enable technical analysis. However, under certain
circumstances, and with approval, technical analysis may not be required prior to information and mission
system recovery. For example, it may not be possible to conclusively identify the root cause of an incident
through additional analysis. The intruder may have deleted or tampered with logs and files, making them
untrustworthy. The existence of multiple unpatched vulnerabilities may make it impossible—or not worth
the effort—to try to identify which vulnerability was exploited. In such cases, it may be more expedient to
begin system recovery and hardening.
A-44. Administrators should carefully assess technical and operational implications before recovering an
information system without technical analysis. Technical analysis provides valuable information to prevent
the same or similar attacks in the future. Such an assessment will indicate how this may impact mission
success. For example, the primary benefit of determining root cause and eradicating it before redeploying an
information or mission system is to prevent similar system compromises and to share that information with
other network defenders, thereby strengthening the overall security of the network. If a root cause is not
identified and eradicated, the system may be compromised again, or the same exploit may be used on other
systems.

## POST INCIDENT ANALYSIS

A-45. An after action review of the incident, including detection, analysis, and action, will improve
operations, processes, and network defense. The primary objectives of an after action review include
identifying infrastructure problems to be addressed, system and configuration weaknesses, and organizational
policy and procedural problems to be reviewed and modified. After action reviews are an opportunity to
determine unclear or undefined roles, responsibilities, interfaces, and authorities while identifying technical
or operational needs.
A-46. Upon completion of the after action review, administrators provide feedback to suggest improvements
to policies, procedures, and infrastructure defenses to NETCOM through the regional cyber center. These
improvements may include updating security policies or implementation guides (for example, SOPs or
security technical implementation guides), and incident handling processes and procedures. During this time,
modifications are made to configurations and filters on network perimeter devices, such as firewalls, routers,
and gateways.
A-47. After action reviews produce information and incident data that can be collected and used in various
ways. This information can be used to establish baselines for benchmarking performance, timeliness, and
types of incidents seen. It can also be used to generate trending and correlation information for historical
purposes to determine whether response actions are resolving problems over time.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**103**
**Appendix A**

### SECTION III – EVENT AND INCIDENT SCHEMA AND CLASSIFICATION

## EVENTS AND INCIDENTS

A-48. An event is any observable occurrence in a network or system (NIST SP 800-61). Events include a
user connecting to a file share, a server receiving a request for a web page, a user sending email, and a firewall
blocking a connection attempt. Adverse events are events with negative consequences, such as—
•
System crashes.
•
Denial-of-service attacks.
•
Unauthorized use of system privileges.
•
Unauthorized access to sensitive data.
•
Execution of malware that destroys data.
A-49. An incident is an occurrence that actually or imminently jeopardizes, without lawful authority, the
integrity, confidentiality, or availability of information or an information system; or constitutes a violation
or imminent threat of violation of law, security policies, security procedures, or acceptable use policies (Title
44, United States Code). Examples of cyber incidents include—
•
An attacker commands a botnet to send high volumes of connection requests to a web server, causing
it to crash (distributed denial-of-service).
•
Users are tricked into opening a malicious file sent via e-mail (phishing). Running the malicious
software may infect their computers and establish connections with an external host.
•
An attacker obtains sensitive data and threatens that the details will be released publicly if the
organization does not pay a designated sum of money (cyber extortion).
•
A user provides or exposes sensitive information to others through peer-to-peer file sharing services.

## CYBER INCIDENT AND REPORTABLE CYBER EVENT

## CATEGORIES

A-50. A cyber incident or reportable cyber event category is a collection of events or incidents sharing a
common underlying cause of which an incident or event is reported. Each cyber incident or event is associated
with one or more categories as part of the incident handling process. Table A-1 on page 105 outlines the
categories of cyber incidents. Refer to CJCSM 6510.01B for more information about categorizing incidents
and reportable events.
**104**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyber Incident Response**
**Table A-1. Cyber incident categories**
**Category**
**Description**
0
**Training and exercises**. Operations performed for training purposes or support to a combatant
command, Service, agency, or field activity.
1
**Root level intrusion (incident)**. Unauthorized privileged access to an information system.
Privileged access, often referred to as administrator or root access, provides unrestricted access
to the system. This category includes unauthorized access to information or unauthorized access
to account credentials that could be used to perform administrative functions. If the information or
mission system is compromised with malicious code that provides remote interactive control, it
will be reported in this category.
2
**User level intrusion (incident)**. Unauthorized non-privileged access to a system. Non-privileged
access, often referred to as user-level access, provides restricted access to the system based on
the privileges granted to the user. This includes unauthorized access to information or
unauthorized access to perform user functions such as accessing web applications, web portals,
or other similar information resources. If the system is compromised with malicious code that
provides remote interactive control, it will be reported in this category.
3
**Unsuccessful activity attempt (event)**. Deliberate attempts to gain unauthorized access to an
information system that are defeated by normal defensive mechanisms. An attacker fails to gain
access to the system (attacker attempts valid or potentially valid username and password
combinations) and the activity cannot be characterized as exploratory scanning. Reporting of
these events is critical to gather useful effects-based metrics.
**Note****.** This does not apply to a virus that is defeated and deleted by antivirus software. Viruses
that are defeated and deleted by antivirus software are not reportable events or incidents and
should not be annotated.
4
**Denial-of-service (incident)**. Activity that denies, degrades, or disrupts normal functionality of an
information system or network.
5
**Non-compliance activity (event)**. Activity that may expose systems or security domains, install
vulnerable applications, or breach cybersecurity policy. Reporting of these events is critical for the
gathering of useful effects-based metrics.
6
**Reconnaissance (event)**. Activity that seeks to gather information used to characterize system
applications, networks, or users that may be useful in formulating an attack. This includes activity
such as mapping networks, system devices and applications, interconnectivity, and their users or
reporting structure. This activity does not directly result in a compromise.
7
**Malicious logic (incident)**. Installation of software designed or deployed by adversaries with
malicious intent for the purpose of gaining access to resources or information without the consent
or knowledge of the user. This only includes malicious code that does not provide remote
interactive control of the compromised system. Malicious code that allows interactive access
should be categorized as category 1 or category 2 incidents, not category 7. Interactive access
may include automated tools that establish an open channel of communications to a system.
8
**Investigating (event)**. Events that are potentially malicious or anomalous activity deemed
suspicious and warrant, or are undergoing, further review. No event will be closed out as
category 8. Category 8 events are re-categorized to category 1–7 or 9 prior to closure.
9
**Explained anomaly (event)**. Suspicious events that, after further investigation, are determined to
be non-malicious activity and do not fit the criteria for any other categories. The reason the
anomaly cannot be otherwise categorized must be clearly specified.

## INITIAL IMPACT ASSESSMENT

A-51. The initial impact assessment table (table A-2 on page 106) describes the severity of a cyber event or
incident and establishes a common framework for evaluating and assessing events or incidents. This ensures
all units share a common view of the severity of a given event or incident. It should only be used during
initial reporting. A more complete impact assessment is conducted later in the cyber incident handling process
as more details are available and analysis conducted.

**Type of Impact**

Operational
High: Loss of ability to
perform mission.
Moderate: Significant
degradation of mission.
Low: Fully mission
capable with reduced
efficiency.
Information
High: Compromise of
classified information.
Low: Compromise of
non-publicly releasable
information.

of protected information
(personally identifiable
information or protected
health information).
Supplemented:

additional resources.

time with existing
resources.

unpredictable and may
require external
assistance.
A-52. The initial assessment must be performed quickly, even with limited details and analysis. As the
investigation continues and the true impact is understood, the report is reassessed and modified.

## MAJOR CYBER INCIDENTS

A-53. A major cyber incident may be interchangeably termed a ‘significant cyber incident.’ A major incident
is either—
•
An incident that is likely to result in demonstrable harm to the national security interests, foreign
relations, or the economy of the United States or to the public confidence, civil liberties, or public
health and safety of the American people.
•
A breach that involves personally identifiable information that, if exfiltrated, modified, deleted, or
otherwise compromised, is likely to result in demonstrable harm to the national security interests,
foreign relations, or the economy of the United States, or to the public confidence, civil liberties, or
public health and safety of the American people.
A-54. Cybersecurity personnel and local network defenders assess each breach on a case-by-case basis to
determine whether it meets the definition of a major cyber incident. Refer to Office of Management and
Budget Memorandum M-23-03, Fiscal Year 2023, for more information about categorization of major cyber
incidents.

### SECTION IV – CYBER INCIDENT RESPONSE REPORTING

A-56. When a cyber incident is suspected, users must stop all activities on the affected system and notify
local network defenders (information systems security officers or information systems security managers)
immediately. Local defenders contact the supporting regional cyber center and NETCOM strategic signal
brigade. Supporting strategic signal brigades notify the signal command (theater). The initial notification
must be conducted through secure, preferably out-of-band, communication channels. For example, via
encrypted e-mail for initial communication on unclassified networks. Response reporting should take place
when the unit is not in direct or indirect fire contact with the enemy. Units should not delay offensive or
defensive actions in the physical domains for the sake of reporting compliance. In this case, the unit submits
cyber incident reports as soon as practicable after breaking enemy contact.
**Note****.** In theaters without a signal command (theater), the strategic signal brigade is the highest
level NETCOM unit.
A-57. Local network defenders in the G-6 or S-6 who observe, suspect, or are notified of a possible cyber
incident should prohibit unnecessary activity on the affected system and immediately notify the servicing
regional cyber center. The regional cyber center notifies the Department of the Army Criminal Investigation
Division and counterintelligence elements as necessary. Local network defenders do not further investigate
the incident or isolate the system until directed by the regional cyber center. Additional actions may be
necessary following coordination with ARCYBER, Criminal Investigation Division, or counterintelligence
elements, as any action could compromise the investigation and integrity of forensic data.
A-58. Users who cannot make immediate contact with local network defenders in their G-6 or S-6 for incident
handling contact their servicing network enterprise center service desk to initiate incident reporting until local
network defenders are engaged. If the service desk is not available, users should report to either the Army
Enterprise Service Desk or the regional cyber center.

## BRIGADE COMBAT TEAM

A-59. The brigade S-6 and the brigade signal company perform incident and problem management for the
brigade and subordinate battalions and companies. When DODIN operations personnel identify an incident
at the brigade combat team, they analyze the incident to determine whether it can be resolved locally. At
echelons brigade and below, the local ability to analyze incidents is very limited. If personnel cannot identify
a local solution, they escalate the incident to the next higher headquarters.

## CORPS AND DIVISION

A-60. The corps or division G-6 and the signal, intelligence, and sustainment company (or the headquarters
and headquarters company, division signal battalion) conduct incident reporting for the corps and division
headquarters. When DODIN operations personnel identify an incident at corps or division (or a subordinate
organization), they analyze the incident to determine whether it can be resolved locally. If the corps or
division G-6 cannot identify a local solution, they escalate the incident to the next higher headquarters. If
DODIN operations personnel cannot find a local solution, and a higher headquarters is not part of the incident
handling protocol, corps and division DODIN operations personnel escalate the incident to the regional cyber
center or joint network operations control center.

### SECTION V – ROLES OF DEPARTMENT OF DEFENSE AND FEDERAL

### CYBERSECURITY CENTERS

A-61. The DOD and other federal entities have established cybersecurity centers to synchronize DODIN
operations, enhance information sharing, maintain situational awareness of cyber incidents, and serve as
conduits between public and private sector stakeholders.
**108**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyber Incident Response**

## UNITED STATES CYBER COMMAND

A-62. USCYBERCOM directs, synchronizes, and coordinates cyberspace planning and operations to defend
and advance national interests in collaboration with domestic and international partners. USCYBERCOM
manages both the threat and asset responses for the DODIN and receives support from the other centers, as
needed.

### DEPARTMENT OFDEFENSECYBERDEFENSECOMMAND

A-63. DOD Cyber Defense Command is a subordinate unified command under USCYBERCOM. In this role,
DOD Cyber Defense Command leads the unified force approach to operations, security, and defense of the
DODIN. This mission covers a broad range of activities on behalf of USCYBERCOM, including threat-
informed steps to reduce cybersecurity risk and lead attack response across the DODIN.

### UNITEDSTATESCYBERCOMMANDINTEGRATEDCYBERCENTER/JOINTOPERATIONS

### CENTER

A-64. The Integrated Cyber Center/Joint Operations Center directs cyberspace operations and defense of the
DODIN. The USCYBERCOM director of operations, J-3 oversees all U.S. cyber forces across the globe
from the Integrated Cyber Center/Joint Operations Center to coordinate and deconflict cyberspace operations
worldwide. The Integrated Cyber Center/Joint Operations Center integrates the military, intelligence
community, other federal agencies, and selected multinational partners. The center includes dedicated space
for USCYBERCOM and National Security Agency personnel, who operate under different authorities. The
center serves to link together the other federal cyber centers, the DOD components, and the intelligence
community.

## UNITED STATES ARMY CYBER COMMAND

A-65. Upon determining the category and impact of an incident, the regional cyber center reports details of
the incident to ARCYBER. ARCYBER determines whether the incident is widespread within the DODIN or
localized. ARCYBER provides recommendations, guidance, and orders to regional cyber centers, network
enterprise centers, and units to respond and prevent repeat incidents.

## DEPARTMENT OF DEFENSE CYBER CRIME CENTER

A-66. The DOD Cyber Crime Center supports the law enforcement, counterintelligence, cybersecurity,
defensive cyberspace operations, and critical infrastructure protection communities through digital forensics,
focused threat analysis, and training. The DOD Cyber Crime Center provides analytical and technical
capabilities to interagency partners conducting national cyber incident response.

## NATIONAL SECURITY AGENCY CYBERSECURITY THREAT

## OPERATIONS CENTER

A-67. The National Security Agency’s Cybersecurity Threat Operations Center characterizes and assesses
foreign cybersecurity threats. The center informs partners of current and likely malicious cyberspace activity
through its analysis of foreign intelligence, with a focus on threat computer network attacks, capabilities, and
exploits. Upon request, the National Security Agency Cybersecurity Threat Operations Center also provides
technical assistance to other U.S. Government departments and agencies.

## CYBER THREAT INTELLIGENCE INTEGRATION CENTER

A-68. Operated by the Office of the Director of National Intelligence, the Cyber Threat Intelligence
Integration Center is the primary platform for intelligence integration, analysis, and supporting activities for
the U.S. Government. The center provides integrated all-source analysis of intelligence related to foreign
cyber threats or related to cyber incidents affecting U.S. national interests.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**109**
**Appendix A**

## NATIONAL CYBER INVESTIGATIVE JOINT TASK FORCE

A-69. The National Cyber Investigative Joint Task Force is a multi-agency center hosted by the Federal
Bureau of Investigation. It is the primary platform to coordinate U.S. Government threat response.

## CYBERSECURITY AND INFRASTRUCTURE SECURITY AGENCY

A-70. The Cybersecurity and Infrastructure Security Agency is the component of the Department of
Homeland Security responsible for cybersecurity and infrastructure protection across all levels of
government, coordinating cybersecurity programs with the states, and improving U.S. government
cybersecurity protection against private and nation-state hackers.

**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**

### Appendix B

## Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities Planning and

## Integration

### SECTION I – THE MILITARY DECISION-MAKING PROCESS

B-1. The military decision-making process is an iterative planning methodology to understand the situation
and mission, develop a course of action, and produce an operation plan or order (ADP 5-0). During the
military decision-making process, CEWO maintains the cyberspace and EW running estimate and plans
cyberspace and EW integration into unit’s operation plans and orders.

## RUNNING ESTIMATE

B-2. A running estimate is the continuous assessment of the current situation used to determine if the current
operation is proceeding according to the commander’s intent and if planned future operations are supportable
(ADP 5-0). Commanders and staffs use running estimates throughout the operations process. In their running
estimates, the commander and each staff member continuously consider the effect of new information and
update—
•
Facts.
•
Assumptions.
•
Friendly force status.
•
Enemy activities and capabilities.
•
Civil considerations.
•
Conclusions and recommendations.
B-3. The staff derives the running estimate from facts, assumptions, and situational analysis (terrain,
friendly situation, enemy situation, and support requirements). CEWOs use the cyberspace and EW running
estimate through each step of the military decision-making process. Planners update the running estimate
when—
•
The commander and staff recognize new facts.
•
The staff can replace assumptions with facts or find previous assumptions invalid.
•
The mission changes.
•
Planners receive updated information from other staff sections (for example, the G-2 or S-2 updates
the enemy situation template or the electromagnetic order of battle).
B-4. The cyberspace and EW running estimate outlines the CEMA section and the assigned or supporting
cyberspace and EW elements’ ability to support various friendly courses of action. CEWOs evaluate the
cyberspace and EW requirements for each proposed course of action and match them with the available
support. Evaluating the available support includes considering the capabilities and limitations of organic and
supporting EW systems. CEMA sections must also account for threat and civilian use of cyberspace and the
electromagnetic spectrum. An accurate running estimate is invaluable throughout the military decision-
making process. If cyberspace and EW elements cannot support a proposed course of action, the running
estimate helps the CEWO identify and articulate the shortfall. The cyberspace and EW running estimate
includes all relevant information, including a mission analysis chart. Figure B-1 starting on page 112 shows
an example of a cyberspace and EW running estimate.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**111**
**Appendix B**
1. SITUATION AND CONSIDERATIONS.
a. Area of Interest. Identify and describe those factors of the area of interest that affect cyberspace and EW
considerations.
b. Characteristics of the Assigned Area.
(1) Terrain. State how terrain affects cyberspace and EW capabilities.
(2) Weather. State how weather affects cyberspace and EW capabilities.
(3) Enemy Forces. Describe enemy disposition, composition, strength, and systems as they relate to
cyberspace and electromagnetic warfare (EW) operations. Describe enemy capabilities and possible
courses of action (COAs) and their effects on cyberspace and EW operations. List threat spectrum-
dependent devices, including—
•
System nomenclature and disposition by echelon.
•
System characteristics (frequency, bandwidth, power, and modulation).
•
Threat tactics, techniques, and procedures, including threat means of communication.
•
What the enemy employment of a system or capability is an indication or warning of.
•
Frequency allocations and cueing cycles for radar systems.
•
Modeling and simulations of systems, based on varying parameters and location.
•
Critical capabilities and vulnerabilities by system.
(4) Friendly Forces. List current cyberspace and EW resources in terms of equipment, personnel, and
systems.
•
Friendly EW systems available, including—
▪
System nomenclature and disposition by echelon.
▪
System capabilities.
▪
System information (configuration and location).
▪
Constraints and limitations of each system.
▪
Tactics, techniques, and procedures for each system.
▪
Critical capabilities and vulnerabilities of each system.
▪
Combinations of systems that can be used together or will cause conflict or require
additional support.
•
Friendly EW team capabilities and limitations, including—
▪
Characteristics of EW systems.
▪
Team line of sight and beyond line of sight characteristics.
▪
Team mobility platform characteristics.
▪
Team sustainment requirements.
•
Friendly non-EW systems that depend on or emit in the electromagnetic spectrum, including—
▪
System nomenclature by disposition and echelon (for example, terrestrial radios, satellite
communications, radar, and unmanned aircraft systems).
▪
System characteristics (frequency ranges, bandwidth requirements, power, and modulation).
▪
Modeling and simulation of each system for configuration and location.
▪
Constraints and limitations of each system.
▪
Which friendly systems are vulnerable to threat EW capabilities.
•
Friendly electromagnetic signatures, including—
▪
System nomenclature by disposition and echelon (for example, vehicles and generators).
▪
System characteristics (frequency ranges, power, and detection range).
•
Identify additional cyberspace and EW resources located at higher echelon, adjacent, or other
units. List those capabilities from other military and civilian partners that may be available to
provide cyberspace and EW support. Compare requirements to current capabilities and suggest
solutions for satisfying discrepancies.
(5) Civil Considerations. Describe civil considerations that may affect cyberspace and the
electromagnetic spectrum. Include possible cyber and EW support needed by civil authorities and
possible interference from civil aspects, including—
•
Civilian networks in the unit’s assigned area (for example, supervisory control and data
acquisition, Internet service providers, and fiber optic infrastructure).
•
Spectrum resources, allocation, and use (for example, Wi-Fi, television, radio, and satellite
ground stations).
•
Physical access to civilian infrastructure and equipment.
•
Civilian use of computer systems and applications in the unit’s assigned area (for example
smartphones, social media, and navigation services).

c. Facts and Assumptions. List all facts and assumptions that affect cyberspace and EW operations.
2. MISSION. Show the restated mission resulting from mission analysis.
3. COURSES OF ACTION.
a. List friendly courses of action that were war-gamed.
b. List enemy actions or courses of action that were templated that impact cyberspace and EW operations.

actions that impact cyberspace and EW operations as they relate to courses of action. Identify issues, risks,
and deficiencies these enemy actions may create with respect to cyberspace and EW operations.
5. COMPARISON. Compare courses of action. Rank order courses of action for each key consideration. Use
a decision matrix to aid the comparison.
6. RECOMMENDATIONS AND CONCLUSIONS.
a. Recommend the most supportable course of action from the perspective of cyberspace and EW
operations.
b. Prioritize and list issues, deficiencies, and risks and provide recommendations on how to mitigate them.

B-5. Commanders and staffs continually collect, process, and evaluate information throughout the military
decision-making process. Staff members update their running estimates as they receive and assess new
information. After course of action approval, the cyberspace and EW running estimate forms the basis for
Appendix 12 (Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities) to Annex C (Operations) to the operation plan or order.

## INTELLIGENCE PREPARATION OF THE OPERATIONAL

## ENVIRONMENT

B-6. Information obtained as part of intelligence preparation of the operational environment is key to many
planning activities. The CEMA section collaborates with the rest of the staff during intelligence preparation
of the operational environment to analyze the mission variables in the assigned area. See chapter 4 for details
about the G-2 or S-2 and CEMA section’s roles in intelligence preparation of the operational environment.

## STEPS OF THE MILITARY DECISION-MAKING PROCESS

B-7. The commander, CEMA section, and other staff integrate cyberspace and EW operations throughout
the military decision-making process. The CEMA section relies heavily on collaboration with other members
of the CEMA working group to plan, integrate, and synchronize cyberspace and EW operations and the G-6
or S-6 spectrum manager to deconflict spectrum use throughout the operations process. The military decision-
making process consists of seven steps:
•
Step 1—Receipt of mission.
•
Step 2—Mission analysis.
•
Step 3—Course of action development.
•
Step 4—Course of action analysis.
•
Step 5—Course of action comparison.
•
Step 6—Course of action approval.
•
Step 7—Orders production, dissemination, and transition.

### STEP1—RECEIPT OFMISSION

B-8. Commanders initiate the military decision-making process upon receipt or in anticipation of a mission.
Members of the CEMA section participate throughout the military decision-making process to ensure
integration and synchronization of cyberspace and EW operations into the overall operation. On receipt of
mission, the CEMA section gathers the necessary tools to participate in step 2—mission analysis. These tools
include—
•
The cyberspace and EW running estimate, which includes—
▪
Facts regarding cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum in the assigned area.
▪
Assumptions regarding cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum in the assigned area.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**113**
**Appendix B**
▪
Friendly forces’ cyberspace and EW capabilities in the assigned area, including available
technical data.
▪
Outgoing unit’s high-payoff target list recommendations, if applicable.
▪
Outgoing unit’s detailed frequencies of interest, if applicable.
▪
Enemy activities and cyberspace and EW capabilities, including available technical data.
▪
Electromagnetic order of battle from the G-2 or S-2.
•
Relevant publications, including Army and joint regulations and doctrine on cyberspace and EW
operations.
•
All documents related to the mission and assigned area, including—
▪
The higher headquarters’ order, including operational graphics.
▪
Maps and terrain products.
▪
Known friendly, neutral, and threat cyberspace and EW capabilities and use of the
electromagnetic spectrum in the unit’s assigned area.
•
Higher headquarters and other organizations’ intelligence and assessment products on—
▪
Friendly, neutral, and threat cyberspace and EW activities, use, and characteristics.
▪
Cyberspace- and EW-related terrain aspects in the unit’s assigned area.
B-9. The CEMA section begins updating the cyberspace and EW running estimate, especially the status of
friendly forces, cyberspace and EW capabilities, use of the electromagnetic spectrum, and key civil
considerations when employing cyberspace attack and electromagnetic attack. Considerations for the running
estimate specific to the assigned area include—
•
Civilian systems.
▪
Airports (air traffic control radar and air-to-ground communications frequencies).
▪
Cellular infrastructure (cell towers and cell service provider facilities).
▪
Internet service providers and their connections to the public Internet.
▪
Emergency services (police, fire, and ambulance).
▪
Hospitals.
•
Allied systems.
▪
Communications.
▪
Radar.
▪
Intelligence, surveillance, and reconnaissance.
▪
Satellite based positioning, navigation, and timing.
▪
Offensive and defensive cyberspace capabilities.
▪
Electromagnetic sensing and attack capabilities.
•
Airspace deconfliction.
•
Joint and other Service systems used in the assigned area.
▪
Communications.
▪
Radar.
▪
Intelligence, surveillance, and reconnaissance.
▪
Offensive cyberspace operations.
▪
Ground-based and airborne EW capabilities.
B-10. The CEMA section conducts an initial assessment of time and resources available to plan, prepare, and
execute cyberspace and EW operations that may support the commander’s concept of operations. Table B-1
on page 115 illustrates the key inputs, substeps, and key outputs of the CEMA section during step 1 of the
military decision-making process.

### STEP2—MISSIONANALYSIS

B-11. The military decision-making process continues with mission analysis to assess the situation. The
commander and staff, informed by subordinate and adjacent commanders and joint and multinational
partners, gather, analyze, and synthesize information to understand current conditions in the operational
environment. Through mission analysis, the commander and staff generate situational understanding. They
can then identify what the command must accomplish, when and where operations must take place, and why
the operation is required.
B-12. The CEMA section analyzes and synthesizes the cyberspace and EW tools gathered during step 1 to
develop understanding of cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum within the unit’s assigned area. The
CEMA section analyzes the higher headquarters order, focusing on the base order and Appendix 12
(Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities) to Annex C (Operations).
B-13. By analyzing the higher headquarters’ order, the CEMA section develops understanding of the unit’s
cyberspace and EW tasks, purpose, and how they contribute to the mission, and the commander’s intent and
concept of operations. During mission analysis, the CEMA section uses the higher headquarters’ order to
develop understanding of available cyberspace and EW assets, the timeline of the operation, and other
Services and multinational partners’ cyberspace and EW missions in the assigned area.
B-14. During mission analysis, the G-2 or S-2 leads intelligence preparation of the operational environment
to analyze the mission variables of enemy, terrain and weather, and civil considerations in an area of interest.
The Air Force staff weather officer can assist the G-2 or S-2 in analysis of weather and terrain considerations
as a subject matter expert in terrestrial and space weather analysis. The CEMA section collaborates with the
G-2 or S-2 throughout intelligence preparation of the operational environment to help analyze the effects of
those variables on cyberspace and EW operations. The G-2 or S-2 continually updates the electromagnetic
order of battle. This collaboration includes using tools and products received from higher headquarters to
conduct the initial terrain analysis.
B-15. Intelligence preparation of the operational environment identifies critical gaps in the commander’s
knowledge of the operational environment, including gaps in one or more of cyberspace and spectrum-related
aspects of the terrain, threat, and overall operational environment. The commander and staff use these gaps
as a guide to establish their information requirements.
B-16. The CEMA section identifies and nominates information requirements to address information gaps
identified during mission analysis to develop situational understanding of cyberspace and the electromagnetic
spectrum. The CEMA section and other staff sections’ input to intelligence preparation of the operational
environment during mission analysis results in—
•
Initial priority intelligence requirements.
•
A complete modified combined obstacle overlay with cyberspace and EW considerations within the
assigned area.
•
Threat overlays, including—
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**115**
**Appendix B**
▪
Cyberspace and electromagnetic spectrum considerations within the assigned area.
▪
How threat units communicate.
▪
Likely threat command and control links.
•
A list of cyberspace- and spectrum-related high-value targets.
•
Identified cyberspace- and spectrum-related military aspects of the terrain.
•
Unrefined event templates and matrixes that include cyberspace and EW considerations.
B-17. The CEMA section analyzes the higher headquarters’ order and the higher commander’s guidance to
determine their specified, implied, and essential cyberspace and EW tasks. The ‘what’ of a mission statement
in the higher headquarters’ plan or order is always a task. The CEMA section determines essential tasks from
the specified and implied cyberspace and EW tasks in the unit’s recommended mission statement.
B-18. The CEMA section analyzes the current task organization of the unit’s cyberspace and EW assets,
command and support relationships for augmentation, and status of current cyberspace and EW capabilities
and their limitations. The CEMA section also analyzes the cyberspace and EW capabilities of adjacent Army
units and joint and multinational partners. This analysis results in the CEMA section determining whether
the unit has the assets needed to complete all specified, implied, and essential tasks and to identify other
cyberspace and EW resources required or available to fulfill those tasks for mission success. The CEWO
informs the commander of all identified cyberspace and EW resource gaps that need forwarding to higher
headquarters. The CEMA section advises the commander of any deviations necessary from the standard task
organization to consider when developing planning guidance.
B-19. The CEMA section identifies constraints placed on the unit regarding cyberspace and EW operations.
Constraints should appear in paragraph 3 of the higher headquarters’ order. They may also appear in
Appendix 12 (Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities) to Annex C (Operations) of the higher headquarters’
order. The CEMA section should be aware of cyberspace and EW limitations, including—
•
Spectrum usage.
•
The types of cyberspace and EW capabilities allowed or disallowed.
•
Cyberspace and EW authorities, either delegated or not delegated to the unit commander.
•
Higher headquarters’ EMCON criteria.
B-20. The CEMA section gathers critical facts based on analyzing the operational and mission variables
provided through intelligence preparation of the operational environment and from adjacent, higher, and joint
organizations and other mission partners. This information helps with developing assumptions for cyberspace
and the electromagnetic spectrum throughout the assigned area. In the absence of facts, the CEMA section
makes informed, relevant assumptions about the use of cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum within
their assigned area. These assumptions include known threat cyberspace and EW capabilities, use of the
electromagnetic spectrum, and projected locations. Staffs should limit their assumptions to only those needed
to support decision making.
B-21. The CEMA section uses the risk management process to mitigate cybersecurity vulnerabilities. The
CEMA section collaborates with the G-6 or S-6 spectrum manager to review the joint restricted frequency
list and mitigate possible frequency and network conflicts. The CEMA section plans electromagnetic
protection to—
•
Mitigate electromagnetic environmental effects.
•
Mitigate electromagnetic compatibility issues.
•
Implement electromagnetic hardening.
•
Implement electromagnetic masking.
•
Implement EMCON.
B-22. During mission analysis, the CEMA section identifies cyberspace- and spectrum-related gaps in the
information received from higher headquarters, adjacent and joint units, and multinational partners. These
gaps are relayed to the commander for consideration as commander’s critical information requirements,
which fall into two categories—priority intelligence requirements and friendly force information
requirements.
B-23. The CEMA section works closely with the G-3 or S-3 and the G-2 or S-2 in developing the initial
information collection plan. The CEMA section also works with these sections to adjust the information
**116**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities Planning and Integration**
collection effort to answer information requirements necessary to develop an effective plan. The CEMA
section nominates cyberspace- and EW-related information requirements as part of their contribution to the
information collection plan.
B-24. The initial information collection plan sets intelligence, surveillance, reconnaissance, and security,
operations in motion. The G-2 or S-2 manages information collection and works closely with the G-3 or
S-3 to collect information supporting intelligence preparation of the operational environment by
synchronizing all available combat information, intelligence products, and electromagnetic support
capabilities to satisfy the commander’s information requirements and develop situational understanding.
B-25. As more information becomes available, the CEMA section updates the cyberspace and EW running
estimate. This may result in recommended adjustments to the operation plan or order. The CEMA section
considers the time available by comparing the time needed to accomplish cyberspace and EW tasks to the
higher headquarters’ timeline and determines whether mission accomplishment is possible within the allotted
time. The CEMA section makes the necessary adjustments to the operation plan or order to stay within the
higher headquarters’ timeline and informs the commander of any task that cannot be accomplished within
the allotted time, especially critical and essential tasks. The commander uses this information to make
informed risk decisions and to inform the higher headquarters of gaps identified due to timeline constraints.
B-26. The CEMA section contributes cyberspace and EW subject matter expertise to development of course
of action evaluation criteria. Developing these criteria during mission analysis helps eliminate bias before
analyzing and comparing courses of action. Table B-2 on page 118 illustrates the key inputs, substeps, and
key outputs during step 2—mission analysis.

### STEP3—COURSE OFACTIONDEVELOPMENT

B-27. Courses of action are broad potential solutions to a specific military problem. Course of action
development generates options for subsequent analysis and comparison to satisfy the commander’s intent
and planning guidance. During course of action development, the staff uses the planning products received
and analyzed from higher headquarters, adjacent and joint units, and multinational partners in conjunction
**118**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities Planning and Integration**
with the commander’s intent and planning guidance. The CEMA section contributes to course of action
development by integrating and synchronizing cyberspace and EW operations into proposed courses of action
using operational art. During course of action development, the CEMA section develops varying methods to
integrate, synchronize, and conduct cyberspace and EW operations that are within the commander’s intent
and planning guidance.
B-28. The CEMA section assists the other staff with examining each proposed course of action for validity
using the evaluation criteria established during mission analysis. When considering the validity of each
proposed course of action, screening criteria should include—
•
**Feasibility.** Cyberspace and EW operations integrated and synchronized into the proposed courses
of action to accomplish the mission within the established time, space, and cyberspace and EW
resource limitations.
•
**Acceptability.** Cyberspace and EW operations integrated and synchronized into the proposed
courses of action to balance cost and risk with the advantage gained.
•
**Suitability.** Cyberspace and EW operations support the courses of action to accomplish the mission
within the commander’s intent and planning guidance.
•
**Distinguishability.** Cyberspace and EW operations integration, synchronization, and conduct of
operations should be different for each course of action developed. Differences should include task
organization, cyberspace and EW capabilities, and the use of cyberspace and EW resources from
adjacent units and joint and multinational partners.
•
**Completeness.**Cyberspace and EW operations integrated and synchronized into the proposed
courses of action should incorporate—
▪
How cyberspace and EW operations support each course of action, accounting for offensive,
defensive, and stability or defense support of civil authorities’ tasks.
▪
How cyberspace and EW operations enable the main and supporting efforts.
▪
How to perform cyberspace and EW operations specified, implied, and essential tasks to create
desired conditions in cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum.
▪
How cyberspace and EW operations will help achieve the commander’s objectives.
▪
How to use cyberspace and EW capabilities to mitigate obstacles, gain access to various
avenues of approach (including observation and fields of fire), and protect key terrain in
cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum.
B-29. During course of action development, the commander and staff need to understand and appreciate the
unpredictable and uncertain nature of their operational environment. The CEMA section should show this
same understanding and appreciation for unpredictability and uncertainty when supporting the development
of schemes of cyberspace and EW operations for each course of action.
B-30. The CEMA section makes recommendations for cyberspace and EW operations as enablers for combat
power (assess the dynamics of combat power).
B-31. The CEMA section identifies and assesses organic and non-organic cyberspace and EW capabilities
available for the operation, along with the dependency of the unit and its systems on cyberspace and the
electromagnetic spectrum. This assessment assists the CEMA section in making a rough estimate of the ratio
of friendly cyberspace and EW capabilities to known or anticipated enemy cyberspace and EW capabilities.
B-32. The CEMA section compares the combined (organic and non-organic) friendly cyberspace and EW
capabilities against enemy cyberspace and EW vulnerabilities, and vice versa. The CEMA section uses this
comparison to identify vulnerabilities in friendly cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum and gain
insight into enemy vulnerabilities to exploit throughout the assigned area.
B-33. Based on the commander’s guidance and the initial results of relative combat power assessment, the
CEMA section generates different schemes of cyberspace and EW operations to support the staff’s proposed
courses of action. The scheme for each course of action should indicate opposition toward each likely enemy
course of action while accounting for essential stability tasks (providing a level of civil security, civil control,
and certain essential services). The staff incorporates brainstorming to generate options to produce the
broadest range of choices in establishing schemes of cyberspace and EW operations for the courses of action.
All staff members should remain unbiased and open-minded when brainstorming proposed options.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**119**
**Appendix B**
B-34. The CEMA section determines the necessary Army and joint requirements for cyberspace operations,
EW, and supported and supporting operations. The CEMA section further considers cyberspace and EW
capabilities of non-organic units and other non-military organizations and agencies.
B-35. The commander and staff develop an operational framework to visualize and describe the application
of combat power in time, space, purpose, and resources while developing the concept of operations. The
three models commonly used to build an operational framework are—
•
Assigned areas.
•
Deep, close, and rear operations.
•
Main effort, supporting effort, and reserve.
B-36. The staff brainstorms to generate options on how to provide the necessary cyberspace and EW
capabilities to support the three components of the operational framework. Once the CEMA section has
explored approaches to provide cyberspace and EW operations support for each course of action, the staff
examines each course of action to determine whether it satisfies the evaluation criteria (see paragraph B-26),
changing, adding, and eliminating courses of action as appropriate. The CEMA section places extensive focus
on cyberspace and EW operations to support each proposed course of action during the screening. The staff
should avoid creating one good course of action among several inadequate courses of action when generating
options.
B-37. The CEMA section determines the necessary cyberspace and EW capabilities to employ as enablers
for combat power required to support deep, close, and rear operations. The CEMA section also considers the
relative cyberspace and EW capabilities necessary to shape the operational environment, resulting in an initial
array that identifies the cyberspace and EW assets needed to accomplish the mission. The CEMA section
adds this information to the unit’s total array of forces used in the commander’s broad concept of operations.
In the concept of operations, the commander describes how arrayed forces accomplish the mission within the
commander’s intent.
B-38. The CEMA section participates in the course of action briefing in which the staff briefs each proposed
course of action to the commander. The CEWO briefs the commander on the cyberspace and EW activities
and capabilities included in each course of action, including anticipating effects on the operational and
mission variables. The CEWO also describes how cyberspace and EW effects may result in or support the
desired outcome in each proposed course of action. After the course of action briefing, the commander selects
or modifies courses of action for continued analysis and issues planning guidance.
B-39. Throughout course of action development, the CEMA section continues to update the cyberspace and
EW running estimate and intelligence preparation of the operational environment products (such as modified
combined obstacle overlays, electromagnetic order of battle, high-value target list, threat courses of action,
and key aspects of terrain). Table B-3 on page 121 illustrates the CEMA section’s key inputs, substeps, and
key outputs of the during course of action development.

B-40. Using the information obtained through the collaborative efforts of all staff sections, the CEMA section
develops cyberspace and EW statements and sketches for the G-3 or S-3 to incorporate with the statement
and supporting sketches for each course of action. The statement expresses how cyberspace and EW
operations contribute to a combined arms approach for the course of action. The sketch provides visuals of
how cyberspace and EW operations will support maneuver forces, including the positioning of forces.
B-41. The commander may reject all courses of action, accept one or more courses of action, or incorporate
elements of two or more presented courses of action into a new course of action. If the commander rejects
all proposed courses of action, the staff starts over at developing new courses of action. Accepted or modified
courses of action move forward to step 4—analysis and war-gaming.

### STEP4—COURSE OFACTIONANALYSIS ANDWAR-GAMING

B-42. Course of action analysis helps the commander and staff to identify difficulties, coordination problems,
or probable consequences of planned actions for each course of action accepted or modified during course of
action development. During this step, the CEMA section identifies cyberspace and electromagnetic spectrum
vulnerabilities in each proposed course of action. These vulnerabilities may include—
•
Likely undesirable cyberspace and EW effects to the operational and mission variables and
undesired impacts on cyberspace and EW capabilities due to the operational and mission variables.
•
Possible coordination issues with non-organic organizations for cyberspace and EW support.
•
Lack of available cyberspace and EW capabilities to achieve the desired outcome.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**121**
**Appendix B**
•
Reduced survivability due to enemy observation of cyberspace activity or electromagnetic
emissions.
B-43. The CEMA section uses this information to revisit and refine cyberspace and EW portions of the course
of action as discrepancies arise. Analysis may also reveal potential execution problems, decisions, and
contingencies and may require restarting the planning process.
B-44. One process used to analyze courses of action is called war-gaming. War-gaming consists of rules and
steps to visualize the flow of an operation, given the force’s strengths and disposition, the enemy’s
capabilities, and each proposed course of action. War-gaming incorporates the mission variables into the
scenario to include the impact and requirements of civilians in the assigned area.
B-45. Through war-gaming, the CEMA section can visualize and articulate the required capabilities to affect
cyberspace and EW targets in the assigned area. The CEMA section can also identify vulnerabilities that may
require cyberspace defense and electromagnetic protection. During war-gaming, the CEMA section assists
the G-3 or S-3 in the war-gaming process by employing and assessing cyberspace and EW operations as part
of the friendly scheme of maneuver. The CEMA section also assists the G-3 or S-3 by providing cyberspace
and EW input into the course of action statement and supporting sketch for each course of action. War-
gaming results in refined courses of action with updated schemes of cyberspace and EW operations, a
completed synchronization matrix, and decision support templates and matrixes for each course of action.
**Note****.** The G-6 or S-6 assesses DODIN operations, spectrum management operations, and their
feasibility for each war-gamed course of action with input from the CEMA section regarding threat
cyberspace and EW capabilities and courses of action.
B-46. A synchronization matrix records the results of a war game and depicts how friendly forces
synchronize in time, space, and purpose relating to an enemy course of action for each proposed friendly
course of action. The depiction includes the synchronization and integration of cyberspace and EW operations
in support of the warfighting functions for each course of action. The decision support template and matrix
illustrate key decisions and potential actions that are likely to arise during the execution of each course of
action.
B-47. The CEMA section participates in the war game briefing, delivering a brief to subordinate cyber and
EW elements to ensure everyone understands the results of the war game. The CEMA section captures all
cyberspace and EW-related points of the war game before presenting the briefing to the commander. The
CEMA section drafts and submits a CERF or electromagnetic attack request format for additional cyberspace
and EW assets to mitigate capability gaps and vulnerabilities identified during war-gaming. Table B-4 on
page 123 illustrates the key inputs, substeps, and key outputs of the CEMA section during course of action
analysis and war-gaming.

### STEP5—COURSE OFACTIONCOMPARISON

B-48. The commander and staff evaluate war-gamed courses of action objectively and independently against
the evaluation criteria established during mission analysis and refined during course of action development.
The goal is to identify the strengths and weaknesses of each proposed course of action, select the course of
action with the highest probability of success, and further develop the operation plan or order.
B-49. The CEMA section analyzes advantages and disadvantages of each proposed course of action against
the evaluation criteria (see paragraph B-26) using a decision matrix. The CEMA section highlights the
cyberspace and EW advantages and disadvantages for each proposed course of action. The CEMA section
presents those findings to the commander and other staff members. Feedback received in response to the
CEMA section’s CERFs and electromagnetic attack request formats contributes to the determining factors
during the advantage and disadvantage analysis.
B-50. The staff implements findings from the analysis of advantages and disadvantages into a decision matrix
for the commander to compare and evaluate courses of action thoroughly and logically. A decision matrix
assists the commander in making a sound decision.
B-51. After analysis and comparison, the staff identifies the preferred course of action and makes a
recommendation. If the staff cannot reach a decision, the chief of staff (executive officer) decides which
course of action to recommend. Table B-5 on page 124 illustrates the actions and key outputs of the CEMA
section during course of action comparison.

### STEP6—COURSE OFACTIONAPPROVAL

B-52. The staff delivers a decision briefing to the commander with the chief of staff highlighting changes to
each course of action resulting from the war game. After the decision briefing, the commander selects the
course of action that best accomplishes the mission. If the commander rejects all courses of action, the staff
restarts the course of action development process. If the commander modifies a proposed course of action or
gives the staff an entirely different course of action, the staff war-games the new course of action and presents
the results to the commander with a recommendation.
B-53. Upon approval of a course of action, the commander issues the final planning guidance that includes a
refined commander’s intent and new commander’s critical information requirements to support execution.
The final planning guidance also contains any additional guidance on priorities for the warfighting functions,
orders preparation, rehearsals, and preparation activities. It also includes priorities to preserve freedom of
action and ensure continuous sustainment.
B-54. The commander identifies the acceptable risk level in the final planning guidance to gain approval
from the higher commander before accepting risks that could affect the higher commander’s mission. Based
on the commander’s decision and final planning guidance, the staff converts the approved course of action
into the concept of operations and issues a warning order to subordinate commanders. The CEMA section
provides input to the warning order, as required. The CEMA section continues refining Appendix 12
(Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities) to Annex C (Operations) of the plan or order and assists the G-6 or
S-6 with Appendixes 1 and 6 of Annex H as appropriate. Table B-6 on page 125 illustrates the key inputs,
substeps, and key outputs of the CEMA section during course of action approval.
