### STEP7—ORDERSPRODUCTION, DISSEMINATION,ANDTRANSITION

B-55. The staff turns the approved course of action into an operation plan or order with a clear, concise
concept of operations and supporting information. The course of action statement becomes the concept of
operations for the plan or order. The CEMA section drafts the final Appendix 12 (Cyberspace
Electromagnetic Activities) to Annex C (Operations). The CEMA section assists the G-6 or
S-6 with appendixes 1 and 6 of Annex H (Signal) as appropriate.
B-56. Before the commander approves the plan or order, the staff ensures its consistency and that it is nested
with the higher commander’s intent through plans and orders reconciliation and crosswalk. The staff
reconciles the plan or order by conducting a detailed review of the entire plan or order and all attachments,
ensuring all staff members are in complete agreement. During reconciliation, the staff explicitly compares
the commander’s intent, mission, and commander’s critical information requirements against the concept of
operations and the different schemes of support, including those for cyberspace and EW operations. Any
discrepancies or gaps in planning receive corrective actions.
B-57. The staff conducts the plans and orders crosswalk by comparing the plan or order with those of the
higher and adjacent commanders to achieve unity of effort and ensure the plan meets the higher commander’s
intent. As with reconciliation, the crosswalk also identifies discrepancies or gaps in planning that require
corrective actions. Step 7 bridges the transition between the planning and preparation activities of the
operations process. The final function of step 7 is the commander’s approval. The commander signs the plan
or order, the G-3 or S-3 disseminates the approved plan or order to subordinate headquarters, and the
commander and staff begin the transition to the preparation activities of the operations process. Table B-7 on
page 126 illustrates the key inputs, substeps, and key outputs of the CEMA section during orders production,
dissemination, and transition.

## OPERATION PLAN OR ORDER

B-58. Cyberspace and EW information appears in various paragraphs to Annex C (Operations) and Annex
H (Signal) of an operation plan or order. The scheme of cyberspace and EW operations appears in paragraph
3.g. (Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities) and 5.g. (Signal) of operation plans, operation orders, and
fragmentary orders. In warning orders, cyberspace and EW operations information appears in paragraph 5.g.
(Signal).

### PARAGRAPH3.E. (SCHEME OFINFORMATIONCOLLECTION)

B-59. The CEWO coordinates with the collection manager and the technical control and analysis cell to
coordinate who and when in time and space the CEMA section can answer information requirements based
on organic assets. This coordination includes priority intelligence requirements and friendly force
information requirements. Refer to ATP 3-12.4 for examples.

### PARAGRAPH3.F. (SCHEME OFFIRES)

B-60. The CEWO fulfills three main objectives in supporting the scheme of fires—
•
Establishing synchronization between electromagnetic support assets and gun target lines or mortar
firing points and optimizing the lethal to nonlethal turnover time to reduce the time commanders (at
echelon) spend making decisions.
•
Applying appropriate electromagnetic attack when applicable to accomplish the commander’s
objectives.
•
Nominating targets for lethal or nonlethal attack, based on cyberspace or electromagnetic spectrum
considerations of either their exposure or value.

### PARAGRAPH3.G. (SCHEME OFPROTECTION)

B-61. Paragraph 3.g. (Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities) describes how cyberspace and EW operations
support the concept of operations and refers readers to Appendix 12 (Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities)
to Annex C (Operations), Annex E (Protection), and Annex H (Signal) as required.

### PARAGRAPH3.K. (COORDINATINGINSTRUCTIONS)

B-62. The CEMA section’s input to paragraph 3.k. (Coordinating Instructions) should include any
cyberspace operations or EW tasks that apply to two or more units, such as task organization of EW teams
with scout elements or SIGINT elements.
**126**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities Planning and Integration**

### PARAGRAPH5.G. (SIGNAL)

B-63. Paragraph 5.g. (Signal) contains information regarding DODIN operations and spectrum management
operations. The CEMA spectrum manager collaborates with the G-6 or S-6 spectrum manager to deconflict
frequency use between friendly EW and communications requirements.

### ANNEXB (INTELLIGENCE)

B-64. The CEMA section draws relevant cyberspace and EW threat information from paragraph 1.c
(Situation) of Annex B (Intelligence). Paragraph 3.d summarizes SIGINT and electromagnetic support tasks.
Appendix 3 summarizes key tasks and requirements for SIGINT support.

### ANNEXD (FIRES)

B-65. The CEMA section provides cyberspace and EW targeting information, including desired effects to
contribute to the scheme of fires.

### ANNEXE (PROTECTION)

B-66. The CEMA section describes how electromagnetic protection, defensive cyberspace operations, and
cybersecurity tasks support the scheme of protection in paragraph 3.a. Scheme of Protection, of the operation
plan or order.

### APPENDIX5 (SPECTRUMMANAGEMENTOPERATIONS)TOANNEXH (SIGNAL)

B-67. The CEMA spectrum manager assists the G-6 or S-6 spectrum manager in preparing Appendix 5—
Spectrum Management Operations, to Annex H (Signal).

### ANNEXL (INFORMATIONCOLLECTION)

B-68. Paragraph 3.a. (5) (d). (Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities Support). Summarize limitations or any
special considerations regarding intelligence, surveillance, and reconnaissance platform or sensor tasking.
Refer to Annex D (Fires) regarding procedures for submitting reconnaissance objectives and determining
collection priorities. This is only applicable to tactical and operational units providing cyberspace and EW
support or conducting cyberspace and EW operations. This is accomplished through participation in the
development of the information collection plan (see paragraph 4-71).

### ANNEXN (SPACEOPERATIONS)

B-69. The CEMA section assists the space officer as necessary in preparing paragraph 3.b. (11) of the
operation plan or order.

### APPENDIX12 (CYBERSPACEELECTROMAGNETICACTIVITIES)TOANNEXC (OPERATIONS)

B-70. The CEWO has primary staff responsibility for preparing Appendix 12 (Cyberspace Electromagnetic
Activities) to Annex C (Operations) of the operation plan or operation order. Attachments to Appendix 12
(Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities) to Annex C (Operations) include—
•
Tab A—Offensive Cyberspace Operations.
•
Tab B—Defensive Cyberspace Operations.
•
Tab C—Electromagnetic Attack.
•
Tab D—Electromagnetic Protection.
•
Tab E—Electromagnetic Support.
B-71. Figure B-2 starting on page 128 shows the instructions for preparation of Appendix 12 (Cyberspace
Electromagnetic Activities) to Annex C (Operations). Refer to FM 5-0 for more information about plans,
orders, and planning processes.

(U)**References:**Add any specific references to cyberspace electromagnetic activities (CEMA), if needed.
**1. (U) Situation.**Include information affecting cyberspace operations and electromagnetic warfare (EW)
that paragraph 1 of Annex C (Operations) does not cover or that needs expansion.
a. (U) Area of Interest. Include information affecting cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum;
cyberspace may expand the area of local interest to a worldwide interest.
b. (U) Assigned Area. Include information affecting cyberspace and the electromagnetic spectrum;
cyberspace may expand the assigned area outside the physical maneuver space.
c. (U) Enemy Forces. List known and templated locations and cyberspace and EW unit activities for one
echelon above and two echelons below the order. Identify the vulnerabilities of enemy information systems
and cyberspace and EW systems. List enemy cyberspace and EW operations that will impact friendly
operations. State probable enemy courses of action and employment of enemy cyberspace and EW assets.
See Annex B (Intelligence) as required.
d. (U) Friendly Forces. Outline the higher headquarters’ CEMA plan. List plan designation, location and
outline of higher, adjacent, and other cyberspace and EW operations assets that support or impact the
issuing headquarters or require coordination and additional support. Identify friendly cyberspace and EW
operations assets and resources that affect the subordinate commander. Identify friendly forces cyberspace
and electromagnetic spectrum vulnerabilities. Identify friendly foreign forces with which subordinate
commanders may operate. Identify potential conflicts within the electromagnetic spectrum, especially for
joint or multinational operations. Deconflict and prioritize spectrum distribution.
e. (U) Interagency, Intergovernmental, and Nongovernmental Organizations. Identify and describe other
organizations in the assigned area that may impact cyberspace and EW operations or implementation of
cyberspace and EW operations specific equipment and tactics. See Annex V (Interagency) as required.
f. (U) Third Party. Identify and describe other organizations, both local and external to the assigned
area that can influence cyberspace and EW operations or the implementation of cyberspace and EW
operations specific equipment and tactics. This category includes criminal and non-state sponsored rogue
elements.
g. (U) Civil Considerations. Describe the aspects of the civil situation that impact cyberspace and EW
operations. See Tab C (Civil Considerations) to Appendix 1 (Intelligence Estimate) to Annex B (Intelligence)
and Annex K (Civil Affairs Operations) as required.

**2.** (U) **Mission**. State the commander’s mission and describe cyberspace and EW operations to support the

**3. (U) Execution****.**
a. Scheme of Cyberspace Electromagnetic Activities. Describe how cyberspace and EW operations
support the commander’s intent and concept of operations. Establish the priorities of support to units for
each phase of the operation. State how cyberspace and EW effects will degrade, disrupt, deny, and deceive
the enemy. State the defensive and offensive cyberspace and EW measures. Identify target sets and effects, by
priority. Describe the general concept for the integration of cyberspace and EW operations. List the staff
sections, elements, and working groups responsible for aspects of CEMA. Include the cyberspace and EW
collection methods for information developed in staff sections, elements, and working groups outside the
CEMA section and working group. Describe the plan for the integration of governmental and
nongovernmental partners and organizations. See Annex C (Operations) as required. This section is
designed to provide insight and understanding of the components of cyberspace and EW and how these
activities are integrated across the operation plan. It is recommended that this appendix include an
understanding of technical requirements. This appendix concentrates on the integration requirements for
cyberspace and EW operations and references appropriate annexes and appendixes as needed to reduce
duplication.
(1) (U) Organization for Combat. Provide direction for the proper organization for combat,
including the unit designation, nomenclature, and tactical task.
(2) (U) Miscellaneous. Provide any other information necessary for planning not already mentioned.
b. (U) Scheme of Cyberspace Operations. Describe how cyberspace operations support the
commander’s intent and concept of operations. Describe the general concept for the implementation of
planned cyberspace operations measures. Describe the process to integrate joint and multinational partners
and nongovernmental organizations into operations, including cyberspace requirements and constraints.
Identify risks associated with cyberspace operations. Include collateral damage, discovery, attribution,
fratricide (to U.S., allied, or multinational networks or information), and possible conflicts. Describe actions
that will prevent enemy and adversary action(s) to critically degrade the combatant command’s ability to
effectively conduct military operations in its assigned area. Identify countermeasures and the responsible
agency. List the warnings, and how they will be monitored. State how the cyberspace operations tasks will
destroy, degrade, disrupt, and deny enemy computer networks. Identify and prioritize target sets and effect(s)
in cyberspace. If appropriate, state how cyberspace operations support the accomplishment of the operation.
Identify plans to detect or assign attribution of enemy and adversary actions in the physical domains and
cyberspace. Ensure subordinate units are conducting defensive cyberspace operations. Synchronize the
CEMA section with the G-39. Pass requests for offensive cyberspace operations to higher headquarters for
approval and implementation. Describe how DODIN operations support the commander’s intent and
concept of operations. Synchronize DODIN operations with the G-6 or S-6. Prioritize the allocation of
applications utilizing cyberspace. Ensure the employment of cyberspace capabilities where the primary
purpose is to achieve objectives in or through cyberspace. Considerations should be made for degraded
networks. (Reference appropriate annexes and appendixes as needed to reduce duplication).

### SECTION II – WORKING GROUPS

B-72. The CEWO and the CEMA section participate in several of the staff working groups. The CEWO is
the staff lead for the CEMA working group (see chapter 4). Other working groups in which the CEMA section
and CEWO participate include—
•
Information collection working group.
•
Targeting working group.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**131**
**Appendix B**
•
Targeting coordination board.
•
Protection working group.
•
Assessment working group.
**Note****.** During periods of high operating tempo, the chief of staff and the staff principals may
combine working groups to synchronize efforts and increase efficiencies while minimizing
requirements on the staff. Examples include combining the civil-military operations working
group, information operations working group, and CEMA working group to streamline
coordination prior to the targeting working group.

## INFORMATION COLLECTION WORKING GROUP

B-73. The CEWO is a key participant in the information collection working group led by the G-2 or S-2. The
working group develops and maintains the unit’s information collection plan to ensure effective use of the
unit’s collection assets. During the information collection working group, the CEWO provides status of
collection efforts against the collection plan and nominates information requirements and recommendations
for EW elements to integrate into the collection plan.
B-74. The CEWO’s inputs must address—
•
The technical capabilities and limitations of available organic electromagnetic support assets.
•
What electromagnetic support assets will be available and can be leveraged by phase of the
operation.
•
The primary, alternate, contingency, and emergency communication plan for information
dissemination by asset.
•
A rollup of the targets identified and processed since the last working group.
•
Recommendations for potential collection efforts until the next working group.
B-75. The CEWO should be prepared to deconflict efforts with other collection assets to best suit the
commander’s collection priorities. The cyberspace and EW running estimate should include maintenance
cycles for each EW system and set reasonable expectations for up and down times to the rest of the
information collection working group.
B-76. Key inputs for the information collection working group include—
•
Commander’s guidance.
•
Commander’s critical information requirements.
•
Future operations requirements.
•
Subordinate unit requirements.
•
Air tasking order nomination.
B-77. Key outputs of the information collection working group include—
•
Priorities and recommendations for collection.
•
An updated collection plan.
•
Recommended changes to commander’s critical information requirements.
•
Input for fragmentary orders.
B-78. Table B-8 on page 133 shows an example of information collection working group activities and
agenda at the division level. Refer to ATP 2-01 for more information about collection management.

## TARGETING WORKING GROUP

B-79. The chief of fires (at the theater army), deputy fire support coordinator (at corps or division), or fire
support officer (at brigade) leads the targeting working group. Members of the targeting working group
perform the detailed staff work associated with targeting. Based on the commander’s guidance and priorities,
the targeting working group determines which targets to engage and how, where, and when to engage them.
The staff then assigns friendly capabilities to locate and track targets and engage those targets to produce the
desired effect on each target. Members present the results of their work to the commander at the targeting
coordination board. The CEWO helps integrate cyberspace and EW operations in the Army targeting
methodology to—
•
**Decide**—providing a running estimate of high-priority target vulnerabilities in cyberspace and the
electromagnetic spectrum and nominating targets based on their value in cyberspace or the
electromagnetic spectrum.
•
**Detect**—using electromagnetic support assets.
•
**Deliver effects**—using electromagnetic attack or other fires capabilities.
•
**Assess effects**—using electromagnetic support assets.
B-80. The CEWO must be prepared to provide technical guidance in the decide phase of the Army targeting
methodology. As in the information collection working group, the CEWO briefs the capabilities and
limitations of available organic and supporting non-organic EW assets and nominates potential detection
assets moving into the detect phase. The CEWO provides continuing recommendations for the delivery of
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**133**
**Appendix B**
electromagnetic attack or other fires capabilities that can effectively create desired effects according to the
commander’s high-payoff target list. The targeting working group must synchronize these effects with other
information activities to maximize the commander’s available detection and delivery capabilities.
B-81. Table B-9 on page 135 shows sample targeting working group activities at the division or corps level.
The composition and activities of the targeting working group may require adjustment for the theater army
or brigade combat team.

B-82. The CEWO’s targeting, responsibilities include—
•
Assisting the G-2 or S-2 with the electromagnetic attack and the electromagnetic support portions
of intelligence preparation of the operational environment—identifying threat spectrum-dependent
capabilities and targets.
•
Briefing the electromagnetic attack plan.
•
Recommending electromagnetic attack methods of target engagement and assisting the targeting
working group in determining electromagnetic attack requirements against identified targets.
•
Ensuring electromagnetic attack can create the desired effects.
•
Planning and coordinating taskings and requests to satisfy electromagnetic attack and
electromagnetic support requirements.
•
Coordinating with SIGINT elements through the collection manager to satisfy electromagnetic
support collection requirements (in the information collection working group).
•
Recommending to the G-3 or S-3 and chief of fires, or fire support coordinator whether to engage a
target with electromagnetic attack.
•
Expediting joint spectrum interference resolution reports to the targeting working group and
targeting coordination board.

## TARGETING COORDINATION BOARD

B-83. The targeting coordination board is a formal meeting to gain the commander’s decision on the
recommendations of the targeting working group. The targeting coordination board follows the same agenda
as the targeting working group and is chaired by the commander or designated representative. The targeting
coordination board should be nested with the higher headquarters’ targeting.
B-84. At the completion of the targeting coordination board, the approved targeting products are
disseminated to subordinate units. The targeting products include—
•
The approved high-payoff target list, attack guidance matrix, and target selection standards. These,
with data from the information collection plan, may be combined into a unit-specific targeting
synchronization matrix.
•
Approved information collection plan. The G-2 or S-2 reorients acquisition assets and updates and
disseminates the information collection plan.
•
Tasks to subordinate units and assets. The G-3 or S-3 should prepare and issue a fragmentary order
to subordinate elements to execute the planned attack and assessment of targets developed by the
targeting working group and targeting coordination board.

## PROTECTION WORKING GROUP

B-85. The chief of protection at echelons division and above leads the protection working group. At the
brigade, the S-3 leads the protection working group. The protection working group uses information derived
from the commander’s guidance, intelligence preparation of the operational environment, targeting, risk
management, warning orders, the critical and defended asset lists, and mission analysis to identify critical
assets. The protection working group determines and prioritizes critical assets for protection. The CEWO
provides—
•
Input to the critical asset list and defended asset list.
•
Subject matter expertise in threat cyberspace and EW capabilities.
•
Subject matter expertise on cyberspace defense and electromagnetic protection measures and
capabilities.
B-86. A critical output of the protection working group is an updated protection running estimate. Table
B-10 on page 137 shows example protection working group activities. Refer to ADP 3-37 for more
information about the protection working group.

## ASSESSMENT WORKING GROUP

B-87. A key aspect of any operation is determining progress toward completing tasks, achieving objectives,
and obtaining a desired end state. Commanders establish an assessment working group to assist them in
identifying changes in an operational environment and determining whether actions are achieving objectives.
While assessment occurs throughout the headquarters, including assessing current operations and conducting
battle damage assessment, the assessment working group’s focus is toward the mid- to long-range planning
horizons. Products from the assessment working group serve as inputs to boards, other working groups, and
planning teams as well as collated, synthesized, and refined information into a collective staff assessment for
the commander (FM 6-0). The CEMA chief or CEWO is not a permanent member of the assessment working
group but may participate as required.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**137**

## This page intentionally left blank.

### Appendix C

## Effects Requests

This appendix discusses how Army units request external cyberspace operations and
electromagnetic warfare support from higher echelon Army or joint forces. Army forces may
require offensive cyberspace operations support for identified targets that require engagement
using an offensive cyberspace capability. Defensive cyberspace operations support may be
necessary when an identified threat in friendly cyberspace is beyond the defensive capabilities
of local network defenders. Electromagnetic warfare support may be required when an Army
unit needs augmentation, when their electromagnetic warfare capabilities or authorities cannot
fulfill their mission requirements, or when the required support must be delivered by airborne
electromagnetic warfare platforms.

### SECTION I – ELECTROMAGNETIC ATTACK

C-1. Typically, Army units at echelons division and brigade combat team have the organic capabilities to
conduct EW within their assigned areas. The joint force commander typically delegates electromagnetic
attack control authority to subordinate commanders to conduct EW missions within their assigned areas.
Commanders must ensure EW has been integrated and synchronized across the staff and according to the
higher commander’s attack guidance. Refer to ATP 3-12.3 for more information about electromagnetic attack
control authority.
C-2. Units may require an EW capability not organically available or additional EW beyond their organic
capabilities to meet requirements identified during targeting. The commander and the CEWO may request
augmentation through a higher, adjacent, or lower headquarters to meet requirements. This section describes
procedures for requesting airborne electromagnetic support through the DD Form 1972 (Joint Tactical
Airstrike Request). It also discusses the use of the five-line electromagnetic attack request for immediate
airborne electromagnetic attack support.

## JOINT TACTICAL AIRSTRIKE REQUEST

C-3. In a joint environment, Army units can request an airborne electromagnetic attack or electromagnetic
support. Airborne electromagnetic attack capabilities can prosecute both deliberate and dynamic targets. For
deliberate targeting, the requesting unit submits the DD Form 1972, accompanied by an airborne
electromagnetic attack or electromagnetic support request tool. Each of these requests has unique information
requirements and request flows. The requesting unit’s fire support element adds the target to its target
nomination list. The target is also added to the joint integrated prioritized target list at the joint task force
headquarters and forwarded to the joint force air component command with its assigned DD Form 1972
number (refer to ATP 3-09.32). Once the joint force air component command approves the Joint Tactical Air
Strike Request, the request is forwarded to the air operations center to execute the attack.
C-4. Table C-1 on page 140 is an example of the request tool that accompanies the DD Form 1972 to request
airborne electromagnetic attack or electromagnetic support capabilities.

## ELECTROMAGNETIC ATTACK REQUEST FORMAT

C-6. Dynamic targeting is used to prosecute targets of opportunity, including both unscheduled targets and
unanticipated targets. Immediate airborne electromagnetic attack may be required, for example, when a
maneuver plan needs enemy communications or sensors jammed before engagement. A unit can request
immediate support using the five-line electromagnetic attack request.
C-7. Electromagnetic attack requests fall within three operational categories: preplanned, preplanned on-
call, and immediate. The CEWO enters requests for electromagnetic attack effects through the targeting
working group using the electromagnetic attack request format (see table C-1 on page 140). Requests for
preplanned electromagnetic attack support are similar to requests for close air support. Requests for
electromagnetic attack effects use DD Form 1972 (see paragraph C-3) with detailed electromagnetic attack
effects requests attached in an electromagnetic attack request format.
C-8. The joint task force headquarters, the joint force land component command, the joint force air
component command, and the air operations center must collaboratively plan airborne attack before an
operation. This planning and coordination provide the joint force air component command the necessary time
to identify and prepare an EW squadron that will remain on standby throughout the mission or operation.
Figure C-2 depicts an example electromagnetic attack request format.
**Requesting command:**
**Requesting Unit:**
**Contact Information:** This person will be responsible for verifying the approved request
before the mission starts and relaying the information to the executing unit.
**Joint Tactical Air Strike Request Number:** Enter the joint tactical air strike request
number for submission with the electromagnetic attack request format.
**Concept of Operations:** Describe the concept of operations. This will include the
objective, forces used, timeline of the mission, and coordination efforts required for
mission success. Relate the impact of mission success to objectives for the integrated
tasking order.
**Electromagnetic Attack Concept of Operations:** Define the desired effect(s) and
timeline.
**CEASE BUZZER Procedures:** This will be in accordance with theater special
instructions. Provide frequency to communicate between the electromagnetic attack
control authority and the electromagnetic attack asset. VHF and UHF are the primary
means to talk to a supporting aircraft. If unable to establish communications, consider
using another asset to relay information. Some aircraft may be internet relay chat capable.
**Friendly Frequency Use for Operation:**
Target requested—list the type and frequency, if known.
Target Communications
System(s) to be Jammed or
Denied:
Intelligence assessment (required—do not copy and paste
frequencies from one day to the next without intelligence
validation assessment).
Target location in latitude and longitude or military grid
reference system.
**Jamming Date-Time Group(s):** From and to, in Zulu time (preferred).
**Type of Electromagnetic Attack Requested:** Preplanned and scheduled; on-call.
**Legend:**

attack brief (figure C-3 on page 143) for electromagnetic attack support when pre-planning is not possible

### SECTION II – CYBERSPACE EFFECTS

## REQUESTING OFFENSIVE CYBERSPACE OPERATIONS SUPPORT

C-10. For offensive cyberspace operations missions, the CEMA working group nominates high-payoff
targets that have met the commanders’ targeting guidance in the targeting working group. Once approved,
the targeting working group adds these targets to the unit’s target selection standards, targeting
synchronization matrix, and attack guidance matrix. The CEMA working group will also make
recommendations to the no-strike list and restricted target list that support the commanders targeting
guidance.
C-11. Combat mission teams provide offensive cyberspace capabilities to units at echelons corps and below
through the request for service process. Upon the commander’s approval, the CEMA section submits a CERF,
along with the high-payoff target list, no-strike list, and restricted target list, to their higher headquarters as
nominations for the joint targeting cycle and joint no-strike list or restricted target list.
C-12. The joint task force headquarters or combatant command uses the information from the CERF to
generate a USCYBERCOM request for service. Once the joint task force or combatant command
headquarters approves cyberspace targets identified in the corps’ high-payoff target list, they will add them
to the joint targeting cycle. The joint task force or combatant command then continues the request for service
process to request offensive cyberspace support from Joint Force Headquarters-Cyber (see figure
C-4 on page 144).

**FM 3-12**

## REQUESTING DEFENSIVE CYBERSPACE OPERATIONS SUPPORT

C-13. Defensive cyberspace operations support may be required to counter threat malicious cyberspace
activities that are beyond the capabilities of local network defenders and cybersecurity personnel to mitigate.
Units at echelons corps and below have limited organic capabilities to perform defensive cyberspace
operations tasks. Units request defensive cyberspace operations support as a reactive measure to defend
against malicious cyberspace activity through the CERF and request for service. The G-6 or S-6 coordinates
with the G-3 or S-3 to develop a CERF to request defensive cyberspace operations support.
C-14. Upon the commander’s approval, the CERF is submitted, validated, and routed through higher
headquarters, until it reaches the joint task force or combatant command, where it is converted to a request
for service. In parallel, the requesting unit notifies a cybersecurity service provider (Defense Information
Systems Agency or NETCOM) of the malicious cyberspace activity.
C-15. The joint task force headquarters or combatant command routes the request for service; the
cybersecurity service provider also notifies DOD Cyber Defense Command of the identified malicious
cyberspace activity. The combatant command has assigned cyber protection teams to defend friendly
cyberspace. The combatant commander has the authority to conduct defensive cyberspace operations
missions within their assigned area of responsibility without requesting authorization from USCYBERCOM.
C-16. The cyber protection teams’ primary focus is conducting cyberspace defense throughout the area of
responsibility and may be unavailable to provide support to requesting units at echelons corps and below. If
**144**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Effects Requests**
the cyber protection teams are unavailable, the combatant command joint cyberspace center forwards the
request for service through the cyberspace operations-integrated planning element to DOD Cyber Defense
Command through the USCYBERCOM portal. The cyberspace operations-integrated planning element
provides USCYBERCOM situational understanding of all defensive cyberspace operations missions
conducted in a combatant commander’s area of responsibility.
C-17. When local network defenders become aware of malicious activity on the network, they forward the
information to DOD Cyber Defense Command. DOD Cyber Defense Command uses the notice from the
local network defenders to begin the process of employing a cyber protection team (either joint or Service-
retained) before receiving the request for service through the routing channels. However, the requesting unit
is still responsible for both initiating the request for service process and informing a cybersecurity service
provider.
C-18. DOD Cyber Defense Command has directive authority for cyberspace operations to conduct defensive
cyberspace operations globally within the DODIN. DOD Cyber Defense Command provides
USCYBERCOM situational understanding information for all defensive cyberspace operations missions.
C-19. DOD Cyber Defense Command exercises tactical control of all Service cyberspace component
commands for DODIN operations and defensive cyberspace operations. DOD Cyber Defense Command has
assigned cyber protection teams that conduct defensive cyberspace operations; however, their primary focus
is generally monitoring and implementing proactive internal defensive measures to mitigate malicious
cyberspace activity on the DODIN globally.
C-20. Initiating cyberspace defense to respond to malicious cyberspace activity uses a top-down approach.
Proactive cyberspace defense occurs when DOD Cyber Defense Command identifies malicious activity on
the DODIN before receiving a request for service or being informed by a cybersecurity service provider and
proactively deploys a cyber protection team to counter malicious cyberspace activity.
C-21. If the DOD Cyber Defense Command cyber protection teams are unavailable to satisfy the request for
service, DOD Cyber Defense Command oversees a joint approval process to task one of the Service
cyberspace component commands to provide a cyber protection team to conduct cyberspace defense to
support the unit’s request. The assigned cyber protection team may perform defensive tasks remotely or co-
locate with the joint task force headquarters or requesting unit. When co-located with the unit, the cyber
protection team remains under the command of its parent unit. Figure C-5 on page 146 illustrates the routing
chain for defensive cyberspace operations support requests for echelons corps and below.

## CYBER EFFECTS REQUEST FORMAT

C-22. Corps and below Army units use the CERF to request cyberspace operations support. Support in
response to a CERF may come from joint cyberspace forces such as the combat mission teams or from joint
or other Service capabilities.
C-23. During the operations process at echelons corps and below, the commander and staff identify the
effects desired in and through cyberspace to support operations against specified targets. If the requesting
and higher echelons determine their current capabilities are insufficient, the commander and staff validate
and process the CERF. The routing process continues through each echelon until the CERF reaches the joint
task force or combatant command, where it is converted to a USCYBERCOM request for service.

### APPROVALPROCESS

C-24. The CERF approval process at echelons corps and below follows the below steps:
•
Identify targets for cyberspace effects.
•
Determine whether organic cyberspace capabilities can create desired effects.
•
Validate target for cyberspace effects.
•
Forward to the next higher Army echelon for deconfliction and synchronization.
**146**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Effects Requests**
•
Determine whether other organic capabilities can create desired effects if organic cyberspace
capabilities do not exist.
•
If current capabilities can fulfill the requirement, synchronize operations.
•
If current capabilities cannot fulfill the requirement, approve target for cyberspace effects.
•
Forward to next higher Army echelon for approval until the CERF enters the joint process as a
USCYBERCOM request for service.
•
Synchronize the operation with the requested cyberspace effect (if possible).

### PREPARATION

C-25. Although the requesting unit may not have detailed target network topology information, it should
provide as much current target information as available. The approval process for cyberspace effects may
take longer than other fires capabilities. Each of the three sections in the CERF are described below in bullet
form. The requesting unit provides all information from the bullet lists below to their higher headquarters
through the CEMA working group or other designated targeting processes.
**Section 1 – Requesting Unit Information**
C-26. The requesting unit provides the following identifying information in section 1:
•

prioritize the CERF. For Army units at echelons corps and below, this entry will usually be the
combatant command.
•

headquarters.
•
**TIME SENT.** Enter the time the requesting united submitted the CERF to higher headquarters.
•
**REQUESTING UNIT.** Enter the name of the requesting unit.
•
**BY.** Enter the rank, last, and first name of the requesting unit’s point of contact that time-stamped
and processed the CERF.
•
**CLASSIFICATION.** Enter the overall classification of the document. Ensure classification
markings are applied to each section and all supporting documents.
•
**POINT OF CONTACT.** Enter the rank, last, and first name of the requesting unit’s point of
contact. Also, enter the phone number and e-mail address.
•
**REQUEST FOR SERVICE NUMBER.** Leave blank. The request for service number is assigned
by the joint task force or combatant command headquarters when the CERF is converted to a request
for service for forwarding to USCYBERCOM.
**Section 2 – Supported Operation Information**

operations the requested effect will support.

requested effect will directly support. Describe the tactical objectives and tasks the requested effect
will directly or indirectly support. Describe the supported objective or objectives the requested effect
will directly support.

(or requesting unit if they are the same).
