**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Training**
•
Camouflage net masking.
•
EMCON—
▪
Limiting electromagnetic emissions.
▪
Exercising EMCON plans, including triggers and transitions between EMCON conditions.

## COMBAT TRAINING CENTERS

G-11. Combat training center rotations simulate real-world operations by imitating conditions units may
encounter during deployment. The combat training center opposing force imposes realistic threat tactics,
techniques, and procedures on units. Combat training centers evaluate rotational training units for combat
readiness determined by the proactive and reactive measures taken to prevent or mitigate obstacles presented
during the training rotation.
G-12. In a combined arms training scenario, cyberspace and EW operations are essential to gaining and
maintaining tactical advantages needed for a favorable resolution. Combat training centers continuously
observe the rotational training unit’s performance in an operational environment that includes simulated
conflict and competition. Combat training centers help commanders assess their units’ overall combat
readiness; proficiency in mission-essential tasks; and those critical and essential tasks that require additional
training. Commanders oversee the implementation of necessary changes during home-station training to
improve their units’ proficiency.

## This page intentionally left blank.

### Appendix H

## Network Hardening Guide

The network hardening guide organizes procedures around four distinct but interrelated areas:
domain management and security; network device management and security; network traffic
monitoring and alerts; and endpoint (host and server) security. This guide should provide a unit
the necessary tools to conduct a risk-based assessment of its cybersecurity posture.

### SECTION I – DOMAIN MANAGEMENT AND SECURITY

## OVERVIEW

H-1. Configuring a hardened tactical network requires both time and technical expertise to apply
cybersecurity measures using a variety of tools and security devices. There is no formal template for the
implementation and configuration of these tools and devices. The guide in this appendix provides units to
help establish their cybersecurity baseline.

## CREATE AND DESIGN ORGANIZATIONAL UNIT

H-2. ‘Organizational unit’ is a construct used to represent an organization whose resources are logically
separate from resources of other, similar organizations. Network administrators use organizational units to
control access to system resources and segregate data according to sensitivity and access rights. Creating and
designing an organizational unit assists administrators to logically group users, computers, and other objects.
This enables the targeted application of cybersecurity policies and access controls to specific groups based
on function or department and minimizes the risk of unauthorized access and simplifies security management.
H-3. A brigade combat team should consist of one domain assigned by the Defense Information Systems
Agency. Domain administrators should reside in the brigade combat team S-6. Battalion S-6s should not be
given domain administrator credentials to prevent them inadvertently erasing the brigade domain.
H-4. Each battalion in the brigade combat team should be a subordinate organizational unit in the brigade
domain. System administrators in the battalion S-6 should have delegated control over their respective
subordinate organizational units. Within each organizational unit, there should be three standardized primary
subordinate organizational units:
•
Users—every user assigned to the battalion organizational unit.
•
Computers—every workstation that belongs to the organizational unit.
•
Groups (otherwise known as security groups)—provide a mechanism to create sub-organizations
within the organizational unit to provide access to resources on a least-privilege basis.
H-5. Users, besides existing within the ‘users’ organizational unit will be nested within appropriate groups
within the ‘groups’ organizational unit. System administrators should be designated in a security group to
grant them the appropriate elevated permissions separate from other users. The administrator steps to design
and create an organizational unit are—
•
Assign users.
•
Assign computers.
•
Join computers to the domain.
•
Create security groups and assign users.
•
Delegate control.

## SECURITY ACCESS

H-6. Security access manages and controls who can access sensitive systems, networks, and data. This
ensures only authorized users can access network resources while preventing unauthorized users from
gaining entry to devices or the network. Identity, credential, and access management helps protect against
cyberspace risks such as data breaches and unauthorized access. Administrators should grant system access
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**187**
**Appendix H**
based on least privilege through role-based authentication. In establishing security access controls,
administrators configure—
•
Security group access to folders.
•
Security group access to printers.
•
File enumeration policy.
**Note****.** To enforce least privilege access, standard users should only be given ‘read,’ ‘read and
execute,’ and ‘list folder contents’ permissions. ‘Read and execute’ permissions allow users to
create, modify, and save documents. These users can only delete files they created.

### SECURITYGROUPACCESS TO AFOLDER

H-7. Administrators should be given ‘modify’ permissions. Only the domain administrator should be
granted ‘full control’ permissions. Full control allows a user to delete folders and all their contents. Deleted
folders are unrecoverable.

### SECURITYGROUPACCESS TO APRINTER

H-8. To access a networked printer, users need to be part of a security group with assigned print permissions.
Security group access to a printer allows the administrator to control which users or devices on a network
can access the shared printer. This prevents unauthorized individuals from printing sensitive documents and
mitigates potential data leaks commonly associated with open printer access.

### FILEENUMERATIONPOLICY

H-9. File enumeration limits access to files and folders based on a user’s role and need to know. A file
enumeration property ensures that users can only see files and folders to which they have been granted
security permission.

## FILE SERVER RESOURCE MANAGER

H-10. The File Server Resource Manager is a set of tools in Windows Server that help administrators manage
stored data on file servers. The File Server Resource Manager allows administrators to manage file storage
quotas, file screens (the types of files a user can store), and storage reports (to identify trends and misuse of
space). This allows administrators to automatically manage access to sensitive files, effectively limiting
vulnerabilities related to data storage and unauthorized access. File Server Resource Manager elements
include—
•
Quota management.
•
File screening.
•
Storage reports.

### QUOTAMANAGEMENT

H-11. Recommended storage allocation for staff sections depends on the level of usage anticipated for each
section and the total file storage space available. Quota management allows administrators to limit the amount
of server storage space for users. Examples include hard limit, template-based, auto apply, and size limit
quotas.

### FILESCREENING

H-12. In custom properties, the administrator can select either active or passive file screening. Active
screening blocks access and passive screening logs the access. Administrators can also configure notification
thresholds and properties similar to quota management.

### STORAGEREPORTS

H-13. Storage reports graph how much space is in use in each folder relative to its capacity and storage trends
over time. These reports provide comprehensive views of disk space consumption across different folders
and users on a file server. This can highlight potential storage issues as users approach their quota limits.
**188**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Network Hardening Guide**

## DISTRIBUTED FILE SYSTEM (SHARED DRIVES)

H-14. A distributed file system allows users to access files from multiple servers or locations. Distributed file
systems store and manage data across the network. This distributes data across multiple servers, increasing
redundancy when faced with server failure, and prevents unauthorized access and data loss from a single
point of failure through encryption, access control, and data replication. It also allows users to share and work
from the same version of a file.

## PRINT SERVERS

H-15. Print servers act as security gateways to help prevent unauthorized access. They allow administrators
to centrally manage printers for the domain and limit unauthorized access to the network via a printer. Print
servers centrally manage printer access and allow administrators to implement user authentication,
encryption, and print job tracking.

## GROUP POLICY OBJECTS

H-16. A group policy object is a collection of policy settings. Group policy objects restrict actions a user can
perform on a workstation and the actions a workstation can perform on a domain. Establishing group policy
objects allows administrators to centrally enforce cybersecurity policies across all users and machines within
a domain, effectively preventing unauthorized access and maintaining consistent security configurations.
H-17. A best practice is to create one group policy object per rule to be implemented. It is generally
inadvisable to establish one group policy object to apply all rules because of undue complexity and single
points of failure. Domain administrators should apply these group policy objects to the network:
•
Disable wireless access.
•
Disable command prompt.
•
Disable power shell.

### DISABLEWIRELESSACCESS

H-18. Disabling wireless access prevents unauthorized users from connecting to Wi-Fi access points and
restricted networks. Disabling access helps mitigate risks of data breaches and unauthorized access to
sensitive information.

### DISABLECOMMANDPROMPT

H-19. Disabling the command prompt prevents users from executing system commands. This helps eliminate
potential compromises in security and allows only authorized administrators to access advanced system
functions.

### DISABLEPOWERSHELL

H-20. Disabling the PowerShell function enhances security by limiting access to tools that can be exploited
by malicious actors. This is important for users who do not require advanced system administration
capabilities and allows the administrator to implement least privilege access.
**Note****.** Administrators should be in a separate organizational unit to allow them to execute
PowerShell scripts. This organizational unit will only work for PowerShell version 5 or higher.

## FOLDER REDIRECTION

H-21. Folder redirection automatically routes standard folders to a different storage location. Folder
redirection allows data created by key users to be saved to a server so they can access their data on any
workstation within the domain. This centralizes user data on a network server, allowing for stricter access
control, easier backups, and reduced risk of data loss. The domain administrator should establish folder
redirection for identified key personnel in the brigade.
•
Commander.
•
Command sergeant major.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**189**
**Appendix H**
•
Executive officer.
•
S-3.
•
Battalion commanders.

## READ-ONLY DOMAIN CONTROLLER

H-22. A read-only domain controller hosts a copy of the Active Directory database without the ability to add
or change entries. A read-only domain controller serves locations that cannot adequately secure a full domain
controller. The read-only domain controller reduces the attack surface and mitigates the risk of unauthorized
changes to user accounts or critical system settings.

### DEPLOYMENT

H-23. Read-only domain controllers are generally deployed for the following reasons:
•
Physical security is not guaranteed in an austere or remote environment. If a malicious actor
manages to access a read-only domain controller, they cannot make database changes. If they
somehow change the database, they cannot compromise Active Directory because those changes do
not replicate to writable domain controllers.
•
Most battalion S-6 sections lack sufficient personnel with the required information technology
certifications to maintain writable domain controllers. Since a read-only domain controller only
provides user authentication, it does not have the same ongoing maintenance requirements (such as
hardware updates, site-link changes, and user credential changes) as a writable domain controller.
•
Some remote units have poor network connectivity or low bandwidth to the brigade network
infrastructure. In this instance, a read-only domain controller is deployed so the remote users need
not authenticate themselves from the writable domain controller over the wide-area network. This
reduces the time required for remote users to log on.

### ALLOWED ANDDENIEDPASSWORDREPLICATIONGROUPS

H-24. A read-only domain controller communicates with a writable domain controller for user authentication
because by default, account credentials are not cached locally on a replication group. If branch office users
from the replication group log in, they are authenticated locally if their credentials are already cached. For
security purposes, to prevent privileged users (domain and enterprise administrators) from being cached
locally on a read-only domain controller, they are normally added to a denied password replication group.

### READ-ONLYDOMAINCONTROLLERPREREQUISITES

H-25. Before installing and configuring a read-only domain controller, the domain administrator should
ensure—
•
The administrator account has a strong password.
•
Static Internet Protocol is configured.
•
The latest Microsoft updates are installed.
•
Firewall is turned off.
•
Domain Name System server Internet Protocol address properties are correct and point to a writable
domain controller. To verify, the administrator resolves the domain name from command prompt.

### INSTALLATION ANDCONFIGURATION

H-26. This process enhances network security in remote locations where physical security is considered
weak. Installing and configuring the read-only domain controller helps to prevent unauthorized users from
modifying sensitive Active Directory data by only allowing read access to the domain information.

### CONFIGURATIONTESTING

H-27. Once the domain administrator configures and reboots the read-only domain controller, they should
test and verify the configuration. The administrator should verify—
•
Connection to the read-only domain controller and not a writable domain controller.
•
They cannot create or delete accounts.
•
Related grayed out (non-selectable) icons.
**190**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Network Hardening Guide**
•
Allowed and denied password replication groups.

## TEST ENVIRONMENT FOR GROUP POLICY OBJECTS AND

## SECURITY CHANGES

H-28. A test environment allows administrators to change group policy objects and security settings without
affecting the active security domain. This allows administrators to safely evaluate the impact of planned
configuration changes offline before deploying them on the network. By using a test environment,
administrators can minimize disruptions or security vulnerabilities when deploying updates across the
network.
H-29. After the new Active Directory domain is established, administrators should create an object in the test
environment to evaluate the effects of the planned changes. When an object is added to the appropriate
organizational unit, the administrator should apply security controls only to the organization to ensure test
changes do not propagate across the entire domain.

## LOCAL CHANGE CONTROL BOARD

H-30. A local change control board reviews and approves proposed changes to the network. A local change
control board helps the S-6 section manage and control changes to balance network functionality and security.
Well-documented changes assist in troubleshooting network problems when they arise. The local change
control board ensures proposed changes are carefully reviewed, assessed, and approved before
implementation. The local change control board mitigates the risk of unauthorized modifications that could
compromise network security.

### SECTION II – NETWORK DEVICE MANAGEMENT AND SECURITY

## SOLAR WINDS NETWORK CONFIGURATION MANAGER

H-31. SolarWinds provides information technology infrastructure management software and services.
SolarWinds helps organizations plan, manage, and optimize cloud environments. SolarWinds helps identify
potential security breaches, investigate suspicious activity, and mitigate threats. SolarWinds includes a suite
of software tools that enable—
•
Continuous monitoring of network activity.
•
Log collection.
•
Vulnerability analysis.
•
Automated response.
H-32. SolarWinds Network Configuration Manager can be configured either through auto-discovery or
manually. This streamlines network device configuration management and helps information technology
teams track, compare, and modify configurations across various network devices.

### AUTO-DISCOVERYMETHOD

H-33. Auto-discovery maps the network infrastructure across different environments automatically. Auto-
discovery—
•
Saves time by eliminating the need to manually enter device configurations.
•
Provides a comprehensive view of the network topology.
•
Enables proactive monitoring of devices and potential issues.
H-34. Disadvantages of auto-discovery include—
•
Potential inaccuracies in discovered data.
•
Network impact from scanning.
•
Less granular control over monitored devices.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**191**
**Appendix H**

### MANUALCONFIGURATION

H-35. While SolarWinds is primarily designed for automatic network configuration, administrators may
choose to configure devices manually. This is beneficial when quick, specific changes are required or when
dealing with unique device configurations not supported by auto-discovery. Conversely, manual
configuration can be time-consuming, is susceptible to data input errors, and suffers from inconsistency
across the network, which may result in security vulnerabilities or operational inefficiencies.

## UNIFIED THREAT MANAGEMENT

H-36. Unified Threat Management combines multiple network security features into a single device. This
consolidates security features such as firewalls, antivirus, content filtering, and intrusion detection. By
consolidating these features, Unified Threat Management allows centralized management and monitoring
and simplifies comprehensive network protection. There are four methods to configure Unified Threat
Management:
•
Add from network (auto-discovery).
•
Add new device (manual).
•
Load from comma-separated values file.
•
Load from a text configuration file.

### ADD FROMNETWORK(AUTO-DISCOVERY)

H-37. Auto-discovery will only detect routers using Cisco Internetwork Operating System up to version 12.3.
This method is typically used when the administrator wants to automatically identify and configure network
devices within the target network, streamlining the setup process by detecting available devices and their
settings. Auto-discovery can lead to over-detection of devices and network traffic, which may result in
unnecessary alerts and degraded network performance.

### ADDNEWDEVICE(MANUAL)

H-38. Manual addition works regardless of whether the device is active on the network. Manually adding
devices may be required for highly specialized configurations, legacy systems integration, or when faced
with cost constraints. Drawbacks to using this method can include increased complexity, inefficient
monitoring, and the possibility of misconfiguration.

### ADDDEVICE FROMCOMMA-SEPARATEDVALUESFILE

H-39. Add from file adds devices to the network security system by importing the details from a comma-
separated values file. Adding a device from a file helps streamline configuration by bulk importing device
settings. This method is popular because the data is human-readable. Some disadvantages include the creation
of a single point of failure, less than granular settings, and overreliance on a single service provider.

### ADDDEVICE FROMCONFIGURATIONFILE

H-40. Add from configuration file adds devices to the network security system by importing the details from
a text file. Adding a device from configuration file streamlines configuration of multiple devices by bulk
importing device settings. This method can work with most Windows machines, typically using the Microsoft
Notepad application. Disadvantages include creating a possible single point of failure, less granular settings,
and overreliance on a single service provider.

## CURRENT DEVICE CONFIGURATIONS

H-41. Administrators should load the current configurations of network devices in Unified Threat
Management. This establishes the baseline configuration of Internet Protocol addresses, subnet masks,
default gateways, Domain Name System servers, and other configuration settings. Documenting current
device configurations ensures all devices—
•
Follow the latest security protocols.
•
Have updated firmware installed.
•
Have unnecessary or unauthorized services disabled.
**192**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Network Hardening Guide**

## CONFIGURE LAYER 2 SECURITY FEATURES

H-42. Layer 2 security features control which devices can access the network. This provides a critical layer
of protection against network attacks. Layer 2 security features help control access to the network switch
level and prevent unauthorized devices from connecting to the protected network. This helps isolate network
segments and limit the impact of breaches. Layer 2 security features include—
•
Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol helper address.
•
Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol snooping.
•
Dynamic Address Resolution Protocol inspection for Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol-
enabled networks.
•
Dynamic Address Resolution Protocol inspection for non-Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol
networks.
•
Port security features.

### DYNAMICHOSTCONFIGURATIONPROTOCOLHELPERADDRESS

H-43. Administrators should configure a Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol helper address on each
network switch to let those devices know where to find the trusted Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol
server. The helper address can be configured either through the SolarWinds Network Configuration Manager
or individually on each device.

### DYNAMICHOSTCONFIGURATIONPROTOCOLSNOOPING

H-44. This process will eliminate the possibility of having a rogue Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol
server assigning Internet Protocol addresses to clients on the network. The switch will only trust packets
coming from an authorized source (tier 2 router or switch).

### DYNAMICADDRESSRESOLUTIONPROTOCOLINSPECTION FORDYNAMICHOST

### CONFIGURATIONPROTOCOLENABLEDNETWORKS

H-45. Dynamic Address Resolution Protocol Inspection (DAI) is a layer 2 security feature that prevents
Address Resolution Protocol spoofing or cache poisoning attacks. DAI relies on Dynamic Host Configuration
Protocol snooping to build a database of trusted Internet Protocol and media access control address pairs for
validation of Address Resolution Protocol requests.
H-46. Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol snooping must be configured before implementing DAI (see
paragraph H-47 for configuring DAI on networks that do not use Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol).
DAI is applied to specific virtual local area networks and relies on specifying trusted and untrusted interfaces.
With DAI enabled, interfaces are configured as untrusted (meaning that DAI will be applied) by default. To
ensure traffic can still flow, it is necessary to configure trunked interfaces (interfaces to higher switches) as
trusted interfaces. If an interface is trusted, no inspection will take place. Access ports associated with the
target virtual local area network should remain untrusted. This configuration implies that DAI should occur
only once at the lowest level switch a user connects through. It is important that all switches in the same
virtual local area network have DAI configured to minimize security gaps.

### DYNAMICADDRESSRESOLUTIONPROTOCOLINSPECTION FORNON-DYNAMICHOST

### CONFIGURATIONPROTOCOLNETWORKS

H-47. In the case of a network configured without Dynamic Host Configuration Protocol, it is still possible
to configure DAI using Address Resolution Protocol access control lists. Access control lists allow the
administrator to statically specify Internet Protocol and media access control address pairs that create the
trusted database to validate requests and connections.
H-48. If devices are already present on the network, the easiest method to generate an access control list is to
pull the Address Resolution Protocol cache on the tier 2 switch and filter entries by the desired virtual local
area network for implementation. This can be accomplished via a script or manually, depending on network
restrictions. If attempting to implement DAI before the network is active, Internet Protocol and media access
control address pairs should be designated from the unit’s Lightweight Directory Access Protocol Data
Interchange Format. Media access control addresses for each workstation should be recorded during imaging.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**193**
**Appendix H**

### PORTSECURITYFEATURES

H-49. A best practice for access ports is to implement the sticky media access control security feature with a
limit of three learnable media access control addresses—this allows for an Internet Protocol telephone
attached to a workstation. The port should be configured for shutdown upon violation. Additionally,
implementing Bridge Protocol Data Unit guard on access ports prevents connection of unauthorized switches
or extensions to the network.

## ADDITIONAL LAYER 2 SECURITY CONFIGURATIONS

H-50. Additional layer 2 security configurations control which devices can access the network. This protects
against network attacks by controlling access to network switches to prevent unauthorized device
connections. This helps isolate network segments and limits the impact of breaches.
H-51. Certain layer 2 security features should be pre-configured on network systems. Administrators should
verify these configurations to ensure they comply with best practices. Key layer 2 security configurations
include—
•
Type 7 passwords.
•
Simple Network Management Protocol strings.
•
Simple Network Management Protocol traps and poll times.
•
Policy-based routing.

### TYPE7 PASSWORDS

H-52. If Type 7 passwords are cryptologically broken, it is possible to enter any type 7 password hash into
various online sources and find the corresponding plain text value. At the beginning of router and switch
configuration, the administrator should check the properties of every username and password assigned to the
system.

### SIMPLENETWORKMANAGEMENTPROTOCOLSTRINGS

H-53. During equipment fielding, equipment is generally configured with Simple Network Management
Protocol login credentials with pre-formatted, easy to social-engineer passwords. Factory default
administrator passwords for many network appliances and systems are easily discoverable by a simple
Internet search. Administrators must change these passwords from the fielded default. Additionally, Simple
Network Management Protocol strings named ‘public’ and ‘private’ must be removed for all devices on the
network, including printers.
**Note****.** Cybersecurity rules require changing vendor default passwords on all network devices. Even
though program managers generally change passwords on routers and switches from the factory
defaults when fielding commercial off-the-shelf equipment, this rule still applies to the program
manager-applied default passwords.

### SIMPLENETWORKMANAGEMENTPROTOCOLTRAPS ANDPOLLTIMES

H-54. Simple Network Management Protocol traps specify the level of information sent to the viewer. As a
best practice, if the network can support the bandwidth (generally minimal), Simple Network Management
Protocol traps should be enabled on the network.

### POLICY-BASEDROUTING

H-55. Policy-based routing should be set by default on tactical communications systems. If it is not, the
administrator should contact the field service representative or program manager for the default settings.
Configuring policy-based routing is beyond the skill set of most brigade networking technicians.
**194**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Network Hardening Guide**

### SECTION III – NETWORK TRAFFIC MONITORING AND ALERTS

## NETFLOW TRAFFIC ANALYZER

H-56. NetFlow Traffic Analyzer provides a deeper understanding of network activity. It achieves this by
examining data gathered from various network devices. By collecting detailed information about network
traffic, NetFlow offers valuable insights into patterns and trends. NetFlow Traffic Analyzer helps
administrators identify unusual or unexpected traffic patterns, that might indicate malicious cyberspace
activity. This enables the detection and mitigation of security exploits, such as unauthorized access attempts
or data breaches. It also helps identify vulnerabilities within the network infrastructure, allowing for timely
remediation and strengthening overall security posture. NetFlow Traffic Analyzer uses Cisco’s NetFlow
Protocol to detect and record information about the data flowing through the network. This tool helps network
administrators understand how data moves within the network. By looking at the data map, administrators
can—
•
Identify problems: They can see unusual patterns or suspicious activity that might indicate a hacker
or a network issue.
•
Prevent security breaches: By identifying vulnerabilities, they can take steps to protect the network
from attacks.
•
Improve performance: Understanding traffic patterns helps them optimize the network for better
speed and efficiency.
H-57. Administrators can set up NetFlow Traffic Analyzer through either—
•
Auto-discovery—the tool can search and connect to network devices on its own.
•
Manual setup—administrators can manually input the information for each device.

### AUTO-DISCOVERY

H-58. Auto-discovery streamlines setup of network monitoring. This method automatically identifies and
integrates network devices. By eliminating the need to manually configure each device, auto-discovery
significantly reduces the time and effort required for implementation. This simplifies setup and enhances the
system’s scalability, allowing for easier management of expanding networks.
H-59. Despite its advantages, auto-discovery has certain limitations. There is a possibility of inaccurate data
collection due to device compatibility issues or misconfiguration. Initial setup for auto-discovery can be
complex, requiring careful configuration to ensure accurate device identification. Additionally, auto-
discovery may impact network performance due to the increased volume of data being processed. Finally,
there is a risk of false negatives, where legitimate traffic is mis-identified as suspicious, or false positives,
where malicious activity goes undetected. Administrators must carefully weigh these potential drawbacks
against the benefits of simplified setup and increased scalability. Auto-discovery allows the tool to
automatically find and connect to any device on the network that can send NetFlow data. Auto-discovery has
both advantages and drawbacks:
•
**Advantages—**
▪
Simplifies network setup—administrators do not have to manually connect each device, saving
time and effort.
▪
Fast and easy monitoring—auto-discovery makes it much quicker to start monitoring network
traffic.
▪
Better network view—auto-discovery can easily handle a large number of devices, giving a
clearer picture of the entire network.
•
**Drawbacks—**
▪
Potential for errors—the auto-discovery feature can misinterpret information, leading to
inaccurate data.
▪
Tricky initial setup—initially getting the auto-discovery feature working correctly place can be
complicated.
▪
Performance hiccups—the automatic search for devices might slow down the network during
discovery.
▪
False alarms—there is a chance of getting false alarms—the tool might flag something as a
problem when it is not, or it might miss a real issue.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**195**
**Appendix H**

### MANUAL

H-60. Manually configuring NetFlow Traffic Analyzer requires an administrator to individually set up each
network device. This setup involves instructing each device to transmit its NetFlow data to a designated
collector, which aggregates and analyzes the information.
H-61. Manual configuration offers some advantages. It provides administrators with granular control over
the data collection process. Additionally, isolating potential issues becomes easier since each device is
configured independently.
H-62. However, manual configuration also presents some drawbacks. It requires more resources in terms of
time and personnel. Configuring each device individually can be time-consuming, particularly in large
networks. Manual configuration offers administrators more control over data collection. This approach
requires individually configuring each network device to specify the transmission of NetFlow data to a
designated collector. The primary advantage of manual configuration lies in its granularity. Administrators
can meticulously define data points collected from each device, tailoring the monitoring system to their
unique requirements. This facilitates troubleshooting by isolating specific network segments or devices for
analysis.
H-63. However, the manual configuration method does come with certain drawbacks. It is labor-intensive to
implement, particularly in large and complex networks. The time required for manual configuration increases
proportionally with the number of devices, making it less scalable compared to auto-discovery.
**Note****.** When using the manual configuration, each device must be physically connected to the
network and powered on. Only then will the device appear within the NetFlow Traffic Analyzer
interface, allowing for configuration.

## CONFIGURING DEVICES TO SEND LOG ENTRIES AND ALERTS

H-64. Configuring network devices to transmit log entries and alerts to a central system logging protocol
(syslog) server is an important aspect of network management. This information can include log entries,
alerts, system messages, and various notifications. Centralizing this data offers several advantages:
•
Efficient troubleshooting—by reviewing the sequence of events recorded in logs, administrators can
swiftly identify the root cause of network problems.
•
Performance monitoring—analyzing device logs allows administrators to monitor performance
metrics and ensure optimal network health.
•
Compliance adherence—maintaining comprehensive logs is often essential for meeting
cybersecurity compliance requirements.
•
Centralized management—consolidating logs from multiple devices into a single location simplifies
network management tasks.
•
Proactive issue response—timely alerts notify administrators of critical issues requiring immediate
attention.
H-65. As a starting point, administrators should configure NetFlow on the outbound interfaces of tier 2
routers. This initial setup allows for an assessment of network activity and helps determine whether further
expansion is necessary. Domain controllers and client devices do not require this configuration.
Administrators need login credentials for Enterprise Security Manager to access and manage the collected
data effectively to accomplish this configuration.

## CONNECTORS IN ENTERPRISE SECURITY MANAGER

H-66.  Connectors are essential software components within Enterprise Security Manager that facilitate
communication between the platform and various devices within the unit’s portion of the network. These
connectors allow the domain manager to interact with and gather information from diverse devices and
applications. By establishing these connector configurations, organizations can leverage the full capabilities
of Enterprise Security Manager for proactive threat detection and response.
**196**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Network Hardening Guide**
H-67. Domain managers use connectors to collect device logs and alerts. These connectors establish secure
and authenticated communication channels, ensuring the confidentiality and integrity of data transmitted
between the devices and Enterprise Security Manager.
H-68. This secure communication enhances network security. By aggregating and analyzing logs and alerts
from various sources, Enterprise Security Manager can effectively detect and respond to potentially malicious
cyberspace activity in a coordinated manner. Domain managers should prioritize configuring connectors
for—
•
Routers and switches—these network infrastructure devices provide valuable insights into network
traffic patterns and potential anomalies.
•
Domain controllers and clients (syslog)—integrating domain controllers and client devices via
syslog connectors enables comprehensive monitoring of user activity and system events.

### ROUTERS ANDSWITCHES

H-69. Connectors on routers and switches facilitate secure collection and transmission of network traffic data
to the Enterprise Security Manager. This allows monitoring and analysis of network activity for security risks
by providing detailed visibility into network communications across different devices.

### DOMAINCONTROLLERS ANDCLIENTS(SYSLOG)

H-70. A key connector in many enterprise environments is the Active Directory connector. Active Directory
functions as a proxy service, facilitating communication between cloud-based applications and the unit’s
Active Directory domain controllers.
H-71. The Active Directory connector plays a vital role in user authentication and data access. When a user
attempts to access a cloud application integrated with this connector, the connector acts as an intermediary.
Instead of requiring a direct connection to the unit domain controller, the cloud application communicates
with the connector. The connector, in turn, securely queries the domain controllers to verify the user’s
credentials and retrieve necessary information.
H-72. This architecture enhances security by eliminating the need for direct connections between cloud
applications and sensitive unit systems. It also simplifies access management for cloud applications by
leveraging the existing user accounts and permissions stored in the unit’s Active Directory server.
**Note****.** Administrator credentials are required to retrieve syslog data from devices. The domain
manager should connect the device before finalizing settings to ensure Enterprise Security
Manager can communicate with the device.

## ENTERPRISE SECURITY MANAGER LOG FORWARDING

H-73. Enterprise Security Manager automatically sends security logs to a central location for analysis and
monitoring. This allows the organizational unit to centrally collect security logs. The domain manager
configures Enterprise Security Manager for log forwarding to the regional cyber center or higher-echelon
network operations and security center. Log forwarding enables—
•
Monitoring for malicious cyberspace activity
•
Analysis of security incidents and events.
•
A single point of visibility to monitor cybersecurity compliance.
**Note****.** Firewall modifications are generally required to forward Enterprise Security Manager logs
to the regional cyber center or higher-level command.

## ENTERPRISE SECURITY MANAGER ALERTS AND DASHBOARD

## CONFIGURATIONS

H-74. Enterprise Security Manager alerts and dashboard configurations allow for the monitoring of key
security areas such as—
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**197**
**Appendix H**
•
Network traffic.
•
User activity.
•
System events.
•
Malicious cyberspace activity.
H-75. Alerts and dashboard configurations allow security teams to visualize critical information, prioritize
alerts, and investigate possible malicious cyberspace activity. Alerts help determine patterns, activity, and
other suspicious behavior. Dashboards display real-time information for analysis and response. There are
five primary Enterprise Security Manager dashboards cybersecurity personnel should examine on a regular
basis.
•
Triggered alarms.
•
Asset threat summary.
•
Recent threat summary.
•
Vulnerability summary.
•
Event analysis dashboard.

### TRIGGEREDALARMS

H-76. The triggered alarms may indicate malicious cyberspace activity that poses a risk to local systems. For
example, a triggered alarm may indicate a denial-of-service attempt. When examining individual events, the
domain manager can create a case to enter notes and information about the event.

### ASSETTHREATSUMMARY

H-77. The asset threat summary view should normally be blank. If something appears, that item requires
immediate investigation and communication to the regional cyber center or higher echelon network
operations and security center.

### RECENTTHREATSUMMARY

H-78. The recent threat summary view should normally be blank. If something appears, that item requires
immediate investigation and communication to the regional cyber center or higher echelon network
operations and security center.

### VULNERABILITYSUMMARY

H-79. The vulnerability summary view displays a consolidated list of security alerts on the network to
identify vulnerabilities. The data is displayed on a dashboard with key metrics and insights to help prioritize
remediation efforts and improve the unit’s cybersecurity posture.

### EVENTANALYSISDASHBOARD

H-80. Domain managers can apply event filters to separate benign events versus malicious events. Domain
managers can apply event filters to parse out benign versus malicious events. The event analysis dashboard
provides a comprehensive view of security events within a system, highlighting both logged and blocked
activities. This dashboard offers a more detailed alternative to the triggered alarms dashboard. Domain
managers can apply event filters to differentiate between harmless and potentially harmful events. This
enables them to identify and prioritize security risks, facilitating timely investigation and response.

## SECURITY ONION

H-81. Security Onion is an open-source Linux distribution for intrusion detection, enterprise security
monitoring, and log management that is approved for use on Army Systems. Security Onion detects malicious
cyberspace activity before it can cause significant damage. It is built on a modified distributed client-server
model. Security Onion provides a comprehensive security monitoring platform that allows network security
teams to identify and investigate possible malicious cyberspace activity in real-time.

**FM 3-12**

**Network Hardening Guide**

### SECTION IV – ENDPOINT SECURITY

H-82. Host Based Security System secures individual network and end user devices. This allows network
managers to monitor and manage all devices on the network. Network administrators should pre-configure
Host Based Security System agents to a standard workstation image. Host Based Security System secures
individual network and end user This allows network managers to monitor and manage all devices on the
network. Network administrators should pre-configure Host Based Security System agents to a standard
workstation image.

## HOST BASED SECURITY SYSTEM DEPLOYMENT

H-83. If the Host Based Security System agent does not point to the correct ePolicy Orchestrator server, it
will not receive updates or communicate alerts. A misconfigured Host Based Security System agent requires
reconfiguring the client installations across the entire domain. Rule sets associated with Host Based Security
System are generally managed by echelons above brigade. For detailed instructions for deploying Host Based
Security System, refer to the cybersecurity tools section on the Cyber Lessons and Best Practices Website.

## HOST BASED SECURITY SYSTEM DASHBOARDS

H-84. Dashboards display real-time data and insights into the security status of individual network devices.
Dashboards help administrators identify possible malicious cyberspace activity and enable timely
intervention and remediation.
H-85. There are multiple views or dashboards that can be configured in Host Based Security System. With
the standard dashboards, administrators can create filters to display alerts for the most important information.
Administrators can also configure custom dashboards.
**Note****.** The effectiveness of a dashboard is dependent on properly configuring the host agents that
report to the ePolicy Orchestrator server.
H-86. In its default configuration, the threat events view displays possible malicious cyberspace activity—
instances that have triggered an alert or blocked an action. Typical filters that can be applied to this dashboard
include filtering by event generated time (for example, 1 day, 1 week, or 1 month), or event type. Building
filters should be done over time as traffic and alerts are identified as benign or malicious. Administrators
should configure alerts so that identified benign events no longer display.

## ASSURED COMPLIANCE ASSESSMENT SOLUTION DEPLOYMENT

## AND SCANNING

H-87. Assured Compliance Assessment Solution consists of two primary components—Security Center and
Nessus Scanners. These components allow an organizational unit to identify and assess security
vulnerabilities. Assured compliance measures provide detailed information about vulnerabilities, enabling
their prioritization and remediation. This improves the security posture and cybersecurity compliance of the
network.
H-88. Security Center resides on a virtual server in the network operations and security center. The Security
Center deployment in the network operations and security center also has a Nessus scanner. Nessus Scanners
are installed on virtual machines on every major networking assemblage at the battalion and brigade.

### INSTALLATION ANDCONFIGURATION

H-89. Administrators configure Assured Compliance Assessment Solution to scan systems for vulnerabilities
and assess the system for cybersecurity compliance. Installation and configuration involve—
•
Deploying Assured Compliance Assessment Solution software on a network.
•
Defining scanning targets.
•
Configuring compliance checks.
•
Establishing reporting mechanisms to monitor and remediate security issues.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**199**
**Appendix H**
**Installing Security Center and Nessus Scanners**
H-90. The administrator should download the latest system image from the Department of Defense Patch
Repository Website before installing. Installing both supports the Security Center which controls and
consolidates vulnerability scan data from multiple Nessus scanners across the network. This allows
centralized viewing, analysis, and prioritization of cybersecurity risks.
**Setting up Scan Zones and Nessus Scans**
H-91. Scan zones should align with the subnets allocated across the local domain. Administrators must assign
which scanner will cover each zone.

### BESTPRACTICES

H-92. When establishing a network baseline, daily scanning is likely necessary to achieve compliance. Once
an acceptable baseline is achieved, weekly Assured Compliance Assessment Solution scans may be
sufficient, subject to the network administrator’s battle rhythm and operating tempo.
**Plug-Ins**
H-93. Plug-ins are snippets of code that determine what is picked up in a scan. Administrators should
download updated plug-ins weekly. When uploading plug-ins to Security Center it is better to check for
updated .tar files rather than downloading all plug-ins. When pushing updates from Security Center in a
bandwidth-limited environment, it is generally a best practice to ensure automatic updates are activated on
only one scanner at a time and to update each subordinate scanner individually.
**Enabling and Disabling Automatic Updates**
H-94. Enabling automatic updates helps ensure compliance with cybersecurity standards and minimizes the
amount of time the network is exposed to a potential exploit. Automatic updates keep the system up to date
with the latest security patches and detection capabilities.
**Checking Compliance**
H-95.  The vulnerability summary checks cybersecurity compliance across multiple scans. It also allows
administrators to identify trends, such as an increase or decrease in the number of vulnerabilities identified
over time. Using Assured Compliance Assessment Solution, units can proactively assess their cybersecurity
posture against security technical implementation guides. These guides provide a framework of best practices
and security controls that organizations should implement to mitigate risks effectively.
**Exporting Vulnerability Lists**
H-96. Administrators can export vulnerability lists as .csv files to give consolidated lists and groups of
computers to subordinate echelons to employ multiple patches on a single restart. Firewalls must be adjusted
to allow scanning on port 8834 throughout the network. This allows Security Center to communicate with
Nessus Scanners.
H-97. Factory service representative-managed command and control information systems are problematic to
patch. Quarterly updates are often delayed, which may leave multiple unpatched vulnerabilities on the system
until the next update.
**Enforcing Subordinate Cybersecurity Compliance**
H-98. A useful technique to enforce compliance in subordinate units is calling them periodically to check
their progress and help them through any issues. This may seem obvious but is often overlooked.

## PATCH MANAGEMENT

H-99. Patch management encompasses the systematic process of updating software, drivers, and firmware
on network devices. This practice addresses security vulnerabilities, resolves software issues, adds new
**200**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Network Hardening Guide**
features, and ensures compliance with cybersecurity standards. Prioritizing patch management enhances
security, improves system performance, and reduces risk of cyber incidents.

### BESTPRACTICES

H-100. By adhering to patch management best practices, units can minimize vulnerabilities, strengthen
security, and ensure the continued integrity of their network infrastructure. Best practices for patch
management include—
•
**Source verification**—administrators must obtain patches and updates exclusively from trusted
sources. These include official vendor websites, the Defense Information Systems Agency, or
authorized distribution channels like the patch quarterly disc.
•
**Thorough testing**—before deploying patches or updates across the network, administrators must
conduct rigorous testing in a controlled environment. Testing should verify that the patch does not
introduce new issues or conflicts with existing software.
•
**Performance validation**—after successful testing, administrators should have a designated system
operator evaluate the performance of the patched system. This step ensures that the patch does not
negatively affect system functionality or stability.
•
**Baseline image update**—once a patch has been thoroughly tested and approved for deployment,
administrators should update the unit’s baseline image. This image serves as a template for
configuring new devices or restoring existing devices to a known secure state.
•
**Distribution strategy**—various tools and methods exist for automating and streamlining the patch
deployment process. Administrators should select a patch distribution solution that aligns with their
unit’s specific environment and requirements.

### PATCHMANAGEMENTSTEPS

H-101. The steps in patch management are—
•
Verifying Windows Server Update Service configuration.
•
Configuring the System Center Configuration Manager server
•
Setting up a test workstation.
**Verifying Windows Server Update Service Configuration**
H-102. The Windows Server Update Service server should point to the Defense Information Systems
Agency update service server. The Windows Server Update Service server should come pre-configured and
not require any adjustment.
**Note****.** Patches pulled by the Windows Server Update Service server are designed to be distributed
through the System Center Configuration Manager server and cannot be accessed manually.
**Configuring the System Center Configuration Manager Server**
H-103. The System Center Configuration Manager server is the preferred method to distribute patches
across a domain. Patch distribution should be pre-scheduled. In a limited bandwidth environment, units
should maintain an SOP for manually pulling and distributing system patches. A best practice is to conduct
system patching outside periods of peak activity.
**Setting Up a Test Workstation**
H-104. Network administrators should designate one workstation per enclave as a test environment to verify
new patches before deploying them across the network. This will allow network administrators to determine
whether additional configuration changes are needed to maintain network functionality. If additional
configuration changes are required, those instructions can be included as a part of the update to ensure patched
systems work properly.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**201**
**Appendix H**

### MANUALPATCHING IN ABANDWIDTH-LIMITEDENVIRONMENT

H-105. If network bandwidth is insufficient to support using Windows Server Update Service and System
Center Configuration Manager, network administrators will likely need to patch systems manually. This
involves downloading patches individually from the Defense Information Systems Agency patch repository
and copying those patches to a local shared drive for further distribution. This method requires administrators
to apply patches system by system. This method is slower and labor intensive, but if properly managed it can
be effective.

## WINDOWS DEPLOYMENT SERVICE CONFIGURATION

H-106. Windows Deployment Service allows administrators to centrally manage and deploy standardized
images to minimize the risk of rogue software installations. A Windows Deployment Service server allows
a unit to centrally manage its image repositories, capture updated system images, and distribute them to
subordinate units.

## PRINTER ACCESS CONTROL

H-107. Administrators should establish access controls on network shared printers. Printer access control
limits access to networked printers to a list of defined users. Implementing access control through password
or common access card login prevents unauthorized access to printing functions. This prevents unauthorized
users printing and removing sensitive documents to which they should not have access. Applying access
controls to printers also prevents unauthorized network access via printer vulnerabilities.

## DEFAULT PASSWORDS

H-108. A default password is a preconfigured password typically used for initial setup of a system. Many
systems, including major communications assemblages and command and control information systems, come
with default passwords and user and administrator accounts configured. Using default passwords on
networked devices or consoles presents a significant network vulnerability. Army password standards require
changing the passwords on routers and system consoles from the factory default. This includes changing
from the default passwords provided by program managers during equipment fielding. Since default
passwords are widely known, threats or the opposing force will try them first in their attempts to infiltrate
the network. Administrators and users must change all default passwords on network and endpoint devices
before connecting them to the network.
**202**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**

### Appendix I

## Army Integrated Reprogramming Cycle

As currently implemented, electromagnetic warfare reprogramming cannot be implemented
within a tactically relevant timeframe. The Cyber Center of Excellence led a comprehensive
doctrine, organization, training, materiel, leadership and education, personnel, facilities, and
policy assessment to define a future state Army integrated reprogramming cycle. This appendix
defines the future state reprogramming cycle.

## OVERVIEW

I-1. The modern electromagnetic operational environment is more dynamic than ever. Rapid advances in
communications and noncommunications technologies contribute to the pace of change. One of the newest
challenges is the widespread employment of software-defined radios. Enemy electromagnetic warfare
systems have also advanced, making the electromagnetic operational environment a challenging maneuver
space where the commander must maintain situational understanding and be able to control electromagnetic
signatures associated with command and control systems, radars, and EW systems.
I-2. Electromagnetic attack effects must include advanced techniques beyond brute-force jamming to
increase survivability and lethality of Army personnel and systems. The increasingly capable threat
throughout the electromagnetic operational environment requires a responsive EW reprogramming enterprise
that supports tactical maneuver with software-defined emitter detection and effects delivery capabilities as
close to combat speed as possible.
I-3. The future Army integrated reprogramming cycle is a modified version of the joint reprogramming
cycle outlined in JP 3-85. The Army integrated reprogramming cycle reflects a consolidated Army
electromagnetic countermeasures development and delivery process. Figure I-1 illustrates the Army
integrated reprogramming cycle for the development of electromagnetic countermeasures.
**Figure I-1. Army integrated reprogramming cycle**
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**203**
**Appendix I**

## PHASES

I-4. The Army integrated reprogramming cycle follows the process in JP 3-85, with minor adjustments to
support imitative, manipulative, or simulative electromagnetic attack. The purple cycle phases identified in
Figure I-1 are the joint naming convention and the green phases are the naming convention used to support
the Army electromagnetic warfare reprogramming enterprise. The Army integrated reprogramming cycle
aligns electromagnetic countermeasures development as a subprocess within the scope of EW
reprogramming, eliminating the need for new policies and allowing development to occur within existing
EW reprogramming authorities.
I-5. The Army integrated reprogramming cycle consists of four phases:
•
Determine the requirement.
•
Determine the response.
•
Create the response.
•
Implement the response.

### DETERMINE THEREQUIREMENT

I-6. Staffs develop and maintain an accurate description of the operational environment, specifically threat
systems and tactics, techniques, and procedures through intelligence preparation of the operational
environment. Maintaining an accurate description of the environment requires the fusion of known
electromagnetic data with the collection, analysis, and validation of threat signature changes.
I-7. Intelligence preparation of the operational environment (see chapter 4) is a collaborative staff effort
led by the intelligence staff. The staff continually updates intelligence preparation of the operational
environment products (including the electromagnetic order of battle) to support situational understanding and
assist commanders and staff in identifying relevant aspects within their assigned area or area of interest, such
as threat signals of interest that can affect mission accomplishment. The commander and staff’s
understanding of intelligence preparation of the operational environment and the electromagnetic order of
battle help in developing high-payoff and high-value targets. Defining the electromagnetic order of battle
requires—
•
Identifying transmitters and receivers in an area of interest.
•
Determining their geographic locations or range of mobility.
•
Characterizing their signals.
•
Determining their roles in the threat’s broader organizational order of battle where possible.
I-8. Threat use of software-defined radios and agile electromagnetic attack capabilities require continual
updates to the electromagnetic order of battle. When EW and SIGINT teams detect unidentified signals in
their assigned areas, those signals require analysis to identify their characteristics. This contributes to a
current, accurate electromagnetic order of battle and allows EW personnel to identify requirements for new
or updated electromagnetic countermeasures.

### DETERMINE THERESPONSE

I-9. Once commanders and staff understand the situation and mission, they develop courses of action to
generate response options that will drive the production of operation plans or orders. The CEMA section
generates cyberspace and EW options to support proposed courses of action. If the CEMA section determines
an existing electromagnetic countermeasure is not sufficient or a new countermeasure is needed, they perform
additional analysis to determine whether a proposed new countermeasure will provide the emitter detection
or effects delivery capability necessary to achieve the commander’s objectives for a given target or set of
targets.
I-10. CEMA staff at echelon using the Electromagnetic Warfare Planning and Management Tool will review
the electromagnetic countermeasures arsenal to determine whether an electromagnetic countermeasure has
been developed for a target and that the electromagnetic attack system it was developed for meets their
operational requirement. If no countermeasure is available, the CEMA staff submits an operational change
request (see appendix E) to request development of an electromagnetic countermeasure to support their
fielded EW system and establish the priority (routine, urgent, or emergency) of the request. CEMA sections
**204**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
**Army Integrated Reprogramming Cycle**
forward the operational change request through their chain of command to the theater army CEMA section
for approval and processing.

### CREATE THERESPONSE

I-11. The create the response phase consists of—
•
Developing and testing an electromagnetic countermeasure.
•
Cataloging the countermeasure and placing it within the electromagnetic countermeasures arsenal
for use by EW community.
I-12. The electromagnetic countermeasures arsenal provides detailed information on electromagnetic
countermeasure techniques, EW systems, and threat signal characteristics to support modeling and
simulation, mission planning, and course of action development. The electromagnetic countermeasures
arsenal also provides the capability to store and deliver electromagnetic countermeasures to fielded EW
systems via the Electromagnetic Warfare Planning and Management Tool.
I-13. Electromagnetic countermeasure developers must determine whether enough threat system data is
available to develop a countermeasure. The signal of interest can be complex and may require reverse
engineering to determine whether an effective electromagnetic countermeasure can be developed to affect
the target.
I-14. The electromagnetic countermeasure developer will conduct a vulnerability assessment that consists
of—
•
Signal analysis.
•
Reverse engineering.
•
Protocol and specification review.
I-15. Once developers discover a vulnerability to the threat system, they develop an electromagnetic
countermeasure to exploit the vulnerability. If the vulnerability assessment fails to expose an obvious
weakness, an electromagnetic countermeasure may still be created through experimentation or trial and error.
By leveraging previously developed electromagnetic countermeasures known to be effective against similar
signals of interest, a baseline capability can be quickly established and modified to tailor the countermeasure
to the target in question. Developers can then evaluate tactics, techniques, and procedures for deploying these
countermeasures through physical testing, modeling, and simulations and predict combat effectiveness
against the threat system.
I-16. Depending on the urgency of the request, developers may not have enough time to generate a carefully
tailored countermeasure for the threat capability. In these cases, they may opt to quickly develop an interim
capability or tactics, techniques, and procedures to detect the signal of interest and deliver a brute force effect
while continuing to develop and refine a more advanced technique.
I-17. The developer tests the electromagnetic countermeasure at a test range to validate tactics, techniques,
and procedures, combat effectiveness, and required safety certification for employment. When testing and
validating new countermeasures, tactics, techniques, and procedures, developers must consider the risk
tolerance of the mission—
•
Electromagnetic protection and defensive electromagnetic attack techniques carry a low risk
tolerance for mission failure. Systems that protect personnel and equipment require stringent testing
and evaluation processes.
•
Electromagnetic attack techniques allow a higher level of accepted risk. Systems designed to deny,
disrupt, or destroy threat systems may not require extended testing and evaluation before
employment.
•
Advanced electromagnetic attack techniques (electromagnetic deception, electromagnetic intrusion,
electromagnetic manipulation, or electromagnetic spectrum-enabled cyberspace attack) require
extensive testing and evaluation to predict their combat effectiveness.
I-18. Once a countermeasure is approved, the electromagnetic countermeasure file and tactics, techniques,
and procedures for employment are packaged and placed in the electromagnetic countermeasures arsenal. A
reprogramming message is then sent to all theater CEMA staff.
**17 September 2025**
**FM 3-12**
**205**
**Appendix I**

### IMPLEMENT THERESPONSE

I-19. Upon receipt of the reprogramming message, the CEMA staff will download the electromagnetic
countermeasure file and supporting products to complete the mission. The CEMA sections determine the
need for a countermeasure and review the electromagnetic countermeasures arsenal. The CEMA section,
using an EW planning tool like Electromagnetic Warfare Planning and Management Tool, downloads a
countermeasure file with supporting tactics, techniques, and procedures. The CEMA section verifies the new
electromagnetic countermeasure effect can be employed withing their delegated electromagnetic attack
control authority.
I-20. The CEMA section disseminates files and associated tactics, techniques, and procedures to the EW
team with the mission task. The team installs the file and adjusts the systems for executing the mission. The
CEMA section then submits a unit loading message (see appendix E) to document implementation of the
reprogramming action.
**206**
**FM 3-12**
**17 September 2025**
